Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Distinguishing Between God's Names and Attributes
Commentator defines 'name' and 'attribute' in the context of divine names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the terminological distinction between God's 'names' (indicating essence) and 'attributes' (indicating an additional meaning) as used in kalam.

Core Idea
God's names indicate His essence; His attributes indicate additional meanings beyond the essence.

Building on the debate about tawqifiyyah, the commentator now clarifies what is meant by 'name' and 'attribute' in this context. This lesson unpacks the precise definitions: a name points to the essence (either alone or with an attribute), while an attribute points to a meaning superadded to the essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the arguments of scholars like al-Ghazali.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how 'name' (ism) is defined as indicating the essence, and 'attribute' (sifah) as indicating an additional meaning.
The passage explicitly contrasts these terms.
2
Understand the contextual usage
The commentator clarifies that 'name' here is opposed to 'attribute', not to verb/particle or nickname. This is a technical theological usage.
This prevents confusion with grammar or honorifics.
3
Apply with examples
The passage gives two examples: 'Allah' (essence alone) and 'al-Rahman' (essence with the attribute of mercy). Reflect on how these fit the definitions.
Consider other divine names and whether they indicate essence alone or combined with an attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاخْتِيرَ أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ تَوقِيفِيَّهْ كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ فَاحْفَظ السَّمْعِيَّة
قوله : (وَاخْتِيرَ ... إلخ) أي اختار جمهور أهل السنة أن أسماءه تعالى توقيفية، وكذا صفاته، فلا تُنبِتُ الله اسماً ولا صفةٌ إلا إذا ورد بذلك توقيف من الشارع، وذهبت المعتزلة إلى جواز إثبات ما كان متصفاً بمعناه، ولم يوهم نقصاً، وإن لم يرد به توقيف من الشارع، ومال إليه القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني، وتوقف فيه إمام الحرمين، وفصل الغزالي فجوّز إطلاق الصفة؛ وهي ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات ومنع إطلاق الاسم؛ وهو ما دلّ على نفس الذات.
والحاصل أنَّ علماء الإسلام اتفقوا على جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري عزَّ وجلَّ إذا ورد بها الإذن من الشارع، وعلى امتناعه إذا ورد المنع منه، واختلفوا حيث لا إذنَ ولا منع، والمختار منعُ ذلك، وهو مذهب الجمهور. انتهى. مصنف في شرحه الصغيره.
قوله : (أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ) بدرج همزة أسماءه الأولى مع القصر للوزن، والمراد بالأسماء: ما قابل الصفات، بدليل قوله : (كذا الصفات)، الاسم ما دلّ على الذات، والصفة ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات وليس المراد بالاسم ما قابل الفعل والحرف ولا ما قابل الكنية واللقب. (الاسم ما دلّ على الذات) إما وحدها كلفظ الجلالة، وإما مع الصفة كلفظ الرحمن.
And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes, so preserve the scriptural [evidence].
His statement: (And it is chosen... etc.) meaning: the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah chose that His names, may He be exalted, are tawqifiyyah, and likewise His attributes, so we do not affirm for God a name nor an attribute unless there has come a tawqif from the Lawgiver. The Mu'tazila went to the permissibility of affirming whatever is descriptive of its meaning, as long as it does not imply deficiency, even if no tawqif from the Lawgiver has come. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani inclined to that, and Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] withheld [judgment], and al-Ghazali distinguished: he permitted the application of the attribute, which is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, and forbade the application of the name, which is what indicates the essence itself.
The conclusion is that the scholars of Islam agreed upon the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted, when permission from the Lawgiver has come, and upon the impossibility when prohibition has come, and they differed where there is neither permission nor prohibition, and the chosen [view] is the prohibition of that, and that is the school of the majority. End. [The commentator] said in his small commentary.
His statement: (that His names) with elision of the hamza of 'asma'ihi the first, with shortening for meter, and what is meant by names: what is opposed to attributes, evidenced by his statement: (likewise the attributes). The name is what indicates the essence, and the attribute is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence. What is meant by name is not what is opposed to verb and particle, nor what is opposed to nickname and honorific. (The name is what indicates the essence) either alone, like the word of majesty, or with the attribute like the word al-Rahman.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a classification of scholarly positions on whether God's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah (dependent on revelation). The commentator begins by quoting the poem line and then explains it. The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that both names and attributes must come from revelation; one cannot invent a name or attribute for God without scriptural authorization. The Mu'tazila allow affirming any name or attribute that corresponds to a meaning that does not imply deficiency, even without revelation. Al-Baqillani inclined to the Mu'tazila position, al-Juwayni suspended judgment, and al-Ghazali distinguished between attributes (which indicate an additional meaning beyond the essence) and names (which indicate the essence itself), permitting the former but not the latter without revelation. The commentator then summarizes a consensus: all agree on permissibility when revelation permits, impermissibility when revelation forbids, and disagreement where revelation is silent; the chosen view (mukhtār) is prohibition, following the majority. Finally, he explains that in the context, 'names' are distinct from 'attributes' and defines both: a name indicates the essence (either alone or with an attribute, e.g., 'Allah' or 'al-Rahman'), while an attribute indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence.

Key Terms
اسم
صفة
ذات
معنى زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'name' and 'attribute' are interchangeable.
Correction: In kalam, a name indicates the essence (alone or with an attribute) while an attribute indicates a meaning additional to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the term 'name' (ism) indicate when applied to God?
A) A meaning additional to the essence.
B) The essence of God, either alone or with an attribute.
C) A linguistic category opposite to verb.
D) A title of honor.

Key Takeaway
In the context of divine names and attributes, a name indicates the essence, while an attribute indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah by His names, do you consciously remember that each name points to His very essence? How does this awareness change the way you utter 'Allah' or 'al-Rahman' today?
?
If an attribute like mercy is a real meaning added to Allah's essence, how should that affect your trust in His mercy, knowing it is not just a name but a true quality of His being?
?
The passage distinguishes between name and attribute. Examine your own heart: do you ever treat God's names as mere labels without feeling the weight of the attributes they convey? What one change can you make in your supplication to reflect this deeper understanding?

The Tawqifiyyah of Divine Names and Attributes
The author states the chosen position and explains the foundation that Allah's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling that Allah's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah (revelation-based) according to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
Allah's names and attributes are not inventions; they come only from revelation.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational principle in Islamic theology: the names and attributes of Allah are tawqifiyyah, meaning they must be taken from the Qur'an and Sunnah. This lesson focuses on the position of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic matn and its explanation once to get the overall meaning.
Notice the phrase 'fahfaz as-sam'iyyah' which emphasizes relying on revelation.
2
Identify the ruling
The passage states that the majority (jumhur) chose that Allah's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah.
3
Understand 'tawqifiyyah'
Tawqifiyyah means that the use of names and attributes is restricted to what has been revealed; no name or attribute can be established except by divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاخْتِيرَ أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ تَوْقِيفِيَّهْ كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ فَاحْفَظ السَّمْعِيَّة
قوله : (وَاخْتبر ... إلخ) أي اختار جمهور أهل السنة أن أسماءه تعالى توقيفية، وكذا صفاته، فلا تُنبِت الله اسماً ولا صفة إلا إذا ورد بذلك توقيف من الشارع، وذهبت المعتزلة إلى جواز إثبات ما كان متصفاً بمعناه، ولم يوهم نقصاً، وإن لم يرد به توقيف من الشارع، ومال إليه القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني، وتوقف فيه إمام الحرمين، وفصل الغزالي فجوّز إطلاق الصفة؛ وهي ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات ومنع إطلاق الاسم؛ وهو ما دلّ على نفس الذات.
والحاصل أنَّ علماء الإسلام اتفقوا على جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري عزَّ وجلَّ إذا ورد بها الإذن من الشارع، وعلى امتناعه إذا ورد المنع منه، واختلفوا حيث لا إذنَ ولا منع، والمختار منعُ ذلك ، وهو مذهب الجمهور. انتهى. مصنف في شرحه الصغيره
And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes, so preserve the sam'iyyah. His saying: (and it is chosen... etc.) i.e. the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah chose that His names, exalted, are tawqifiyyah, and likewise His attributes, so Allah is not established with a name or attribute except if a revelation from the Lawgiver has come with that. And the Mu'tazila went to the permissibility of establishing what is qualified by its meaning and does not imply deficiency, even if no revelation from the Lawgiver has come with it. And al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani inclined to it, and Imam al-Haramayn paused (reserved judgment), and al-Ghazali made a distinction: he permitted the use of the attribute, which indicates a meaning additional to the essence, and prohibited the use of the name, which indicates the essence itself. And the summary is that the scholars of Islam agreed on the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator, mighty and majestic, when permission from the Lawgiver has come, and on its prohibition when prohibition has come, and they differed where there is neither permission nor prohibition. And the chosen view is to prohibit that, and it is the madhhab of the majority. End. [From] the author in his smaller commentary.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on whether the names and attributes of Allah (swt) are tawqifiyyah, meaning they must be derived from revelation and not invented by human reasoning. It begins by stating the chosen position: that Allah's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah, as indicated by the verse 'And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes, so preserve the sam'iyyah.' The commentator then elaborates: the majority (jumhur) of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that no name or attribute can be used for Allah unless it has been revealed by the Lawgiver (the Prophet ﷺ via the Qur'an and Sunnah). Then it mentions the Mu'tazili position, which allows using any attribute that does not imply deficiency, even without explicit revelation. Al-Baqillani inclined toward this view, while al-Juwayni (Imam al-Haramayn) suspended judgment. Al-Ghazali made a distinction: he permitted using attributes (which denote a meaning superadded to the essence) but prohibited using names (which denote the essence itself) without revelation. The passage then gives a final summary: all scholars agree on the cases where permission or prohibition explicitly exists; they differ only on cases where there is neither. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is to prohibit such usage, and this is the madhhab of the majority.

Key Terms
توقيفية
جمهور
الاسم
الصفة
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That we can invent names and attributes for Allah based on our own reasoning
Correction: The majority view prohibits this; only revealed names and attributes may be used.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for using a name or attribute for Allah?
A) It must be logically sound
B) It must be derived from revelation
C) It must be approved by scholars
D) It must be commonly used

Key Takeaway
The names and attributes of Allah are derived exclusively from revelation, not human reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described Allah using a term or attribute you picked up from culture or emotion, without checking if it's in the Qur'an or Sunnah? Will you stop doing that starting today?
?
When you think of Allah’s mercy or anger, is every aspect of your understanding based on revelation, or do you sometimes let your own sense of fairness fill in the gaps? How would you feel if you discovered you were attributing something false to Him?
?
If a friend suggested a beautiful name for Allah that is not from revelation, would you have the courage to say 'I cannot use that'? What would that cost you, and is it worth it?

Scholarly Opinions on Unrevealed Names and Attributes
The author presents the differing views of various scholars and then summarises the consensus and the chosen position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly positions on using names and attributes for Allah that are not explicitly revealed.

Core Idea
Scholars differ on unrevealed names; the majority prohibit, but some permit with conditions.

While all scholars agree on the basic rule of tawqifiyyah, they differ on what to do when there is no explicit revelation. This lesson surveys the opinions of the Mu'tazila, al-Baqillani, al-Juwayni, and al-Ghazali, and concludes with the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that lists the various scholarly positions.
Look for the key scholars mentioned: Mu'tazila, al-Baqillani, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ghazali.
2
Identify each position
Note the specific view of each scholar or group. The Mu'tazila permit, al-Baqillani inclines to that, al-Juwayni suspends, and al-Ghazali distinguishes between names and attributes.
3
Understand al-Ghazali's distinction
Al-Ghazali permits using attributes (denoting a meaning) but prohibits names (denoting the essence) without revelation.
4
Grasp the final summary
The passage concludes that all scholars agree on cases with explicit permission or prohibition, but differ on non-explicit cases. The chosen view (mukhtar) is prohibition, which is the majority position.

Source Text

?

Are you willing to submit to this discipline, even if it means abandoning some expressions you felt were beautiful? What does your resistance or acceptance reveal about your heart's attachment to following revelation over personal preference?
?

Which position do you find more compelling, and why? More importantly, does your choice reflect a desire to honour Allah correctly or simply to justify your own comfort?

The Principle of Tawqifiyyah and Its Sources
Definition of tawqifiyyah and the distinction between weak hadith in belief vs. practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of tawqifiyyah and the conditions for applying names to Allah, including the different roles of weak hadith in matters of belief and practice.

Core Idea
Divine names are revealed; weak hadith can inform practice but not belief.

This passage begins by defining the fundamental principle that all names and attributes of Allah must be based on revelation. It then addresses an important nuance: weak hadith can be used for practical naming but not for establishing belief. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawqifiyyah
Begin by reading the first sentence that defines tawqifiyyah as the dependence of applying names on their occurrence in specific sources.
Note the sources listed: Qur'an, sound hadith, and consensus.
2
Identify the exception for weak hadith
The passage then states that weak hadith is treated differently. Focus on the two scenarios: 'ilmiyyat (belief) and 'amaliyyat (practice).
Weak hadith is not sufficient for belief but is sufficient for practice.
3
Understand the rationale
The reason given is that weak hadith is acted upon in matters of virtues of deeds (فضائل الأعمال). Compare this to the strict requirement for belief.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. It shows that naming Allah is a serious matter that requires certainty in belief.
This principle safeguards against attributing unverified names to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (تَوْقِيفِيَّة) أي : يتوقف جواز إطلاقها عليه تعالى على ورودها في كتاب أو سنة صحيحة أو حسنةٍ أو إجماع؛ لأنَّه غير خارج عنها، بخلاف السنة الضعيفة إن قلنا : إن المسألة من العلميات؛ أي: الاعتقاديات بحيث يعتقد أن ذلك الاسم من أسمائه تعالى، وإن قلنا : إن المسألة من العمليات بحيث نستعمله ونطلقه عليه تعالى .. فالسنةُ الضعيفةُ كافية في ذلك ؛ لأنَّهم قالوا : الحديث الضعيف يُعمل به في فضائل الأعمال، وأما القياس فقيل : كالإجماع ما لم يكن ضعيفاً، وعليه فيقاس (واهب) بناء على أنه لم يرد على (وهاب)، وأطلق بعضهم منع القياس قال المصنف في «شرحه الصغير»: (وهو الظاهر، لاحتمال إيهام أحد المترادفين دون الآخر كالعالم والعارف، والجواد والسخي والحليم والعاقل). انتهى
وبالجملة فما أذن الشارع في إطلاقه واستعماله جاز، وإن أوهم كالصبور والشكور والحليم، فإن الصبور يُوهم وصول مشقة له تعالى؛ لأن الصبر حبس النفس على المشاق، فيفسر في حق تعالى بالذي لا يعجلُ بالعقوبة على من عصاه، والشكور يُوهم وصول إحسان إليه ؛ لأن معناه كثير الشكر لمن أحسن إليه، مع أن الإحسان كله من الله ، فيفسرُ في حقه تعالى بالذي يجازي على يسير الطاعات كثير الدرجات، ويُعطي بالعمل في أيام معدودة نعماً في الآخرة غير محدودة، وقيل: المجازي على الشكر ، وقيل : المثني على من أطاعه والحليم يُوهم وصول أذى إليه، وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحدٌ بأذى، فيفسر في حقه تعالى بالذي لا يعجل بالعقوبة على من عصاه، فيرجع له معنى الصبور، ولا يرد على قولنا : (وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحد بأذى قوله : من آذى مسلماً فقد آذاني، ومن آذاني فقد آذى الله
And His saying: (tawqifiyyah) i.e. the permissibility of applying them to Him, exalted, depends on their occurrence in the Book, a sound or good sunna, or consensus; because He is not outside them, unlike the weak sunna if we say the matter is from the 'ilmiyyat (belief matters) – i.e. those pertaining to belief – so that one believes that that name is among His names, exalted; and if we say the matter is from the 'amaliyyat (practical matters) – in that we use it and apply it to Him, exalted – then the weak sunna is sufficient for that, because they said: weak hadith is acted upon in the virtues of deeds. As for qiyas, it is said: like consensus as long as it is not weak, and upon that (the name) 'Wahib' is analogized because it has not been reported from (the name) 'Wahhab', and some absolutely prohibited qiyas. The author in his smaller commentary said: (and it is apparent, due to the possibility that one of the synonyms might imply something that the other does not, like al-Alim and al-Arif, al-Jawad and al-Sakhi, al-Halim and al-Aqil). End. In summary, whatever the Lawgiver has permitted to apply and use is permissible, even if it implies something, like al-Sabur, al-Shakur, and al-Halim. For al-Sabur implies the occurrence of hardship upon Him, exalted, because patience is the restraint of the soul against hardships, so it is interpreted concerning Him to mean the One who does not hasten punishment upon one who disobeys Him. And al-Shakur implies the receipt of beneficence from Him, because its meaning is one who gives much thanks to one who does good to him, while all beneficence is from Allah, so it is interpreted concerning Him to mean the One who rewards for small acts of obedience with many degrees, and gives for deeds done in a few days unlimited blessings in the Hereafter, and it is said: the One who rewards gratitude, or the One who praises those who obey Him. And al-Halim implies the occurrence of harm upon Him, and He, exalted, is not reached by anyone with harm, so it is interpreted concerning Him to mean the One who does not hasten punishment upon one who disobeys Him, and thus it returns to the meaning of al-Sabur. And it does not refute our saying: (He, exalted, is not reached by anyone with harm) the saying: 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah.'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage provides detailed explanation of what 'tawqifiyyah' means and addresses practical issues. It first states that the permissibility of applying a name or attribute to Allah depends on its occurrence in the Qur'an, a sahih (sound) or hasan (good) hadith, or ijma' (consensus). Unlike weak hadith: if the matter is related to belief (i.e., one must believe that name actually belongs to Allah), weak hadith is not sufficient; but if it is related to practice (merely using the name as a description), then weak hadith is acceptable, similar to its use in virtuous deeds. The passage then discusses qiyas (analogical reasoning): some scholars consider it like consensus unless it is weak, allowing analogical extension, e.g., deriving 'Wahib' from 'Wahhab' if not reported. Others absolutely forbid qiyas; the author (al-Muallif) in his smaller commentary says the apparent view is prohibition, because synonyms may carry different implications (e.g., 'Alim' vs. 'Arif', 'Jawad' vs. 'Sakhi', 'Halim' vs. 'Aqil') – one might suggest a meaning the other does not. The passage then gives examples of names that might imply deficiency if taken literally: al-Sabur (the patient) might imply hardship on Allah, but it is interpreted as 'the One who does not hasten punishment'; al-Shakur (the thankful) might imply receipt of favors, but it is interpreted as 'the One who rewards generously'; al-Halim (the forbearing) might imply exposure to harm, but it is interpreted as 'the One who does not hasten punishment'. Finally, an objection is raised: a hadith says 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah.' This might imply that Allah is harmed. The passage dismisses this objection, stating it does not refute the principle that no one reaches Allah with harm; the hadith is understood metaphorically.

Key Terms
توقيفية (tawqifiyyah)
السنة الضعيفة (weak sunna/hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any name derived from a weak hadith can be used for Allah without distinction.
Correction: The passage clarifies that weak hadith can only be used for practical naming, not for establishing a name of Allah in belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for using a weak hadith to apply a name to Allah?
A) It can be used in both belief and practice
B) It can be used only in practice, not in belief
C) It can be used only in belief, not in practice
D) It cannot be used at all

Key Takeaway
Tawqifiyyah means divine names must come from revelation, and weak hadith can only inform their practical use, not belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually used a name for Allah without verifying its source? How will you ensure from now on that every name you use is rooted in revelation?
?
This lesson distinguishes between belief and practice when it comes to weak hadith. In your own life, are you careful to distinguish between what you believe about Allah and what you merely repeat from hearsay? What practical step can you take to guard your beliefs?
?
If a trusted teacher or speaker uses a name for Allah that you suspect is based on weak evidence, what would you do? How does this lesson empower you to seek the truth without being disrespectful?

The Ruling on Qiyas for Divine Names
Discussion of qiyas for divine names with two scholarly positions and author's preferred view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on using qiyas (analogical reasoning) to derive divine names and the reasoning behind the prohibition, especially due to potential differences in implication between synonyms.

Core Idea
Qiyas is not allowed for divine names because synonyms differ in implication.

After establishing the principle of revelation, the passage addresses the controversial method of qiyas. Can we derive a new name like 'Wahib' from 'Wahhab'? Scholars disagree, and the author provides his preferred view, supported by examples showing how synonyms can have distinct connotations.

Guided Path
1
Read the two positions on qiyas
The passage presents two views: one allows qiyas as long as it is not weak, and the other absolutely prohibits it. Note the example of 'Wahib' derived from 'Wahhab'.
The author mentions that some permitted it while others prohibited it absolutely.
2
Understand the author's preferred view
The author in his smaller commentary states that the apparent view is prohibition. Focus on the reason given: the possibility that one synonym might imply something the other does not.
3
Study the examples of synonym pairs
Three pairs are given: al-Alim and al-Arif, al-Jawad and al-Sakhi, al-Halim and al-Aqil. Think about the subtle differences in meaning between each pair.
For instance, al-Alim implies knowledge, while al-Arif adds intimacy.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this reasoning strengthens the prohibition of qiyas: naming Allah requires precision, and synonyms are not always interchangeable.
This shows the care needed in divine names.

Interpreting Names with Apparent Deficiency
Examples of names that might imply deficiency and their correct interpretations, plus an objection from a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn how names that seem to imply deficiency (like al-Sabur, al-Shakur, al-Halim) are correctly interpreted to befit Allah's perfection, and how a potential objection from a hadith is resolved.

Core Idea
Names like al-Sabur imply perfection after proper interpretation.

The passage illustrates that even though some divine names might superficially suggest imperfection, they are all revealed and must be understood in a manner befitting Allah. It provides three examples, each with its literal implication and correct interpretation, and then addresses a potential objection from a hadith that might suggest otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the general principle
The passage begins by stating that whatever the Lawgiver has permitted to apply and use is permissible, even if it suggests something. This sets the context for the examples.
The principle: permissibility even with potential implication.
2
Study each name: al-Sabur, al-Shakur, al-Halim
For each name, note the literal implication (e.g., hardship, receipt of favor, harm) and then the correct interpretation that removes any deficiency.
The interpretations focus on Allah's actions, not His essence.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the interpretations
See how each interpretation transforms the meaning to one that befits Allah, e.g., al-Sabur means He does not hasten punishment.
The key is to avoid anthropomorphic implications.
4
Read the objection and answer
The passage ends with an objection from a hadith that says harming a Muslim harms Allah. The answer explains that this does not contradict the principle, implying it is metaphorical.
This shows how problematic texts are reconciled with established doctrine.

?

?
The hadith 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah' could be taken literally to imply Allah can be harmed. Are there any other verses or hadith that you have been interpreting too literally, leading to a flawed understanding of Allah's transcendence? What step will you take to correct that this week?
?
The authors reinterpreted names that seemed to imply deficiency. Is there an attribute of Allah that you find difficult to reconcile with His perfection? Will you commit to seeking sound theological interpretation for that attribute, even if it challenges your initial understanding?

6 min
The Principle of Tawqifiyyah and the Role of Weak Hadith and Qiyas
Commentary on the rule of tawqifiyyah and two related issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of tawqifiyyah, the sources from which divine names must be derived, and the rulings regarding weak hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Divine names require explicit textual authorization; weak hadith and qiyas have limited scope.

The commentator establishes that no divine name is permissible unless it comes from the Quran, authentic hadith, or consensus. He then addresses two nuanced cases: weak hadith, which suffices for practical use but not for belief, and qiyas, which is generally forbidden due to ambiguity in synonyms.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawqifiyyah
Start by reading the first sentence which defines tawqifiyyah. Notice the three sources mentioned: Quran, authentic/good hadith, and consensus.
The phrase 'because He is not beyond them' emphasizes that reason alone cannot invent names.
2
Understand the weak hadith distinction
The passage differentiates between intellectual (belief) and practical matters. For belief, weak hadith is insufficient; for practice, it is acceptable. Reflect on why this distinction is important.
This is based on the principle that weak hadith is used in virtues of deeds but not in creed.
3
Analyze the qiyas discussion
Read the section on qiyas. Note that some scholars allow it while others forbid it. The author favors forbidding because synonyms may imply different meanings. Consider the example given.
The example shows that 'Wāhib' cannot be analogized from 'Wahhāb' because the former might imply something unsuitable.
4
Synthesize the key takeaway
After reading, summarize the rule: names come from revelation; weak hadith has limited use; qiyas is controversial but the preferred view forbids it.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (تَوقِيفِيَّة) أي : يتوقف جواز إطلاقها عليه تعالى على ورودها في كتاب أو سنة صحيحة أو حسنةٍ أو إجماع؛ لأنَّه غير خارج عنها، بخلاف السنة الضعيفة إن قلنا : إن المسألة من العلميات؛ أي: الاعتقاديات بحيث يعتقد أن ذلك الاسم من أسمائه تعالى، وإن قلنا : إن المسألة من العمليات بحيث نستعمله ونطلق عليه تعالى .. فالسنةُ الضعيفةُ كافية في ذلك ؛ لأنَّهم قالوا : الحديث الضعيف يُعمل به في فضائل الأعمال، وأما القياس فقيل : كالإجماع ما لم يكن ضعيفاً، وعليه فيقاس (واهب) بناء على أنه لم يرد على (وهاب)، وأطلق بعضهم منع القياس قال المصنف في «شرحه الصغير»: (وهو الظاهر، لاحتمال إيهام أحد المترادفين دون الآخر كالعالم والعارف، والجواد والسخي والحليم والعاقل). وبالجملة فما أذن الشارع في إطلاقه واستعماله جاز، وإن أوهم كالصبور والشكور والحليم، فإن الصبور يُوهم وصول مشقّة له تعالى؛ لأن الصبر حَبْسُ النفس على المشاق ، فيفسر في - تعالى بالذي لا يعجلُ بالعقوبة على من عصاه، والشكور يُوهم وصول إحسان إليه ؛ لأن معناه كثير الشكر لمن أحسن إليه، مع أن الإحسان كله من الله ، فيفسرُ في حقه تعالى بالذي يجازي على يسير الطاعات كثير الدرجات، ويُعطي بالعمل في أيام معدودة نعماً في الآخرة غير محدودة، وقيل: المجازي على الشكر ، وقيل : المثني على من أطاعه والحليم يُوهم وصول أذًى إليه، وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحدٌ بأذى، فيفسر في حقه تعالى بالذي لا يعجل بالعقوبة على من عصاه، فيرجع له معنى الصبور، ولا يرد على قولنا : (وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحد بأذى قوله : من آذى مسلماً فقد آذاني، ومن آذاني فقد آذى الله)؛ لأنَّ معناه أنَّه فعل معه فِعْلَ المؤذي، وقد تقدَّم لك أنَّ أسماء النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم توقيفية اتفاقاً، وسبق حكمة ذلك فتفطن لها . قوله : (كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ) أي: مثل أسمائه تعالى صفاته في كونها توقيفية، فلا يجوز إثبات صفة له تعالى إلا بتوقيف من الشارع لنا . وقوله: (فَاحْفَظ السَّمْعِيَّة)؛ أي: إذا عرفت أن إطلاق الأسماء والصفات عليه تعالى تتوقف على الإذن الشرعي فاحفظ الأسماء والصفات الواردة بالسمع حقيقة كالواردة في الكتاب والسنة، أو حكماً كالثابتة بالإجماع كالصانع والموجود والواجب والقديم، كما ذكره المؤلف في «كبيره». وقوله : (والصفة ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات) بأن دلّت على ذلك المعنى الزائد وحده، كلفظ (قدرة) ؛ فإنه دلَّ على المعنى القائم بذاته سبحانه وتعالى، وبهذا يُعلم أن مراد المصنف بالصفات في قوله : (كذا الصفات الأسماء الدالة على الأمور الثابتة للذات فهي أيضاً توقيفية، فلا يُعبر عن قدرة الله بالجراءة مثلاً لعدم وروده.
And his statement: (tawqīfiyyah) means: the permissibility of applying them to Him, exalted is He, depends on their occurrence in the Book or an authentic or good (ḥasan) sunnah or consensus; because He is not beyond them, unlike the weak sunnah if we say: the matter is among the intellectual matters (ʿilmiyyāt), i.e., belief matters such that one believes that name is among His names, exalted is He; and if we say: the matter is among the practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt) such that we use it and apply it to Him, exalted is He, then the weak sunnah is sufficient for that because they said: the weak ḥadīth is acted upon in the virtues of deeds. As for qiyās (analogy), it was said: it is like consensus as long as it is not weak, and accordingly (wāhib) is analogized based on the fact that (wahhāb) has not been reported, and some forbade qiyās; the author said in his 'Small Commentary': (It is the apparent view, due to the possibility that one of the synonyms may imply something the other does not, like al-ʿālim and al-ʿārif, al-jawād and al-sakhī, al-ḥalīm and al-ʿāqil). In summary, whatever the Lawgiver has permitted to apply and use is permissible, even if it implies [something unsuitable], like al-ṣabūr, al-shakūr, and al-ḥalīm. For al-ṣabūr implies that hardship reaches Him, exalted is He, because patience is restraining the soul in the face of hardships, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who does not hasten punishment upon those who disobey Him. Al-shakūr implies that beneficence reaches Him, because its meaning is one who gives much thanks to one who does good to him, while all beneficence is from Allah, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who rewards for a few acts of obedience with many degrees (of reward) and gives in exchange for deeds done in a limited number of days unlimited favors in the Hereafter; and it was said: the One Who rewards for thankfulness; and it was said: the One Who praises the one who obeys Him. Al-ḥalīm implies that harm reaches Him, and no one reaches Him with harm, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who does not hasten punishment upon those who disobey Him, so it comes back to the meaning of al-ṣabūr. And our statement: 'And He, exalted is He, no one reaches Him with harm' is not refuted by his statement: 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah'—because its meaning is that he did the act of one who harms [as if harming the Prophet and Allah in terms of the act], and it has preceded for you that the names of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are tawqīfiyyah by consensus, and the wisdom of that has preceded, so be attentive to it. His statement: (likewise the attributes) means: like His names, exalted is He, His attributes are tawqīfiyyah, so it is not permissible to affirm an attribute for Him, exalted is He, except by a tawqīf from the Lawgiver to us. And his statement: (so preserve the transmitted [names and attributes]) means: when you know that applying names and attributes to Him, exalted is He, depends on the legal permission, then preserve the names and attributes that are transmitted (samʿiyyah) literally, like those in the Book and Sunnah, or effectively, like those established by consensus, such as al-Ṣāniʿ, al-Mawjūd, al-Wājib, and al-Qadīm, as the author mentioned in his 'Great [Commentary]'. And his statement: (and the attribute is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence) means it indicates that additional meaning alone, like the word (qudra), for it indicates the meaning subsisting in His essence, glorified and exalted is He; and by this it is known that the author intends by 'attributes' in his statement: (likewise the attributes are names indicating matters established for the essence) and they are also tawqīfiyyah, so one should not express Allah's power as 'al-jarāʿah' for example due to its non-occurrence [in revelation].
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the rule that divine names and attributes are tawqīfiyyah, meaning they can only be derived from revelation. The commentator begins by explaining the term tawqīfiyyah: the permissibility of applying a name to God depends on its occurrence in the Quran, an authentic or good hadith, or consensus, because God is not beyond these sources. Then he discusses the weak hadith: if the matter is doctrinal (ʿilmiyyāt), a weak hadith is insufficient, but if it is practical (ʿamaliyyāt), a weak hadith suffices because weak hadiths are acted upon in the virtues of deeds. Next, he addresses qiyās (analogy): some say it is like consensus unless it is weak, and thus the name 'Wāhib' could be analogized from 'Wahhāb' because 'Wāhib' is not reported, but others forbid qiyās; the author supports forbidding it because synonyms may imply different connotations. Then he gives a summary: any name the Lawgiver has permitted in revelation is permissible even if its literal meaning implies something unsuitable, but such names must be interpreted properly to avoid anthropomorphism. He provides three examples: al-Ṣabūr (the Patient) implies that hardship reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who does not hasten punishment; al-Shakūr (the Thankful) implies that beneficence reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who rewards abundantly for little obedience; al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing) implies that harm reaches God, so it is interpreted similarly to al-Ṣabūr. Then he addresses an objection: the hadith 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah' does not contradict the principle that no one reaches God with harm, because it means the person did an act of harming, not that harm actually reaches God. He then confirms that even the Prophet's names are tawqīfiyyah by consensus. Finally, he defines attribute (ṣifa): it indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, like qudra (power), and thus attributes are also tawqīfiyyah; it is forbidden to use synonyms not found in revelation, such as 'al-jarāʿah' for power.

Key Terms
توقيفية
السنة الضعيفة
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a weak hadith can be used to establish a divine name as a matter of belief.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for belief matters, only strong sources (Quran, sahih/hasan hadith, consensus) are sufficient; weak hadith is only for practical use.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using a weak hadith to affirm a divine name as a matter of belief?
A) It is sufficient if the weak hadith is supported by qiyas.
B) It is not sufficient; only the Quran, authentic or good hadith, or consensus are acceptable.
C) It is sufficient because weak hadith is used in virtues of deeds.
D) It is sufficient only if the matter is practical.

Key Takeaway
Divine names must come from revelation; weak hadith and qiyas have limited scope and are not reliable for establishing belief-based names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever called upon Allah by a name that you assumed was appropriate but is not explicitly found in the Quran or authentic hadith? How does the principle of tawqifiyyah challenge you to verify your du'a and correct any missteps? Will you commit to checking your supplications against revelation starting today?
?
The passage warns that qiyas in divine names can lead to attributing unsuitable meanings to Allah because synonyms are not truly equivalent. Think of a time you understood Allah's attribute based on your own reasoning rather than revelation. How might that understanding be flawed? What will you do to replace it with knowledge from authentic sources?
?
You may have followed a practice or recited a name based on a weak hadith without distinguishing between belief and action. Which of your current religious practices are built on such weak evidence? Are you willing to abandon or adjust them for the sake of upholding tawqifiyyah, even if it feels difficult?

Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
Interpreting Names with Problematic Literal Meanings
Explanation of how three divine names are interpreted and an objection answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine names like al-Sabur, al-Shakur, and al-Halim are interpreted to avoid anthropomorphism while remaining permissible.

Core Idea
Revealed names with problematic literal meanings are permissible through proper interpretation, not rejection.

Some divine names, if taken literally, could imply imperfections. The commentator demonstrates how such names are interpreted in a way befitting Allah, using three examples: al-Sabur, al-Shakur, and al-Halim. He also addresses an objection based on a hadith about harming the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary statement
Begin with the sentence 'In summary, whatever the Lawgiver has permitted...' to understand the overarching principle: any name permitted by revelation is fine, even if its literal meaning seems unsuitable, because it can be interpreted appropriately.
2
Analyze the example of al-Sabur
Notice that al-Sabur (the Patient) literally implies patience with hardship reaching Allah. The commentator explains that it is interpreted as 'the One who does not hasten punishment', avoiding any notion of hardship.
This shows that interpretation (ta'wil) resolves the apparent problem.
3
Analyze the examples of al-Shakur and al-Halim
Similarly, al-Shakur (the Thankful) implies receiving beneficence, so it is interpreted as rewarding abundantly. Al-Halim (the Forbearing) implies harm reaching Him, so it is interpreted similarly to al-Sabur. Note how different literal meanings converge to the same interpretation.
4
Address the objection
Read the objection based on the hadith 'Whoever harms a Muslim...'. The commentator clarifies that this does not imply actual harm reaches Allah, but rather the person performs an act of harming.
This reinforces the principle of avoiding anthropomorphism.

And his statement: (tawqīfiyyah) means: the permissibility of applying them to Him, exalted is He, depends on their occurrence in the Book or an authentic or good (ḥasan) sunnah or consensus; because He is not beyond them, unlike the weak sunnah if we say: the matter is among the intellectual matters (ʿilmiyyāt), i.e., belief matters such that one believes that name is among His names, exalted is He; and if we say: the matter is among the practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt) such that we use it and apply it to Him, exalted is He, then the weak sunnah is sufficient for that because they said: the weak ḥadīth is acted upon in the virtues of deeds. Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the rule that divine names and attributes are tawqīfiyyah, meaning they can only be derived from revelation. The commentator begins by explaining the term tawqīfiyyah: the permissibility of applying a name to God depends on its occurrence in the Quran, an authentic or good hadith, or consensus, because God is not beyond these sources. Then he discusses the weak hadith: if the matter is doctrinal (ʿilmiyyāt), a weak hadith is insufficient, but if it is practical (ʿamaliyyāt), a weak hadith suffices because weak hadiths are acted upon in the virtues of deeds. Next, he addresses qiyās (analogy): some say it is like consensus unless it is weak, and thus the name 'Wāhib' could be analogized from 'Wahhāb' because 'Wāhib' is not reported, but others forbid qiyās; the author supports forbidding it because synonyms may imply different connotations. Then he gives a summary: any name the Lawgiver has permitted in revelation is permissible even if its literal meaning implies something unsuitable, but such names must be interpreted properly to avoid anthropomorphism. He provides three examples: al-Ṣabūr (the Patient) implies that hardship reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who does not hasten punishment; al-Shakūr (the Thankful) implies that beneficence reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who rewards abundantly for little obedience; al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing) implies that harm reaches God, so it is interpreted similarly to al-Ṣabūr. Then he addresses an objection: the hadith 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah' does not contradict the principle that no one reaches God with harm, because it means the person did an act of harming, not that harm actually reaches God. He then confirms that even the Prophet's names are tawqīfiyyah by consensus. Finally, he defines attribute (ṣifa): it indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, like qudra (power), and thus attributes are also tawqīfiyyah; it is forbidden to use synonyms not found in revelation, such as 'al-jarāʿah' for power.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the name 'al-Ḥalīm' (the Forbearing) interpreted?
A) It is interpreted as the One who harms those who disobey Him.
B) It is interpreted as the One who does not hasten punishment.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the name al-Ṣabūr, do you ever feel that Allah is slow to respond to your prayers? How does the correct interpretation—that He does not hasten punishment—change your perception of His patience with your own failures? What sin will you repent from today because of His forbearance?
?
The name al-Shakūr means that Allah rewards small deeds with immense reward. Have you ever belittled your own acts of worship, thinking they are too insignificant? How will you change your view of your daily obedience because of this? What one small good deed will you commit to doing consistently starting today?
?
The hadith about harming a Muslim is treated as harming Allah—yet Allah is not actually harmed. Are there people you have harmed, even unintentionally, through your words or actions? How can you make amends today, knowing that your harm reaches the Prophet and Allah in a metaphorical sense? What step will you take to seek forgiveness from someone you have wronged?

Attributes Are Also Tawqifiyyah: Definition and Examples
Clarification that attributes fall under tawqifiyyah and their definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes are also tawqifiyyah and be able to define an attribute (sifa) as indicating an additional meaning beyond the essence.

Core Idea
Like names, attributes require revelation; an attribute is what denotes a distinct meaning in the essence.

The commentator extends the rule of tawqifiyyah from names to attributes. He also provides a technical definition of an attribute: it indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, using 'qudra' (power) as an example. This prevents using synonyms not found in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the extension to attributes
Start with 'His statement: (likewise the attributes)...' to see that the same rule applies: attributes must come from revelation.
2
Understand the definition of sifa
Pay attention to the definition: an attribute is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence. The example 'qudra' clarifies that it points to a quality of Allah, not to His essence alone.
3
Note the practical implication
The passage ends with a prohibition: one cannot call Allah's power 'al-jarāʿah' (boldness) because it is not revealed. This illustrates the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (تَوقِيفِيَّة) أي : يتوقف جواز إطلاقها عليه تعالى على ورودها في كتاب أو سنة صحيحة أو حسنةٍ أو إجماع؛ لأنَّه غير خارج عنها، بخلاف السنة الضعيفة إن قلنا : إن المسألة من العلميات؛ أي: الاعتقاديات بحيث يعتقد أن ذلك الاسم من أسمائه تعالى، وإن قلنا : إن المسألة من العمليات بحيث نستعمله ونطلق عليه تعالى .. فالسنةُ الضعيفةُ كافية في ذلك ؛ لأنَّهم قالوا : الحديث الضعيف يُعمل به في فضائل الأعمال، وأما القياس فقيل : كالإجماع ما لم يكن ضعيفاً، وعليه فيقاس (واهب) بناء على أنه لم يرد على (وهاب)، وأطلق بعضهم منع القياس قال المصنف في «شرحه الصغير»: (وهو الظاهر، لاحتمال إيهام أحد المترادفين دون الآخر كالعالم والعارف، والجواد والسخي والحليم والعاقل). وبالجملة فما أذن الشارع في إطلاقه واستعماله جاز، وإن أوهم كالصبور والشكور والحليم، فإن الصبور يُوهم وصول مشقّة له تعالى؛ لأن الصبر حَبْسُ النفس على المشاق ، فيفسر في - تعالى بالذي لا يعجلُ بالعقوبة على من عصاه، والشكور يُوهم وصول إحسان إليه ؛ لأن معناه كثير الشكر لمن أحسن إليه، مع أن الإحسان كله من الله ، فيفسرُ في حقه تعالى بالذي يجازي على يسير الطاعات كثير الدرجات، ويُعطي بالعمل في أيام معدودة نعماً في الآخرة غير محدودة، وقيل: المجازي على الشكر ، وقيل : المثني على من أطاعه والحليم يُوهم وصول أذًى إليه، وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحدٌ بأذى، فيفسر في حقه تعالى بالذي لا يعجل بالعقوبة على من عصاه، فيرجع له معنى الصبور، ولا يرد على قولنا : (وهو تعالى لا يصل إليه أحد بأذى قوله : من آذى مسلماً فقد آذاني، ومن آذاني فقد آذى الله)؛ لأنَّ معناه أنَّه فعل معه فِعْلَ المؤذي، وقد تقدَّم لك أنَّ أسماء النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم توقيفية اتفاقاً، وسبق حكمة ذلك فتفطن لها . قوله : (كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ) أي: مثل أسمائه تعالى صفاته في كونها توقيفية، فلا يجوز إثبات صفة له تعالى إلا بتوقيف من الشارع لنا . وقوله: (فَاحْفَظ السَّمْعِيَّة)؛ أي: إذا عرفت أن إطلاق الأسماء والصفات عليه تعالى تتوقف على الإذن الشرعي فاحفظ الأسماء والصفات الواردة بالسمع حقيقة كالواردة في الكتاب والسنة، أو حكماً كالثابتة بالإجماع كالصانع والموجود والواجب والقديم، كما ذكره المؤلف في «كبيره». وقوله : (والصفة ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات) بأن دلّت على ذلك المعنى الزائد وحده، كلفظ (قدرة) ؛ فإنه دلَّ على المعنى القائم بذاته سبحانه وتعالى، وبهذا يُعلم أن مراد المصنف بالصفات في قوله : (كذا الصفات الأسماء الدالة على الأمور الثابتة للذات فهي أيضاً توقيفية، فلا يُعبر عن قدرة الله بالجراءة مثلاً لعدم وروده.
And his statement: (tawqīfiyyah) means: the permissibility of applying them to Him, exalted is He, depends on their occurrence in the Book or an authentic or good (ḥasan) sunnah or consensus; because He is not beyond them, unlike the weak sunnah if we say: the matter is among the intellectual matters (ʿilmiyyāt), i.e., belief matters such that one believes that name is among His names, exalted is He; and if we say: the matter is among the practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt) such that we use it and apply it to Him, exalted is He, then the weak sunnah is sufficient for that because they said: the weak ḥadīth is acted upon in the virtues of deeds. As for qiyās (analogy), it was said: it is like consensus as long as it is not weak, and accordingly (wāhib) is analogized based on the fact that (wahhāb) has not been reported, and some forbade qiyās; the author said in his 'Small Commentary': (It is the apparent view, due to the possibility that one of the synonyms may imply something the other does not, like al-ʿālim and al-ʿārif, al-jawād and al-sakhī, al-ḥalīm and al-ʿāqil). In summary, whatever the Lawgiver has permitted to apply and use is permissible, even if it implies [something unsuitable], like al-ṣabūr, al-shakūr, and al-ḥalīm. For al-ṣabūr implies that hardship reaches Him, exalted is He, because patience is restraining the soul in the face of hardships, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who does not hasten punishment upon those who disobey Him. Al-shakūr implies that beneficence reaches Him, because its meaning is one who gives much thanks to one who does good to him, while all beneficence is from Allah, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who rewards for a few acts of obedience with many degrees (of reward) and gives in exchange for deeds done in a limited number of days unlimited favors in the Hereafter; and it was said: the One Who rewards for thankfulness; and it was said: the One Who praises the one who obeys Him. Al-ḥalīm implies that harm reaches Him, and no one reaches Him with harm, so it is interpreted with respect to Him, exalted is He, as the One Who does not hasten punishment upon those who disobey Him, so it comes back to the meaning of al-ṣabūr. And our statement: 'And He, exalted is He, no one reaches Him with harm' is not refuted by his statement: 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah'—because its meaning is that he did the act of one who harms [as if harming the Prophet and Allah in terms of the act], and it has preceded for you that the names of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are tawqīfiyyah by consensus, and the wisdom of that has preceded, so be attentive to it. His statement: (likewise the attributes) means: like His names, exalted is He, His attributes are tawqīfiyyah, so it is not permissible to affirm an attribute for Him, exalted is He, except by a tawqīf from the Lawgiver to us. And his statement: (so preserve the transmitted [names and attributes]) means: when you know that applying names and attributes to Him, exalted is He, depends on the legal permission, then preserve the names and attributes that are transmitted (samʿiyyah) literally, like those in the Book and Sunnah, or effectively, like those established by consensus, such as al-Ṣāniʿ, al-Mawjūd, al-Wājib, and al-Qadīm, as the author mentioned in his 'Great [Commentary]'. And his statement: (and the attribute is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence) means it indicates that additional meaning alone, like the word (qudra), for it indicates the meaning subsisting in His essence, glorified and exalted is He; and by this it is known that the author intends by 'attributes' in his statement: (likewise the attributes are names indicating matters established for the essence) and they are also tawqīfiyyah, so one should not express Allah's power as 'al-jarāʿah' for example due to its non-occurrence [in revelation].
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the rule that divine names and attributes are tawqīfiyyah, meaning they can only be derived from revelation. The commentator begins by explaining the term tawqīfiyyah: the permissibility of applying a name to God depends on its occurrence in the Quran, an authentic or good hadith, or consensus, because God is not beyond these sources. Then he discusses the weak hadith: if the matter is doctrinal (ʿilmiyyāt), a weak hadith is insufficient, but if it is practical (ʿamaliyyāt), a weak hadith suffices because weak hadiths are acted upon in the virtues of deeds. Next, he addresses qiyās (analogy): some say it is like consensus unless it is weak, and thus the name 'Wāhib' could be analogized from 'Wahhāb' because 'Wāhib' is not reported, but others forbid qiyās; the author supports forbidding it because synonyms may imply different connotations. Then he gives a summary: any name the Lawgiver has permitted in revelation is permissible even if its literal meaning implies something unsuitable, but such names must be interpreted properly to avoid anthropomorphism. He provides three examples: al-Ṣabūr (the Patient) implies that hardship reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who does not hasten punishment; al-Shakūr (the Thankful) implies that beneficence reaches God, so it is interpreted as the One who rewards abundantly for little obedience; al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing) implies that harm reaches God, so it is interpreted similarly to al-Ṣabūr. Then he addresses an objection: the hadith 'Whoever harms a Muslim has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah' does not contradict the principle that no one reaches God with harm, because it means the person did an act of harming, not that harm actually reaches God. He then confirms that even the Prophet's names are tawqīfiyyah by consensus. Finally, he defines attribute (ṣifa): it indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, like qudra (power), and thus attributes are also tawqīfiyyah; it is forbidden to use synonyms not found in revelation, such as 'al-jarāʿah' for power.

Key Terms
الصفات
توقيفية
القُدرة
السمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-Ṣabūr
B) al-Mawjūd
C) al-Raḥmān
D) al-Jarāʿah

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are tawqifiyyah; they must be derived from revelation and are defined as indicating an additional meaning beyond the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you ever describe Allah using terms that you think fit His majesty but have no basis in revelation, simply because they feel right to you? What would it cost you to restrict your speech about Allah only to what He has taught you?

Temporal Attributes: Actions of Allah, Not Essence
Ash'ari classification of temporal attributes as attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunnah view attributes like al-ridha and al-ghadab as attributes of action rather than essence, and how they affirm their effects while negating their subsistence in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes of action reflect His will, not change in His essence.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial distinction in Ash'ari theology: some divine attributes, such as pleasure and anger, are not eternal attributes subsisting in Allah's essence, but rather attributes of His actions. This prevents any notion of change or temporality in Allah while affirming His real responses to creation. The lesson will clarify this position and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation to understand the key points.
Notice that the passage first lists the temporal attributes, then explains the Ash'ari stance.
2
Identify the two categories of attributes
Identify that al-ridha and al-ghadab are called temporal attributes. Understand that Ahl al-Sunnah negate their subsistence in the essence but affirm their concomitants, making them attributes of action.
This is the core distinction: attributes of essence vs. attributes of action.
3
Understand the interpretation
The commentator explains that these attributes are interpreted either by their source or outcome, ultimately referring to the divine will. Reflect on how this avoids implying change in Allah.
For example, mercy is interpreted as bestowing favor, which is an act of will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصفات الحادثة : الرضا والغضب، نفاها أهل السنة بمعنى قيامها بالذات، وأثبتوا لوازمها، فهي صفات أفعال عندهم لا صفات ذات . ونفي الصفات صفات النقص على التفصيل وارد [؟] وقول من يقول : (قولك للملك : لست لصا ، ولست سفيهاً، ولست ... دليل على النقص .. خطأ؛ لأنه عقلاً ولغةً لا نقص فيه، بل النقص عرفي، وليس بيننا وبين الله تعالى أعراف، ونحن إنما نفي النقص عند وجود القائل به، وإلا يكفينا النفي الإجمالي.
وتفسير هذه الصفات إما بمنشئها كـ (الإنعام) للرحمة، أو بنهايتها كـ (الإنعام) أيضاً، فهي صفة فعل، فتعود للإرادة .
And the temporal attributes: al-ridha (pleasure) and al-ghadab (anger). Ahl al-Sunnah negated them in the sense of their subsisting in the essence, and they affirmed their concomitants. So they are attributes of actions according to them, not attributes of essence. And the negation of the attributes of deficiency in detail is reported? And the saying of one who says: 'Your saying to a king: You are not a thief, you are not foolish, you are not ... is evidence of deficiency' is wrong, because intellectually and linguistically there is no deficiency in it; rather deficiency is customary, and between us and Allah, Most High, there are no customs. And we only negate deficiency when there is someone who asserts it; otherwise summary negation suffices us. And the interpretation of these attributes is either by their source like (bestowing favor) for mercy, or by their outcome like (bestowing favor) as well; so they are an attribute of action, and they return to will.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage discusses how to understand attributes like al-ridha (pleasure) and al-ghadab (anger) that seem to imply change in Allah. The commentator says Ahl al-Sunnah negate these attributes in the sense that they subsist in the essence (because that would imply temporality in the divine), but they affirm their concomitants (their effects). Thus these are considered attributes of action (sifat af'al), not attributes of essence (sifat dhat). The commentator then addresses a common mistake: some think that saying 'You are not a thief' to a king implies deficiency, but the commentator refutes this, arguing that such a negation is not a deficiency intellectually or linguistically; deficiency is a customary concept, and there are no customs between us and Allah. Therefore, we only negate such deficiencies when someone falsely attributes them to Allah; otherwise a general negation (nafi ijmaili) suffices. Finally, he notes that attributes like mercy can be interpreted either by their source (e.g., bestowing favor) or by their outcome (again, bestowing favor), and they are ultimately referred to the divine will. This passage clarifies the Ash'ari position on temporal attributes.

Key Terms
صفات حادثة
صفات أفعال
صفات ذات
الرضا
الغضب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming attributes like anger in Allah implies He experiences change or human-like emotions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these are attributes of action, not essence, and are interpreted via their effects, thus avoiding change in the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do Ahl al-Sunnah treat attributes like al-ridha and al-ghadab?
A) They affirm them as essential attributes.
B) They negate them entirely.
C) They negate their subsistence in the essence but affirm their effects.
D) They consider them metaphors with no reality.

Key Takeaway
Temporal attributes like pleasure and anger are attributes of Allah's actions, not His essence, and they ultimately refer to His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Understanding the Negation of Deficiency from Allah
Refutation of a misunderstanding about negating deficiency from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct manner of negating deficiency from Allah, distinguishing between customary and rational deficiency, and the principle that detailed negation is only needed when someone falsely attributes deficiency.

Core Idea
Negating deficiency from Allah is a response to false claims, not an admission of potential.

This passage addresses a subtle issue: when we say 'Allah is not a thief,' does that imply a deficiency? The commentator refutes this, explaining that such a negation is not a deficiency in language or reason; rather, deficiency is a customary concept, and there are no customs between us and Allah. He also clarifies that detailed negation is only necessary when someone specifically attributes deficiency; otherwise, a general negation suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage to understand the commentator's argument. Then read the translation to grasp the refutation.
The passage begins with a reference to a mistaken view.
2
Understand the refutation
Identify why the commentator says the view is wrong: intellectually and linguistically there is no deficiency in such negation; deficiency is customary, and there are no customs with Allah.
This is key: deficiency is urfi (customary), not rational or linguistic.
3
Learn the principle
The commentator states that detailed negation is only used when someone has made a false attribution; otherwise summary negation suffices. Reflect on how this protects divine transcendence.
Summary negation: saying 'Allah is free from all deficiency' is enough.

Imam Malik on the Verse of Istiwa: The Salaf Approach
Narrates Imam Malik's famous answer on istiwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Salaf methodology for dealing with divine attributes mentioned in ambiguous Quranic verses, as exemplified by Imam Malik's famous response.

Core Idea
Affirm the meaning, negate the modality, and do not ask 'how'.

One of the most frequently quoted anecdotes in Islamic theology is Imam Malik's answer to a man who asked about the meaning of 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa.' This brief exchange encapsulates the entire Salaf approach to the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses). Let us examine the text and unpack its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Imagine the setting: a man asks Imam Malik about the verse, and Malik pauses before delivering a balanced response.
Notice the gravity of Malik's tone — he orders the man removed.
2
Analyze the four-part response
Identify the four elements: 1) istiwa is not unknown in language, 2) the modality (kayf) is beyond human comprehension, 3) belief in what Allah says is obligatory, 4) asking about 'how' is an innovation.
These four points are the core of the Salaf position.
3
Understand the principle of tafwid
Reflect on how this response demonstrates tafwid — consigning the precise modality of divine attributes to Allah while affirming their reality.
Avoid the common mistake of thinking this rejects all interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل رجل الإمام مالكاً عن هذه الآية فأطرق رأسه مليا، ثم قال: الاستواء غير مجهول، والكيف غير معقول والإيمان به واجب، والسؤال عنه بدعة، وما أظنُّك إلا ضالا ، فأمر به فأخرج
And a man asked Imam Malik about this verse. He bowed his head for a long time, then said: 'Al-istiwa' is not unknown, the 'how' is not comprehensible, belief in it is obligatory, and asking about it is an innovation. I do not think you are anything but misguided.' Then he ordered that he be taken out.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a historical anecdote illustrating the classic Salaf approach to the mutashabihat. A man asks Imam Malik about the meaning of 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa.' Imam Malik's response is carefully balanced: he affirms that the word 'istiwa'' itself is known in the Arabic language (it is not an unknown term), but the modality (kayf) is beyond human comprehension. Belief in what God has stated is obligatory, yet inquiring into the 'how' is a blameworthy innovation. He warns the questioner that such questioning is a sign of misguidance, and he has the man removed. The response encapsulates the principle of tafwid: accept the text without asking how, and do not attempt to interpret it into a figurative meaning, while still affirming the reality of the attribute. This episode is frequently cited in kalam works to support the position that one should not delve into the modality of divine attributes.

Key Terms
الاستواء
الكيف
الإيمان به واجب
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret Malik's words as a rejection of any interpretation (ta'wil) altogether.
Correction: His statement is part of the Salaf method of tafwid, which affirms the reality of the attribute but consigns the modality to Allah; it does not forbid all forms of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) absolutely, though caution is advised.

Checkpoint
What did Imam Malik say about asking about the 'how' of istiwa?
A) It is permissible if done respectfully.
B) It is a necessary part of faith.
C) It is an innovation (bid'a).
D) It is obligatory for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik's response teaches us to affirm the reality of divine attributes while refraining from asking about their modality, as such inquiry is considered a blameworthy innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses describing Allah's attributes and feel an inner restlessness for an explanation, can you humbly submit that some things are beyond your comprehension, or do you demand to know the how? What will you do to train your heart to accept tafwid?
?
Are you ever tempted to debate or question the attributes of Allah beyond what is revealed, entertaining doubts or philosophical arguments? How can you guard yourself against falling into innovation like the questioner?
?
Imagine someone asks you about the verse of istiwa: would your response reflect the same balance of affirmation and restraint that Malik displayed, or would you be quick to interpret or dismiss? What must you learn from this incident to protect your own faith?

Salaf and Khalaf on Divine Face, Hand, and Fingers
Explanation of Salaf and Khalaf views on God's face, hand, and fingers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main approaches (Salaf and Khalaf) to interpreting verses and hadith that mention God's face, hand, and fingers.

Core Idea
God's face, hand, and fingers are literal for Salaf, symbolic for Khalaf.

In this lesson, we examine how Muslim theologians handle apparent anthropomorphic descriptions of God. The passage lists three scriptural examples and contrasts two schools of thought. This is fundamental to understanding Islamic positions on divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the three expressions and two interpretations.
Notice the three references: face (verse), hand (verse), fingers (hadith).
2
Understand the Salaf position
The Salaf affirm that God has a face, hand, and fingers, but without asking how (tafwid).
Their approach is to take the literal wording while denying any physical similarity.
3
Understand the Khalaf interpretation
The Khalaf reinterpret: face means essence, hand means power, and 'between two fingers' means between two attributes (power and will).
This avoids anthropomorphism while preserving the attributes.
4
Compare the two views
Reflect on the difference: one leaves the meaning to God, the other assigns a metaphorical meaning.
Both aim to uphold Tawhid, but via different methodologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممَّا يُوهم الجوارح قوله تعالى: ﴿وَسَقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ الرَّحمن: ٢٧] ويدُ اللَّهِ فَوْقَ أَيْدِيهِمْ ﴾ [الفتح: ١٠]، وحديث مسلم : إنَّ قلوب بني آدمَ كُلَّها كقلب واحدٍ بين إصبعين مِنْ أصابع الرحمن (۱) ، فالسلف يقولون : الله وجه ويد وأصابع لا نعلمها، والخلف يقولون: المراد من الوجه الذاتُ، وباليد القدرة، والمراد من قوله : بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن بين صفتين من صفاته، وهاتان الصفتان: القدرة والإرادة (۲).
And among what suggests [that God has] limbs are His sayings: 'And there remains the Face of your Lord' [ar-Rahman 27] and 'The hand of God is over their hands' [al-Fath 10], and the hadith of Muslim: 'Indeed the hearts of the children of Adam, all of them, are like a single heart between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful' (1). So the Salaf say: God has a face, a hand, and fingers — we do not know them. And the Khalaf say: what is meant by face is the Essence, and by hand is power, and what is meant by His saying 'between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful' is between two of His attributes, and these two attributes are power and will (2).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses three anthropomorphic expressions: God's face, hand, and fingers. For God's face, the verse from Surat ar-Rahman is cited; for God's hand, the verse from al-Fath; for the fingers, the hadith of Muslim. The Salaf position is to affirm these attributes without asking how (tafwid). The Khalaf interpret: the face means the essence (dhat), the hand means power (qudra), and the fingers mean two attributes — power and will (irada). This interpretation avoids corporealism while maintaining the reality of God's attributes.

الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Khalaf reject the attributes entirely.
Correction: The Khalaf do not reject the attributes; they reinterpret them metaphorically while affirming their reality.

Checkpoint
According to the Khalaf, what do the 'two fingers' in the hadith refer to?
A) Two literal fingers
B) Two angels
C) Two attributes: power and will
D) Two parts of the heart

Key Takeaway
The Salaf affirm divine attributes without asking how, while the Khalaf reinterpret anthropomorphic terms to preserve transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Salaf are content to say 'we do not know how' these attributes are true. Can you live with that uncertainty about God, or do you feel compelled to find an explanation? What does your need for certainty reveal about your trust in God?

The Quran as Eternal Speech and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Assertion of the Quran's eternity and refutation of Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the eternal internal speech of God (kalam nafsi) and the created verbal expression, and why Ahl al-Sunna affirm the former and refute the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The Quran as God's speech is eternal, yet its recited words are created.

This lesson addresses a key theological dispute regarding the nature of the Quran. The author defends the Sunni position that God's attribute of speech is eternal and uncreated, while clarifying that the physical words we recite are temporal. This distinction refutes the Mu'tazila claim that the entire Quran is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow. Note the two contrasting positions presented.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'kalam nafsi' and 'lafz'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
Identify why the Mu'tazila concluded that God's speech must be created. Their reasoning is based on letters and sounds being temporal.
They consider speech to be composed of letters and sounds, which are created.
3
Grasp the Sunni distinction
Learn the crucial difference between internal speech (kalam nafsi) as an eternal attribute and the uttered words (lafz) as created. Ahl al-Sunna affirm only the former as uncreated.
This distinction is central to the Sunni creed.
4
Note the practical caution
Understand why the imams forbade saying 'the Quran is created' even when referring to the uttered words, except in a teaching context.
It could cause misunderstanding about God's eternal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القرآنُ كلامُ الله تعالى الأزلي ٤١ - وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ أَيْ: كَلامَهُ عَنِ الحُدُوثِ وَاحْذَرِ انْتِقَامَهُ قوله : (وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ ... إلخ) أي : واعتقد أيُّها المكلف تنزه القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى عن الحدوث، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين بخلق الكلام، زعماً منهم أن من لوازمه الحروف والأصوات وذلك مستحيل عليه تعالى؛ فكلام الله تعالى عندهم مخلوق لأن الله خلقه في بعض الأجرام، ومذهب أهل السنة أن القرآن بمعنى الكلام النفسي ليس بمخلوق ؛ وأما القرآن بمعنى اللفظ الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق، لكن يمتنع أن يقال: القرآن مخلوق ويراد به اللفظ الذي نقرؤه إلا في مقام التعليم ؛ لأنَّه ربَّما أوهم أن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى مخلوق، ولذلك امتنعت الأئمة من القول بخلق القرآن.
The Quran — the eternal speech of God. 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, to be transcendent of temporality, and beware of His vengeance.' His saying: ('And declare the Quran... etc.') means: Believe, O responsible person, that the Quran, meaning His speech, is transcendent of temporality, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that speech is created, claiming that its necessary concomitants are letters and sounds, and that is impossible for Him, Exalted is He. So the speech of God, according to them, is created because God created it in some bodies. And the school of Ahl al-Sunna is that the Quran in the sense of internal speech (kalam nafsi) is not created. As for the Quran in the sense of the utterance (lafz) that we recite, it is created. But it is forbidden to say 'the Quran is created' meaning the utterance we recite, except in a teaching context, because it might give the impression that the Quran as God's speech is created. Therefore, the imams refrained from saying 'the Quran is created.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of the poem: 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, to be transcendent of temporality, and beware of His vengeance.' The author explains that one must believe the Quran (as God's attribute of speech) is eternal, not created. This opposes the Mu'tazila who argued that speech necessarily involves letters and sounds, which are temporal, so God's speech must be created in some object. Ahl al-Sunna distinguish between the internal, self-subsistent speech (kalam nafsi), which is eternal and uncreated, and the verbal expression (lafz), which is created. However, the author warns that even when one means the created utterance, one should not say 'the Quran is created' except in a teaching context, because it could be misunderstood as referring to God's eternal speech. The early imams forbade this phrase precisely to avoid such confusion.

الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunna believe the Quran we recite is also uncreated.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna distinguish: the internal speech (kalam nafsi) is uncreated, but the recited words and letters are created.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, which aspect of the Quran is not created?
A) The written letters
B) The recited sounds
C) The internal speech (kalam nafsi)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Quran as God's eternal attribute of speech is uncreated, while its verbal expression in creation is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Mu'tazila denied God's eternal speech because they could not reconcile it with letters and sounds. What attributes of God do you struggle to accept, and could it be because you are measuring Him by your own experience? Will you submit to revelation where your intellect falls short?
