Quran as God's Eternal Speech and Refutation of Createdness
Definition of Quran as eternal speech and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the Quran as God's eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi) and the created recited words, and why the Mu'tazila view is rejected.

Core Idea
The Quran as God's speech is eternal; the words we recite are created.

This lesson tackles a major theological dispute: whether the Quran is created or eternal. The author clarifies the Sunni position by distinguishing two aspects of the Quran: the eternal internal speech of Allah and the temporal recited expressions. This distinction refutes the Mu'tazila while avoiding confusion about applying 'created' to the Quran absolutely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the author contrasts the Mu'tazila claim with the Sunni position.
The passage begins with a poetic line and immediately unpacks its meaning.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'Quran'
Identify the distinction between internal speech (kalam nafsi) and uttered speech (lafdh). Note which is eternal and which is created according to Ahl al-Sunna.
The internal speech is an attribute of Allah, hence eternal.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila argue that speech necessarily involves letters and sounds, which are temporal. Understand why this leads them to claim the Quran is created.
They also hold that God cannot be attributed with temporal things.
4
Learn the caution about saying 'the Quran is created'
The Imams forbade saying 'the Quran is created' even if one means the recited words, because it may be misunderstood as referring to God's eternal speech.
This caution is for protecting the belief from misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القرآنُ كلامُ الله تعالى الأزلي] ٤١ - وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ أَيْ: كَلامَهُ عَنِ الحُدُوثِ وَاحْذَرِ انْتِقَامَهُ قوله : (وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ ... إلخ) أي : واعتقد أيُّها المكلف تنزه القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى عن الحدوث، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين بخلق الكلام، زعماً منهم أن من لوازمه الحروف والأصوات وذلك مستحيل عليه تعالى؛ فكلام الله تعالى عندهم مخلوق لأن الله خلقه في بعض الأجرام، ومذهب أهل السنة أن القرآن بمعنى الكلام النفسي ليس بمخلوق ؛ وأما القرآن بمعنى اللفظ الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق، لكن يمتنع أن يقال: القرآن مخلوق ويراد به اللفظ الذي نقرؤه إلا في مقام التعليم ؛ لأنَّه ربَّما أوهم أن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى مخلوق، ولذلك امتنعت الأئمة من القول بخلق القرآن.
[The Quran is the eternal speech of Allah Almighty] 41 - And deem the Quran, i.e. His speech, transcendent of temporality (huduth), and beware of His vengeance. His statement: 'And deem the Quran ... etc.' Meaning: And believe, O legally responsible person, that the Quran, meaning His speech, is transcendent of temporality, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that speech is created, claiming that its necessary concomitants are letters and sounds, and that is impossible for Him; so God's speech according to them is created because God created it in some bodies. For that reason, the Imams refrained from saying 'the Quran is created'.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It begins by quoting the line from the poem 'And deem the Quran...' and then provides the commentary. It defines the Quran as God's eternal speech (al-kalām al-azalī), stating that it is not temporally originated (ḥādith). It refutes the Mu'tazila who claim that speech is created because it entails letters and sounds. Then it clarifies the Sunni position: the Quran as internal speech (kalam nafsi) is uncreated, but the recited words are created. However, one should not say 'the Quran is created' in absolute terms because it may be misunderstood.

Superiority of the Quran or the Prophet?
Compares whether the Quran as recited words is superior or the Prophet Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over the superiority of the Quran versus the Prophet, and why refraining from comparison is the safest position.

Core Idea
When in doubt, silence is golden – refrain from comparing the Quran and the Prophet.

This lesson explores a nuanced theological question: Is the recited Quran superior to the Prophet Muhammad? The author presents a weak supporting tradition, offers a reasoned argument for Allah's superiority over the Quran, and ultimately advises suspending judgment. Let's examine the reasoning and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text slowly. Notice the punctuation and flow. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is concise; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the two views
The author presents two positions: one based on a weak hadith claiming every letter is better than Muhammad, and the correct view that Allah is better than the Quran.
3
Understand the argument
The argument: Allah is better than every created thing. The Quran is an act of the reciter (a created act), and the Prophet is better than the reciter and all his acts. Therefore, the Prophet is better than the Quran.
This follows from the principle that the agent is better than the act.
4
Learn the final recommendation
Despite the argument, the safest position is to refrain from making such comparisons altogether, as ignorance of this matter does not harm one's faith.
This reflects scholarly caution.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider why avoiding unnecessary theological speculation is valued in Islamic tradition. How does this apply to other debates?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل القرآن بمعنى اللفظ المقروء ،أفضل، أو سيدنا محمد ؟ تمسك بعضهم بما يُروى: كُلُّ حرف خيرٌ من محمَّدٍ وآلِ محمدٍ»، لكنه غير محقق الثبوت . والحقُّ أَنَّه الله أفضلُ ؛ لأنَّه أفضلُ من كلِّ ،مخلوقٍ، كما يُؤخذ من كلام الجلال المحلى على «البردة) ، ويؤيده أنه فعل القارئ، والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضل من القارئ وجميع أفعاله، والأسلم الوقف عن مثل هذا ؛ فإنّه لا يضر خلو الذهنِ عنه انتهى - ملخصاً من حاشية الشيخ الأمير .
Is the Quran in the sense of the recited word better, or our master Muhammad? Some clung to what is narrated: 'Every letter is better than Muhammad and the family of Muhammad,' but it is not verified as authentic. And the truth is that Allah is better [than the Quran]; because He is better than every created thing, as is taken from the words of al-Jalal al-Mahalli on al-Burda, and it is supported by the fact that [the Quran] is an act of the reciter, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, is better than the reciter and all his acts. And the safest is to refrain from such [comparison]; for vacating the mind of it does no harm. End – summarized from the gloss of al-Shaykh al-Amir.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a question: Is the Quran (as the recited word) superior, or is the Prophet Muhammad superior? Some rely on a hadith saying 'every letter is better than Muhammad and his family,' but the author notes this hadith is not verified. The correct view, according to the author, is that Allah is better (أفضل) than the Quran because He is better than all creation, as derived from al-Jalal al-Mahalli's commentary on al-Burda. Additionally, the Quran is an action (فعل) of the reciter, and the Prophet is better than the reciter and all his actions. Therefore, the Quran as a created act is less than the Prophet. However, the safest approach is to refrain from such comparisons altogether, as ignorance of this matter does not harm one's faith. The passage ends by citing the gloss of al-Shaykh al-Amir.

Key Terms

اللفظ المقروء
سيدنا محمد
الحق
فعل القارئ
الوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must definitively decide whether the Quran or the Prophet is superior; it is a necessary theological question.
Correction: The passage teaches that the safest and recommended stance is to refrain from making such comparisons, as it does not affect one's faith.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final recommendation regarding the comparison of the Quran and the Prophet?
A) The Quran is definitely better
B) The Prophet is definitely better
C) It is safest to refrain from such comparisons
D) Both are equal

Key Takeaway
The safest approach is to refrain from comparing the Quran and the Prophet, as it does not harm one's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a theological question that scholars say it is safer to refrain from, does your heart feel restlessness or contentment? Will you trust their wisdom and let go of the need to know?
?
The passage teaches that vacating your mind of this comparison does no harm. What other debates or comparisons have you been holding onto that distract you from sincere devotion? Are you willing to drop them today?
?
Consider your love for the Prophet ﷺ and for the Quran. Does your heart find peace in following the safer path of refraining, or do you feel a desire to determine which is greater? Examine your intention: Is it for Allah and His Messenger, or for your own ego?

Refuting the Createdness of the Quran
Refutes Mu'tazila claim that the Quran is created, addressing the makhluq/muhdath nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran is not created and how the Mu'tazila argument is refuted, including the futile distinction between 'muhdath' and 'makhluq'.

Core Idea
Both 'created' and 'originated' deny the Quran's eternity — a futile distinction.

This lesson tackles the Mu'tazila claim that the Quran is created. The author shows that whether you call it 'created' (makhluq) or 'originated' (muhdath), the problem remains: it would not be eternal. The commentary illustrates this with a vivid analogy. The lesson ends with a stark warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Get the overall argument before diving into details.
Notice the use of 'al-huduth' and 'makhluq'.
2
Identify the two terms
Identify 'makhluq' (created) and 'muhdath' (originated). Understand why al-Balkhi thought 'makhluq' implies falsehood and his preference for 'muhdath'.
Al-Balkhi is a Mu'tazili who made a subtle distinction.
3
Understand the refutation
See how the author refutes al-Balkhi by equating 'huduth' with 'khalq'—both imply temporal origination. The rain/drainpipe analogy shows the futility of his distinction.
The analogy: fleeing rain only to stand under a drainpipe.
4
Reflect on the warning
Consider the final warning: 'Beware of His vengeance.' Understand the high stakes of this theological error.
This is a strong deterrent against accepting createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (عَنِ الحُدُوثِ) أي : الوجود بعد العدم، فليس مخلوقاً، بل هو صفة ذاته العليَّةِ، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بأنه مخلوقٌ، وليس صفة ذاته العليَّة، وإِنَّما عبر بالحدوث مع أن المشهور بين القوم التعبير بالخلق لضرورة النظم، أو للرد على محمد البلخي من المعتزلة القائل: بأن كلام الله تعالى مُحدثٌ وليس بمخلوق)، زعماً منه أن قولنا : (مخلوق) يُوهم أنه كذب يتعالى الله عنه، ورُدَّ بأن الحدوث مثل الخلق، فهو كمن هرب من المطر، ووقف تحت الميزاب انتهى مصنف في صغيره . قوله : (وَاحْذَرِ انْتِقَامَهُ) أي : وخفِ انتقام الله منك، إن قلت بحدوثه.
His saying: 'about origination (al-huduth)' means existence after non-existence. So it [the Quran] is not created, rather it is an attribute of His supreme essence, contrary to the Mu'tazila in their saying that it is created and that it is not an attribute of His supreme essence. And he only expressed [the matter] with 'origination' (al-huduth) while the common expression among the people is 'creation' (al-khalq) due to the necessity of the poetic meter, or to refute Muhammad al-Balkhi of the Mu'tazila who said that the speech of Allah is originated (muhdath) and not created (makhluq), claiming that our saying 'created' implies falsehood — exalted is Allah above that. And it was refuted by [saying] that origination (huduth) is like creation (khalq), so he is like one who flees from rain and stands under a drainpipe. End — [as] the author stated in his short work. His saying: 'And beware of His vengeance' means fear Allah's vengeance upon you if you say it is originated.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that the Quran is created. The commentator explains that the phrase 'about origination' (عن الحدوث) means existence after non-existence, and that the Quran is not created (مخلوق) but rather an attribute of Allah's essence. He notes that the poet used 'origination' (حدوث) instead of the more common term 'creation' (خلق) either because of poetic necessity or to refute Muhammad al-Balkhi, who distinguished between 'originated' (محدث) and 'created' (مخلوق), claiming that 'created' implies falsehood. The refutation is that both terms imply the same thing regarding the Quran: it would not be eternal. The example of a person fleeing rain only to stand under a drainpipe illustrates the futility of such a distinction. The passage then includes a warning: 'Beware of His vengeance' — meaning fear Allah's punishment if you say the Quran is originated/created.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling the Quran 'originated' (muhdath) is different from 'created' (makhluq) and avoids implying falsehood.
Correction: The passage shows both terms imply temporal origination, so 'originated' is just as problematic as 'created'. Both deny that the Quran is an eternal attribute of Allah.

Key Takeaway

Terminological Foundations: Qur'an and the Speech of Allah
Establishes terminological foundations and refutes a Mu'tazilite distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the terms 'Qur'an' and 'speech of Allah' and how they apply to the eternal attribute versus the recited words.

Core Idea
The term 'Qur'an' most often refers to the recited words, not the eternal attribute.

Before we can interpret scriptural evidence about the Qur'an's createdness, we need to clarify what the terms 'Qur'an' and 'speech of Allah' actually mean. The poem's commentator explains that these terms are used in two senses: the eternal, uncreated attribute of speech in God's essence (kalam nafsi) and the verbal, recited words (kalam lafzi). This lesson establishes that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's precise terminology.
Pay attention to the words 'الأكثر' (most often) which indicates common usage.
2
Identify the two terms and their applications
Note that both 'Qur'an' and 'the speech of Allah' are applied to both the psychological and verbal senses, but with different frequencies.
The commentator explicitly states which is more common for each term.
3
Understand the meaning of 'huduth'
The phrase 'an al-huduthi' is explained as existence after non-existence, and the divine speech is not created but an attribute of God's essence.
This directly opposes the Mu'tazila position.
4
Analyse the refutation of al-Balkhi
The commentator explains why the poet used 'huduth' instead of 'khalq' — to refute Muhammad al-Balkhi's claim that 'created' implies falsehood, showing that the distinction is futile.
The metaphor 'fled from rain and stood under a waterspout' is key.

المعنى، وعبّر عنه جبريل بألفاظ من عنده وقيل : المنزَّلُ المعنى، وعبر عنه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من عنده لكن التحقيق الأول؛ لأنَّ الله خلقه أولاً في اللوح المحفوظ، ثم أنزله في صحائف إلى سماء الدنيا في محلّ يُقال له : (بيت العزة) في ليلة القدر، كما قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ) [القدر: ١، ثم أنزله على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مفرقاً بحسب الوقائع .
وقوله : (الذِي قَدْ دَلَّا) صفةٌ للفظ ؛ والألف في (دلا) للإطلاق، والمراد الذي قد دلّ على الصفة القديمة (۱) بطريق دلالة الالتزام كما تقدَّمَ .
والحاصلُ أنَّ كلَّ ظاهرٍ من الكتاب والسنة دلَّ على حدوث القرآن، فهو محمول على اللفظ المقروء، لا على الكلام النفسي ، لكن يمتنع أن يقال : القرآن مخلوق إلا في مقام التعليم كما سبق .
The application of 'Qur'an' to the recited utterance, repelling the misconception of that by interpreting it as His speech, exalted is He. So 'Qur'an' is applied to both the psychological and the verbal, but most often it is applied to the verbal. As for 'the speech of Allah', it is also applied to both the psychological and the verbal, but most often it is applied to the psychological. And it has preceded in the discussion of speech in more detail, so refer to it if you wish (1).
His saying: ('an al-huduthi) meaning: existence after non-existence, so it is not created, rather it is an attribute of His exalted essence, contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that it is created and not an attribute of His exalted essence. And he used the term 'huduth' (originated) even though the common usage among the people is the term 'khalq' (creation) due to necessity of the poetic meter, or to refute Muhammad al-Balkhi of the Mu'tazila who said: 'The speech of Allah is originated (muhdath) and not created (makhluq),' claiming from him that our saying 'created' suggests it is falsehood, exalted is Allah above that. And it was refuted by saying that originatedness is like creation, so he is like one who fled from rain and stood under a waterspout. Ended what the author said in his shorter work (2).
His saying: (wa-ihdhar intiqamahu) meaning: and fear Allah's retribution against you if you say it is originated.
42 - So every text that indicates originatedness, interpret it as referring to the utterance that indicates [the eternal attribute].
His saying: (fa-kullu nassin... etc.) meaning: when you have realized what preceded, then every text... etc., so the 'fa' is the 'fa' of explication. This is in reality a response to what the Mu'tazila have clung to from the texts that indicate originatedness, such as 'Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance' [al-Hijr: 9]. And what is meant by 'text' is the apparent meaning of the Book or the Sunnah.
And his saying: (lil-huduthi dalla) meaning: it indicates the originatedness of the Qur'an, so 'lam' is in the sense of 'ala (upon), and the alif in 'dalla' is for absolute expression. And his saying: (ihmil... etc.) is the predicate of the subject 'kull', and the connector is omitted, and the estimation is: interpret it ... etc.
And his saying: ('ala al-lafzi) meaning: upon the Qur'an in the sense of the utterance revealed to our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, which is worshipped by its recitation, and which is challenged by the shortest sura of it. And the more correct view is that what is revealed is the utterance and the meaning. And it was said: what is revealed is (1) See p. 218. (2) See Hidaya al-Mureed 1/509.
The Divine Matters
the meaning, and Gabriel expressed it with words from himself. And it was said: what is revealed is the meaning, and the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, expressed it from himself. But the verification is the first, because Allah created it first in the Preserved Tablet, then sent it down in sheets to the heaven of the world in a place called 'the House of Might' on the Night of Decree, as He said: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' [al-Qadr: 1], then He sent it down to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, piecemeal according to events.
And his saying: (alladhi qad dalla) is a description of the utterance; and the alif in 'dalla' is for absolute expression, and what is meant is that which indicates the eternal attribute (1) by way of implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam) as has preceded.
And the conclusion is that every apparent [text] from the Book and the Sunnah that indicates the originatedness of the Qur'an is to be interpreted as referring to the recited utterance, not to the psychological speech. But it is forbidden to say 'the Qur'an is created' except in a teaching context, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on a line of the poem (line 42) that instructs the reader how to interpret scriptural evidence that appears to indicate the Qur'an is created (hadith). The author begins by clarifying the terminology: the term 'Qur'an' is applied to both the eternal, uncreated attribute of speech in God's essence (al-kalam al-nafsi) and to the recited words in human language (al-lafz al-maqru'), but most often it is used for the latter. Conversely, 'the speech of Allah' (kalam Allah) is also applied to both, but more commonly to the eternal attribute. This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.

Next, the author explains the phrase 'an al-huduthi' (from origination) in the poem: it means existence after non-existence. The divine speech is not created (makhluq); it is an attribute of God's essence. This opposes the Mu'tazila who claim it is created and not an attribute. The commentator notes that the poem uses 'huduth' instead of the more common 'khalq' either for poetic necessity or to refute the Mu'tazilite Muhammad al-Balkhi, who held that the Qur'an is 'originated' (muhdath) but not 'created', fearing that 'created' implies falsehood. The refutation is that 'originated' and 'created' are equivalent in meaning, so his attempt to avoid the term is futile — it is like fleeing rain only to stand under a downspout.

Then the line of the poem is quoted: 'So every text that indicates originatedness, interpret it as referring to the utterance that indicates [the eternal attribute].' The commentary explains that this is a response (jawaab) to the Mu'tazila's proof texts such as 'We have sent down the Remembrance' (15:9). 'Text' here means any apparent (zaahir) proof from Quran or Sunnah. The imperative 'ihmil' (interpret) applies to such texts: they must be understood as referring to the recited verbal Qur'an, not to God's eternal speech. The phrase 'alladhi qad dalla' (which indicates) describes the verbal utterance as that which points to the eternal attribute by way of implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam).

The author then discusses the nature of revelation: the more correct view is that both the meaning (ma'na) and the literal wording (lafz) were revealed from Allah; other views (that only meaning was revealed and Gabriel or the Prophet clothed it in words) are mentioned but the first is preferred because Allah first created it in the Preserved Tablet, then sent it down in sheets to the House of Might in the lowest heaven on Laylat al-Qadr, then revealed it piecemeal to the Prophet.

Finally, the conclusion is summarized: every apparent text from Quran and Sunnah that seems to point to the Qur'an's createdness must be interpreted as referring to the verbal, recited Qur'an, not to the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi). However, one is forbidden to say 'the Qur'an is created' except in a teaching context (maqam al-ta'lim), as clarified earlier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that 'originated' (huduth) is different from 'created' (khalq) to avoid implying falsehood about God.
Correction: The passage shows that originatedness and creation are equivalent; this attempted distinction is futile, like fleeing rain and standing under a waterspout.

Checkpoint

A) It refers to the eternal attribute of speech.
B) It refers to the recited verbal words.
C) It refers to the Preserved Tablet.
D) It refers to the psychological speech.

Key Takeaway
The terms 'Qur'an' and 'speech of Allah' are used in two senses, but the former more often refers to the recited words, the latter to the eternal attribute; the attempt to distinguish 'originated' from 'created' is futile.

The Rule: Interpreting Scriptural Evidence for Createdness
Gives the interpretive rule for createdness texts and explains the nature of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretive principle that all scriptural texts indicating the Qur'an's originatedness refer to the verbal, recited Qur'an, not to the eternal attribute.

Core Idea
Every apparent text about the Qur'an's createdness applies to the recited words, not God's eternal speech.

Having established the terminological distinction, the poem then provides the key hermeneutical rule for dealing with verses that seem to imply the Qur'an is created. This lesson unpacks that rule, shows how it refutes the Mu'tazilite use of proof texts, and clarifies the nature of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the line of poetry and its explanation
Read the Arabic line and the commentator's explanation. Note that it is a response to Mu'tazilite proof texts like 'We have sent down the Remembrance' (15:9).
The 'fa' at the beginning is the 'fa' of explication.
2
Understand the rule: 'ihmil' (interpret)
The imperative 'ihmil' commands that such texts be interpreted as referring to the verbal utterance, not the eternal speech.
The omitted connector is 'hu' (it) — 'interpret it as...'
3
Examine the phrase 'alladhi qad dalla'
This describes the verbal utterance as indicating the eternal attribute by way of implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam).
Implicative indication means that mentioning the recited Qur'an necessarily implies the eternal attribute.
4
Read the discussion on revelation
The commentator affirms that both the meaning (ma'na) and the wording (lafz) were revealed from Allah, recounting the process from the Preserved Tablet to the Prophet.
This counters views that only meaning was revealed and human agents added words.

المعنى، وعبّر عنه جبريل بألفاظ من عنده وقيل : المنزَّلُ المعنى، وعبر عنه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من عنده لكن التحقيق الأول؛ لأنَّ الله خلقه أولاً في اللوح المحفوظ، ثم أنزله في صحائف إلى سماء الدنيا في محلّ يُقال له : (بيت العزة) في ليلة القدر، كما قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ) [القدر: ١، ثم أنزله على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مفرقاً بحسب الوقائع .
وقوله : (الذِي قَدْ دَلَّا) صفةٌ للفظ ؛ والألف في (دلا) للإطلاق، والمراد الذي قد دلّ على الصفة القديمة (۱) بطريق دلالة الالتزام كما تقدَّمَ .
والحاصلُ أنَّ كلَّ ظاهرٍ من الكتاب والسنة دلَّ على حدوث القرآن، فهو محمول على اللفظ المقروء، لا على الكلام النفسي ، لكن يمتنع أن يقال : القرآن مخلوق إلا في مقام التعليم كما سبق .
The application of 'Qur'an' to the recited utterance, repelling the misconception of that by interpreting it as His speech, exalted is He. So 'Qur'an' is applied to both the psychological and the verbal, but most often it is applied to the verbal. As for 'the speech of Allah', it is also applied to both the psychological and the verbal, but most often it is applied to the psychological. And it has preceded in the discussion of speech in more detail, so refer to it if you wish (1).
His saying: ('an al-huduthi) meaning: existence after non-existence, so it is not created, rather it is an attribute of His exalted essence, contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that it is created and not an attribute of His exalted essence. And he used the term 'huduth' (originated) even though the common usage among the people is the term 'khalq' (creation) due to necessity of the poetic meter, or to refute Muhammad al-Balkhi of the Mu'tazila who said: 'The speech of Allah is originated (muhdath) and not created (makhluq),' claiming from him that our saying 'created' suggests it is falsehood, exalted is Allah above that. And it was refuted by saying that originatedness is like creation, so he is like one who fled from rain and stood under a waterspout. Ended what the author said in his shorter work (2).
His saying: (wa-ihdhar intiqamahu) meaning: and fear Allah's retribution against you if you say it is originated.
42 - So every text that indicates originatedness, interpret it as referring to the utterance that indicates [the eternal attribute].
His saying: (fa-kullu nassin... etc.) meaning: when you have realized what preceded, then every text... etc., so the 'fa' is the 'fa' of explication. This is in reality a response to what the Mu'tazila have clung to from the texts that indicate originatedness, such as 'Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance' [al-Hijr: 9]. And what is meant by 'text' is the apparent meaning of the Book or the Sunnah.
And his saying: (lil-huduthi dalla) meaning: it indicates the originatedness of the Qur'an, so 'lam' is in the sense of 'ala (upon), and the alif in 'dalla' is for absolute expression. And his saying: (ihmil... etc.) is the predicate of the subject 'kull', and the connector is omitted, and the estimation is: interpret it ... etc.
And his saying: ('ala al-lafzi) meaning: upon the Qur'an in the sense of the utterance revealed to our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, which is worshipped by its recitation, and which is challenged by the shortest sura of it. And the more correct view is that what is revealed is the utterance and the meaning. And it was said: what is revealed is (1) See p. 218. (2) See Hidaya al-Mureed 1/509.
The Divine Matters
the meaning, and Gabriel expressed it with words from himself. And it was said: what is revealed is the meaning, and the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, expressed it from himself. But the verification is the first, because Allah created it first in the Preserved Tablet, then sent it down in sheets to the heaven of the world in a place called 'the House of Might' on the Night of Decree, as He said: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' [al-Qadr: 1], then He sent it down to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, piecemeal according to events.
And his saying: (alladhi qad dalla) is a description of the utterance; and the alif in 'dalla' is for absolute expression, and what is meant is that which indicates the eternal attribute (1) by way of implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam) as has preceded.
And the conclusion is that every apparent [text] from the Book and the Sunnah that indicates the originatedness of the Qur'an is to be interpreted as referring to the recited utterance, not to the psychological speech. But it is forbidden to say 'the Qur'an is created' except in a teaching context, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on a line of the poem (line 42) that instructs the reader how to interpret scriptural evidence that appears to indicate the Qur'an is created (hadith). The author begins by clarifying the terminology: the term 'Qur'an' is applied to both the eternal, uncreated attribute of speech in God's essence (al-kalam al-nafsi) and to the recited words in human language (al-lafz al-maqru'), but most often it is used for the latter. Conversely, 'the speech of Allah' (kalam Allah) is also applied to both, but more commonly to the eternal attribute. This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.

Next, the author explains the phrase 'an al-huduthi' (from origination) in the poem: it means existence after non-existence. The divine speech is not created (makhluq); it is an attribute of God's essence. This opposes the Mu'tazila who claim it is created and not an attribute. The commentator notes that the poem uses 'huduth' instead of the more common 'khalq' either for poetic necessity or to refute the Mu'tazilite Muhammad al-Balkhi, who held that the Qur'an is 'originated' (muhdath) but not 'created', fearing that 'created' implies falsehood. The refutation is that 'originated' and 'created' are equivalent in meaning, so his attempt to avoid the term is futile — it is like fleeing rain only to stand under a downspout.

Then the line of the poem is quoted: 'So every text that indicates originatedness, interpret it as referring to the utterance that indicates [the eternal attribute].' The commentary explains that this is a response (jawaab) to the Mu'tazila's proof texts such as 'We have sent down the Remembrance' (15:9). 'Text' here means any apparent (zaahir) proof from Quran or Sunnah. The imperative 'ihmil' (interpret) applies to such texts: they must be understood as referring to the recited verbal Qur'an, not to God's eternal speech. The phrase 'alladhi qad dalla' (which indicates) describes the verbal utterance as that which points to the eternal attribute by way of implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam).

The author then discusses the nature of revelation: the more correct view is that both the meaning (ma'na) and the literal wording (lafz) were revealed from Allah; other views (that only meaning was revealed and Gabriel or the Prophet clothed it in words) are mentioned but the first is preferred because Allah first created it in the Preserved Tablet, then sent it down in sheets to the House of Might in the lowest heaven on Laylat al-Qadr, then revealed it piecemeal to the Prophet.

Finally, the conclusion is summarized: every apparent text from Quran and Sunnah that seems to point to the Qur'an's createdness must be interpreted as referring to the verbal, recited Qur'an, not to the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi). However, one is forbidden to say 'the Qur'an is created' except in a teaching context (maqam al-ta'lim), as clarified earlier.

دلالة الالتزام
اللفظ المنزل

Misconception: Some say the Qur'an's meaning is from Allah but the wording is from Gabriel or the Prophet.
Correction: The passage affirms the more correct view that both meaning and wording are revealed from Allah, based on the process of revelation from the Preserved Tablet.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how should we interpret verses like 'Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance' (15:9)?
A) They prove the Qur'an is created in its essence.
B) They refer to the verbal, recited Qur'an, not the eternal attribute.
C) They are abrogated and not applicable.
D) They apply only to the Torah and Gospel.

Key Takeaway
All scriptural evidence for createdness must be interpreted as referring to the verbal Qur'an, not the eternal attribute; both meaning and wording are from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly believe that this very utterance is from Allah, both its words and its meaning? How would your recitation change if you had full conviction that you are uttering the eternal speech of your Lord? Commit to reciting a single verse tomorrow with the awareness that you are addressing Allah and He is addressing you.
?
Have you ever felt that the Qur'an is merely a historical book influenced by its time? This lesson affirms that the Qur'an is divine in its entirety. Do you struggle with any part of the Qur'an that challenges your modern assumptions? What would it take for you to submit fully to its guidance, even if it contradicts your desires? Decide to read this lesson's source passage again and ask Allah for certainty.
?
The Mu'tazila used partial verses to argue against the eternity of the Qur'an. Are there beliefs or actions in your life where you use partial evidence from Islam to justify your own preferences rather than seeking the full understanding of the Salaf? Repent tonight and resolve to seek knowledge from reliable sources.

The Impossible Opposites of Allah's Necessary Attributes
Introduces the third category of impossible attributes as opposites of necessary ones, with methodological justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for something to be impossible for Allah and why scholars explicitly discuss these impossible attributes rather than inferring them from necessary attributes.

Core Idea
What is impossible for Allah is simply the opposite of what is necessary for Him.

Having studied the necessary and possible attributes of Allah, we now turn to the third category: the impossible. These are the opposites of the necessary attributes. For example, just as it is necessary for Allah to exist, it is impossible for Him not to exist. The poem and commentary explain this category and the methodological reason for stating it explicitly.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic poem line and its literal translation. Notice how the line directly states the impossibility of opposites.
The line uses 'يستحيل' (it is impossible) as a key term.
2
Understand the third category: impossible = opposites of necessary
The commentary explains that this is the third division in the overall scheme. Recognize that every necessary attribute has an opposite that is impossible for Allah.
Example: existence is necessary, so non-existence is impossible.
3
Grasp the methodological reason for explicit mention
The author follows the scholars in preferring matching indication (dalalat al-mutabaqah) over implicative or inclusive indication. This is because ignorance in this field is dangerous, so they spell out impossible attributes explicitly.
This shows the care taken in kalam to avoid misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: ما يستحيلُ على الله تعالى مِنْ أضدادِ الصفاتِ الواجبة]
٤٣ - وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ضِدُّ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ فِي حَقِّهِ كَالكَوْنِ فِي الجِهَاتِ
قوله : (وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ... إلخ) هذا شروعٌ في ثالث الأقسام المتقدمة في قوله : فكلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعاً وَجَبًا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبًا
لله والجائز والممتنعا
فهذا هو القسم الثالث في الإجمال السابق، وإن كان ثانياً في التفصيل، وإنما أخر الجائز في التفصيل، لطول الكلام عليه، ولا شك في علم استحالة هذا القسم من وجوب القسم الأول له تعالى، وإنما تعرَّض له المصنف على طريق القوم من عدم اكتفائهم بدلالة الالتزام ولا بدلالة التضمن بل مالوا إلى الدلالة المطابقيَّةِ لخطر الجهل في هذا الفنّ .
Secondly: what is impossible for Allah, exalted is He, from the opposites of the necessary attributes.
43 - And the opposite of the possessor of attributes is impossible in His right, like existence in directions.
His saying: (And it is impossible ... etc.) This is a beginning of the third of the divisions that preceded in his saying: 'So everyone who is legally obligated must know what is necessary for Allah, and the possible, and the impossible.' So this is the third division in the previous summary, even though it is second in detail. And he delayed the possible in detail only due to the length of discussion on it. There is no doubt in the knowledge of the impossibility of this division from the necessity of the first division for Him, exalted is He. The author only addressed it following the way of the people (the scholars) of not being satisfied with implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam) nor with inclusive indication (dalalat al-tadamun), but rather they inclined to matching indication (dalalat al-mutabaqah) due to the danger of ignorance in this art.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage marks the start of the third major section in the poem's classification of what must be known about Allah: after the necessary attributes (wajibat) and the possible attributes (ja'izat), the author now discusses the impossible attributes (mustahilat). These are the opposites (addad) of the necessary attributes. The line of the poem states: 'And the opposite of the possessor of attributes is impossible in His right, like existence in directions.' That is, just as it is necessary that Allah possesses attributes of perfection, it is impossible for Him to have their opposites—for example, it is impossible for Him to be located in a direction (like spatial confinement).

The commentary explains that this is the third division in the overall scheme. Although in the detailed exposition it is actually the second (since the possible attributes are discussed later due to their length), in the summary it is the third. The author chose to discuss the impossible attributes after the necessary ones, even though knowing the impossible is derived from knowing the necessary (by simple negation). However, the author follows the method of the scholars (al-qawm) who do not rely solely on implicative or inclusive indication; instead, they prefer direct, matching indication (dalalat al-mutabaqah) because the subject of kalam is weighty and ignorance of it is dangerous. So they spell out the impossible attributes explicitly rather than leaving them to logical inference.

Key Terms
يستحيل
ضد
الصفات الواجبة
الجائز
الممتنع
دلالة المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing the impossible attributes is merely a logical deduction from the necessary ones, so explicit mention is unnecessary.
Correction: The scholars insist on explicit matching indication (dalalat al-mutabaqah) because the subject is weighty and ignorance is dangerous; they do not rely solely on implicative or inclusive inference.

Checkpoint
Why does the author discuss the impossible attributes explicitly rather than leaving them to logical inference from the necessary attributes?
A) Because the impossible attributes are more important than the necessary ones.
B) Because the scholars prefer matching indication (dalalat al-mutabaqah) due to the danger of ignorance in this art.
C) Because the Mu'tazila denied the impossible attributes.
D) Because the poem's meter required it.

Key Takeaway
The impossible attributes of Allah are simply the opposites of His necessary attributes, and scholars state them explicitly using matching indication to avoid any misunderstanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The scholars insisted on explicit statements of impossible attributes due to the danger of ignorance. Where in your own understanding of Allah is there vagueness that could lead to error, and what will you do today to clarify it?
?
If you were to list three things that are impossible for Allah right now, could you? Why might your inability to do so be a spiritual danger, and what step will you take to change that?

Impossibility of Not Being Self-Subsisting
Definition of impossibility of not being self-subsisting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for Allah to not be self-subsisting and what this means for His absolute independence.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence means He depends on nothing and nothing depends on Him.

The author begins a series of impossibilities that protect Allah's perfection. The first is the negation of not being self-subsisting. This passage defines what it means to be qa'im bi nafsihi and why its opposite is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two conditions that would violate self-subsistence.
The passage is very concise.
2
Identify the two violations
The passage mentions two ways Allah would not be self-subsisting: being an attribute in a locus and needing a specifier. Understand each.
An attribute in a locus depends on that locus.
3
Connect to the attribute of qiyam bi nafsihi
Explain why these two negations are impossible for Allah, affirming His absolute independence.
This attribute is essential for Allah's divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيلُ عليه أيضاً ألا يكون قائماً بنفسه، بأن يكون صفةٌ يقوم بمحلّ أو يحتاج إلى مخصص، وهذا ضدُّ القيام بالنفس. وألا يكون واحداً بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته، أو يكونَ في صفاته تعدد من نوع واحدٍ كقدرتين وإرادتين وهكذا، أو يكون لأحدٍ صفةٌ كصفته تعالى، أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال، وهذا كله ضد الوحدانية. وأن يكون عاجزاً عن ممكن ما، وهذا ضدُّ القدرة. وأن يوجد شيئاً من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده أو يعدم شيئاً مع كراهته لعدمه؛ أي: عدم إرادته له أو مع الذهول أو الغفلة، فالذهول: ذهاب الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة أو من أحدهما، والأول نسيان والثاني سهو، وأما الغفلة فهي السهو، أو مع التعليل؛ بأن يكون الباري علةً تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار، ولا توقف على وجود شروط وانتفاء موانع، كحركة الخاتم، فإنَّها نشأت عند القائلين بالتعليل عن حركة
And it is also impossible for Him that He should not be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi), such that He is an attribute that subsists in a locus or needs a specifier; this is the opposite of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). And that He should not be one, such that He is composed in His essence, or has a like in His essence, or that there is multiplicity in His attributes of one kind, like two powers, two wills, and so on, or that someone has an attribute like His attribute, or that there exists with Him in existence an efficient in any action; all of this is the opposite of oneness (al-wahdaniyyah). And that He should be incapable of any possible thing; this is the opposite of power (al-qudrah). And that He should bring into existence something from the world while disliking its existence, or annihilate something while disliking its non-existence, i.e., lack of His will for it, or with absent-mindedness (al-dhuhul) or heedlessness (al-ghaflah). Al-dhuhul is the departure of something from the memory and perception or from one of them; the first is forgetfulness (nisyan) and the second is inadvertence (sahw). As for al-ghaflah, it is inadvertence (sahw). Or with ta'lil (causation), such that the Creator is a cause from which creatures originate without choice and without being dependent on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like the motion of the ring, for it originates, according to those who hold ta'lil, from the motion...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage continues the list of impossibilities for God. The author states it is impossible for God not to be self-subsisting, meaning He is not an attribute that subsists in another being nor does He need a specifier (mukhassis); this is the opposite of the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs). Next, it is impossible for Him not to be one (wahid), detailing ways that would violate oneness: composition in essence (tarkib), having an equal (mithl), multiplicity in attributes of the same kind (e.g., two powers), or anyone having an attribute like His, or another being sharing in effectiveness. All this is opposite of wahdaniyyah (oneness). Then, it is impossible for Him to be incapable of any possible thing; this is opposite of power (qudrah). Finally, it is impossible for Him to create something while disliking its existence or annihilate something while disliking its non-existence — that is, without willing it or with forgetfulness (nisyan) or inadvertence (sahw). The author defines dhuhul as departure from memory/perception, distinguishing forgetfulness and inadvertence. He also includes the impossibility of creation by ta'lil (causal emanation) without choice or conditions, giving the example of the ring's motion from the motion of the hand, but the text cuts off before completing the explanation. This last point refutes philosophical theories of emanation.

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'self-subsisting' only means not needing a physical place.
Correction: The passage clarifies it also means not needing any external cause or specifier.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two ways Allah would not be self-subsisting?
A) Being an attribute in a locus or needing a specifier
B) Being composed or having an equal
C) Being incapable or unwilling
D) Being forgetful or heedless

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-subsistence is necessary; its opposite is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that you depend entirely on the One who is truly self-subsisting?
?
When you face a problem, do you first turn to creation (like people or your own efforts) as if they are the ultimate cause, or do you remember that only Allah is independent and needs no specifier? What would change if you truly internalized that He does not need anything from you?
?
Reflect on a moment when you felt deeply in need of help. Did you realize that your neediness is a sign that you are not self-subsisting, and that only Allah is free from all need? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your dependence on Him?

Impossibility of Not Being One
Enumeration of violations of oneness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various ways the negation of oneness is impossible for Allah, including composition, equality, multiplicity, and shared efficacy.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness means He is indivisible, unique, and sole agent.

After establishing self-subsistence, the author turns to oneness (wahdaniyya). This passage enumerates several violations of oneness and affirms that all are impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage listing five violations of oneness. Notice the structure: each violation is introduced by 'أو'.
The passage uses 'ألا يكون واحداً' as the starting point.
2
List the five violations
Identify: composition in essence, having an equal, multiplicity in attributes of same kind, someone having an attribute like His, and another efficient in existence.
These cover internal and external threats to oneness.
3
Reflect on the unity of agency
The final violation (another efficient) is crucial for tawhid in actions. Understand why it is impossible.
This refutes polytheism and philosophical dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيلُ عليه أيضاً ألا يكون قائماً بنفسه، بأن يكون صفةٌ يقوم بمحلّ أو يحتاج إلى مخصص، وهذا ضدُّ القيام بالنفس. وألا يكون واحداً بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته، أو يكونَ في صفاته تعدد من نوع واحدٍ كقدرتين وإرادتين وهكذا، أو يكون لأحدٍ صفةٌ كصفته تعالى، أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال، وهذا كله ضد الوحدانية. وأن يكون عاجزاً عن ممكن ما، وهذا ضدُّ القدرة. وأن يوجد شيئاً من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده أو يعدم شيئاً مع كراهته لعدمه؛ أي: عدم إرادته له أو مع الذهول أو الغفلة، فالذهول: ذهاب الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة أو من أحدهما، والأول نسيان والثاني سهو، وأما الغفلة فهي السهو، أو مع التعليل؛ بأن يكون الباري علةً تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار، ولا توقف على وجود شروط وانتفاء موانع، كحركة الخاتم، فإنَّها نشأت عند القائلين بالتعليل عن حركة
And it is also impossible for Him that He should not be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi), such that He is an attribute that subsists in a locus or needs a specifier; this is the opposite of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). And that He should not be one, such that He is composed in His essence, or has a like in His essence, or that there is multiplicity in His attributes of one kind, like two powers, two wills, and so on, or that someone has an attribute like His attribute, or that there exists with Him in existence an efficient in any action; all of this is the opposite of oneness (al-wahdaniyyah). And that He should be incapable of any possible thing; this is the opposite of power (al-qudrah). And that He should bring into existence something from the world while disliking its existence, or annihilate something while disliking its non-existence, i.e., lack of His will for it, or with absent-mindedness (al-dhuhul) or heedlessness (al-ghaflah). Al-dhuhul is the departure of something from the memory and perception or from one of them; the first is forgetfulness (nisyan) and the second is inadvertence (sahw). As for al-ghaflah, it is inadvertence (sahw). Or with ta'lil (causation), such that the Creator is a cause from which creatures originate without choice and without being dependent on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like the motion of the ring, for it originates, according to those who hold ta'lil, from the motion...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage continues the list of impossibilities for God. The author states it is impossible for God not to be self-subsisting, meaning He is not an attribute that subsists in another being nor does He need a specifier (mukhassis); this is the opposite of the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs). Next, it is impossible for Him not to be one (wahid), detailing ways that would violate oneness: composition in essence (tarkib), having an equal (mithl), multiplicity in attributes of the same kind (e.g., two powers), or anyone having an attribute like His, or another being sharing in effectiveness. All this is opposite of wahdaniyyah (oneness). Then, it is impossible for Him to be incapable of any possible thing; this is opposite of power (qudrah). Finally, it is impossible for Him to create something while disliking its existence or annihilate something while disliking its non-existence — that is, without willing it or with forgetfulness (nisyan) or inadvertence (sahw). The author defines dhuhul as departure from memory/perception, distinguishing forgetfulness and inadvertence. He also includes the impossibility of creation by ta'lil (causal emanation) without choice or conditions, giving the example of the ring's motion from the motion of the hand, but the text cuts off before completing the explanation. This last point refutes philosophical theories of emanation.

Impossibility of Incapability
Affirmation of impossibility of incapability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that it is impossible for Allah to be incapable of any possible thing, affirming His absolute power.

Core Idea
Allah's power covers every possible thing; incapability is impossible.

The author now addresses the attribute of power (qudrah). This brief but decisive passage asserts that incapability (ajz) is impossible for Allah, because He can do everything possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the single sentence
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it is a single clause: 'And that He should be incapable of any possible thing; this is the opposite of power.'
This is the shortest impossibility in the list.
2
Understand the scope
The phrase 'any possible thing' means everything that is not intrinsically impossible (like creating a square circle). Allah's power encompasses all possibilities.
Power does not extend to impossibilities because they are not proper objects of power.
3
Relate to divine perfection
Incapability would contradict Allah's perfection as the necessarily powerful being. Reflect on why this impossibility is stated.
This statement is foundational for understanding Allah's omnipotence.

Impossibility of Creating with Dislike or Without Will
Impossibility of creation with dislike, forgetfulness, inadvertence, or causal emanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that it is impossible for Allah to create or annihilate with dislike, forgetfulness, inadvertence, or through causal emanation without will.

Core Idea
Every act of Allah is by perfect will and knowledge; He does nothing reluctantly or absent-mindedly.

The author concludes this section by addressing the relationship between Allah's will, knowledge, and action. This passage refutes several misconceptions: that Allah could create while disliking, or while being forgetful or inattentive, or that the world emanates from Him without will or conditions. It includes definitions of dhuhul and ghaflah, and introduces the refutation of ta'lil (causal emanation).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage completely
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it covers three types of impossibility: dislike, forgetfulness/inadvertence, and causal emanation.
The passage cuts off at the end; the explanation of ta'lil is incomplete.
2
Understand dislike and lack of will
Allah cannot create something while hating its existence or annihilate while hating its non-existence, because His will is always effective.
'Dislike' here means absence of will.
3
Understand dhuhul and ghaflah
The author defines dhuhul as departure from memory/perception, which includes forgetfulness (nisyan) and inadvertence (sahw). Ghaflah is defined as inadvertence (sahw). These are impossible for Allah.
These definitions show the depth of Islamic theology on divine knowledge.
4
Grasp the refutation of ta'lil
The last part refutes the theory that Allah is a mere cause from which the world emanates necessarily, without will or conditions. The example of the ring's motion from the hand is given, but incomplete.
This refutes philosophical emanationism.

Self-Subsistence and Oneness of Allah
Negates not self-subsistent and not one, defining opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-subsistence and oneness are necessary attributes of Allah and what their opposites imply.

Core Idea
Allah must be self-sufficient and uniquely one; any composition or similarity is impossible.

Sanusi begins by listing impossibilities that directly oppose two fundamental divine attributes: self-subsistence and oneness. This passage establishes the boundaries of tawḥīd by negating what cannot be said of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on self-subsistence
Read the Arabic for the first impossibility: not being self-subsistent. Notice how it defines the opposite.
Self-subsistence means not needing a substrate or a particularizer.
2
Examine the components of oneness
The passage lists five ways oneness is violated. Identify each one and reflect on its meaning.
These include composition, similarity, multiplicity, equality in attributes, and partners in agency.
3
Connect to tawḥīd
Understand that negating these impossibilities is the foundation of Islamic monotheism.
Tawḥīd is not just saying 'Allah is one' but actively denying all opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيلُ عليه أيضاً ألا يكون قائماً بنفسه، بأن يكون صفةٌ يقوم بمحلّ أو يحتاج إلى مخصص، وهذا ضدُّ القيام بالنفس. وألا يكون واحداً بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته، أو يكونَ في صفاته تعدد من نوع واحدٍ كقدرتين وإرادتين وهكذا، أو يكون لأحدٍ صفةٌ كصفته تعالى، أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال، وهذا كله ضد الوحدانية. وأن يكون عاجزاً عن ممكن ما، وهذا ضدُّ القدرة. وأن يوجد شيئاً من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده أو يعدم شيئاً مع كراهته لعدمه؛ أي: عدم إرادته له أو مع الذهول أو الغفلة، فالذهول: ذهاب الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة أو من أحدهما، والأول نسيان والثاني سهو، وأما الغفلة فهي السهو، أو مع التعليل؛ بأن يكون الباري علةً تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار، ولا توقف على وجود شروط وانتفاء موانع، كحركة الخاتم، فإنَّها نشأت عند القائلين بالتعليل عن حركة الإصبع، فعندهم حركة الإصبع علة في حركة الخاتم، ونحن نقول: الخالق لحركة الإصبع ولحركة الخاتم هو الله تعالى من غير تأثير لحركة الإصبع في حركة الخاتم، أو مع الطبع؛ بأن يكون الباري طبيعة تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار مع التوقف على وجود الشروط وانتفاء الموانع، كالنار فإنَّها تؤثر بطبعها عندهم في الإحراق مع وجود شرط المماسَّة وانتفاء مانع البلل ونحن نقول: المؤثر في الإحراق هو الله تعالى ولا تأثير للنار أصلاً، وهذا كله ضد الإرادة.
And it is also impossible for Him not to be self-subsistent, such that He is an attribute subsisting in a locus or in need of a particularizer; this is the opposite of self-subsistence. And that He not be one, such that He is composite in His essence, or has a similar in His essence, or in His attributes there is multiplicity of a single kind like two powers and two wills and so on, or someone has an attribute like His attribute, or there is with Him in existence an effective agent in any act; all of this is the opposite of uniqueness. And that He be powerless over any possible thing; this is the opposite of power. And that He bring into existence anything of the world while disliking its existence, or annihilate anything while disliking its non-existence, i.e., due to not willing it, or due to absent-mindedness or heedlessness. For absent-mindedness is the departure of something from the memory and perceptive faculty or from one of them; the first is forgetting and the second is inadvertence; as for heedlessness, it is inadvertence. Or [that He acts] by causality (ta'lil), such that the Creator is a cause from which creatures arise without choice, and without depending on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like the movement of a ring: it arises, according to the proponents of causality, from the movement of the finger. According to them, the movement of the finger is a cause of the movement of the ring. But we say: the Creator of the movement of the finger and the movement of the ring is Allah, without any influence of the finger's movement on the ring's movement. Or [that He acts] by nature (tab'), such that the Creator is a nature from which creatures arise without choice, but depending on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like fire: it affects by its nature, according to them, with burning when the condition of contact is present and the obstacle of moisture is absent. But we say: the effective agent in burning is Allah, and fire has no effect at all. All of this is the opposite of will.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a list of impossibilities regarding Allah's attributes, each contrasted with a necessary divine attribute. It first negates that Allah is not self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih), which would mean He is an attribute subsisting in a substrate or needs a specifier. Then it negates that He is not one (wāḥid), explaining that oneness excludes composition, similarity, multiplicity of attributes of the same kind (like two powers), someone having an attribute like His, or any other effective agent alongside Him. Next, it negates powerlessness over any possible thing, which is the opposite of power (qudra). Then it negates any act of creation or annihilation contrary to His will, due to dislike, absent-mindedness (dhuhūl: forgetfulness or inadvertence), or heedlessness (ghafla: inadvertence). Finally, it refutes two false doctrines: causality (ta'līl), where Allah is a necessary cause without choice, and naturalism (ṭab'), where Allah is a nature producing effects necessarily with conditions. In both cases, the author affirms that Allah is the sole effective agent, and fire and the finger have no real effect. All these negations are against the attribute of will (irāda).

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس
وحدانية
مخصص
مركب
مماثل
تعدد
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think oneness only means no partners, but it also excludes composition in essence or having a similar.
Correction: Oneness (waḥdāniyya) includes no composition, no similarity, no multiplicity of the same attribute, no equal in attributes, and no other effective agent.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the negations of oneness in this passage?
A) Composition in essence
B) Having a similar in essence
C) Multiplicity of different attributes
D) Another effective agent in any act

Key Takeaway
Allah must be self-subsistent and uniquely one; any form of composition, similarity, or partnership is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah cannot be composite, do you ever find yourself thinking of Him as if He has parts—like considering His mercy separate from His justice? How will you train your mind to see His attributes as a perfect whole without division?
?
The passage says no one can have an attribute like Allah's attribute. Think of the most beautiful attribute you admire in a human being. How does this statement challenge you to avoid attributing that quality to Allah in the same sense? What concrete step will you take today to purify your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
If Allah alone is the effective agent in every act, then where does that leave your sense of control over your own actions? Does this truth unsettle you? What would it mean for you to live each moment with the awareness that no one besides Him truly effects anything? How will you surrender that illusion of control today?

Divine Power and Will: Negating Powerlessness and Involuntary Creation
Negates powerlessness and creation against will due to dislike or inattention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's power covers all possibles and that His will is not constrained by dislike, absent-mindedness, or heedlessness.

Core Idea
Allah's power is absolute and His will is always effective, free from any external compulsion or forgetfulness.

After establishing self-subsistence and oneness, Sanusi turns to power and will. He first negates any possible powerlessness, then clarifies that creation never occurs contrary to Allah's will—whether due to dislike, absent-mindedness, or heedlessness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation of powerlessness
Read the line about power: Allah cannot be powerless over any possible thing. That would contradict qudra.
Possible means anything whose existence is not impossible.
2
Examine the will section
The passage lists ways creation could happen against will: dislike, absent-mindedness (dhuhul), and heedlessness (ghafla). Understand each.
Dhuhul includes forgetfulness and inadvertence; ghafla is inadvertently.
3
Reflect on the implications
Realize that Allah's will is always perfectly aligned with His creation—nothing occurs without His volition.
This negates the idea that Allah could create reluctantly or accidentally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيلُ عليه أيضاً ألا يكون قائماً بنفسه، بأن يكون صفةٌ يقوم بمحلّ أو يحتاج إلى مخصص، وهذا ضدُّ القيام بالنفس. وألا يكون واحداً بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته، أو يكونَ في صفاته تعدد من نوع واحدٍ كقدرتين وإرادتين وهكذا، أو يكون لأحدٍ صفةٌ كصفته تعالى، أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال، وهذا كله ضد الوحدانية. وأن يكون عاجزاً عن ممكن ما، وهذا ضدُّ القدرة. وأن يوجد شيئاً من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده أو يعدم شيئاً مع كراهته لعدمه؛ أي: عدم إرادته له أو مع الذهول أو الغفلة، فالذهول: ذهاب الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة أو من أحدهما، والأول نسيان والثاني سهو، وأما الغفلة فهي السهو، أو مع التعليل؛ بأن يكون الباري علةً تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار، ولا توقف على وجود شروط وانتفاء موانع، كحركة الخاتم، فإنَّها نشأت عند القائلين بالتعليل عن حركة الإصبع، فعندهم حركة الإصبع علة في حركة الخاتم، ونحن نقول: الخالق لحركة الإصبع ولحركة الخاتم هو الله تعالى من غير تأثير لحركة الإصبع في حركة الخاتم، أو مع الطبع؛ بأن يكون الباري طبيعة تنشأ عنه الخلائق من غير اختيار مع التوقف على وجود الشروط وانتفاء الموانع، كالنار فإنَّها تؤثر بطبعها عندهم في الإحراق مع وجود شرط المماسَّة وانتفاء مانع البلل ونحن نقول: المؤثر في الإحراق هو الله تعالى ولا تأثير للنار أصلاً، وهذا كله ضد الإرادة.
And it is also impossible for Him not to be self-subsistent, such that He is an attribute subsisting in a locus or in need of a particularizer; this is the opposite of self-subsistence. And that He not be one, such that He is composite in His essence, or has a similar in His essence, or in His attributes there is multiplicity of a single kind like two powers and two wills and so on, or someone has an attribute like His attribute, or there is with Him in existence an effective agent in any act; all of this is the opposite of uniqueness. And that He be powerless over any possible thing; this is the opposite of power. And that He bring into existence anything of the world while disliking its existence, or annihilate anything while disliking its non-existence, i.e., due to not willing it, or due to absent-mindedness or heedlessness. For absent-mindedness is the departure of something from the memory and perceptive faculty or from one of them; the first is forgetting and the second is inadvertence; as for heedlessness, it is inadvertence. Or [that He acts] by causality (ta'lil), such that the Creator is a cause from which creatures arise without choice, and without depending on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like the movement of a ring: it arises, according to the proponents of causality, from the movement of the finger. According to them, the movement of the finger is a cause of the movement of the ring. But we say: the Creator of the movement of the finger and the movement of the ring is Allah, without any influence of the finger's movement on the ring's movement. Or [that He acts] by nature (tab'), such that the Creator is a nature from which creatures arise without choice, but depending on the existence of conditions and the absence of obstacles, like fire: it affects by its nature, according to them, with burning when the condition of contact is present and the obstacle of moisture is absent. But we say: the effective agent in burning is Allah, and fire has no effect at all. All of this is the opposite of will.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a list of impossibilities regarding Allah's attributes, each contrasted with a necessary divine attribute. It first negates that Allah is not self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih), which would mean He is an attribute subsisting in a substrate or needs a specifier. Then it negates that He is not one (wāḥid), explaining that oneness excludes composition, similarity, multiplicity of attributes of the same kind (like two powers), someone having an attribute like His, or any other effective agent alongside Him. Next, it negates powerlessness over any possible thing, which is the opposite of power (qudra). Then it negates any act of creation or annihilation contrary to His will, due to dislike, absent-mindedness (dhuhūl: forgetfulness or inadvertence), or heedlessness (ghafla: inadvertence). Finally, it refutes two false doctrines: causality (ta'līl), where Allah is a necessary cause without choice, and naturalism (ṭab'), where Allah is a nature producing effects necessarily with conditions. In both cases, the author affirms that Allah is the sole effective agent, and fire and the finger have no real effect. All these negations are against the attribute of will (irāda).

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
كراهة
ذهول
غفلة
نسيان
سهو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah dislikes an event, He might still create it; but that would contradict His will.
Correction: Allah's will is always in accordance with His knowledge and wisdom; He does not create what He dislikes without a reason (but the creation itself is willed).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is 'dhuhul'?
A) Dislike
B) Heedlessness
C) Departure of something from memory and perception
D) Inadvertence only

Key Takeaway
Allah has perfect power over all possibles and His will is never subject to dislike, absent-mindedness, or heedlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you done something with a distracted heart, 'absent-minded' as the passage describes? How does knowing that Allah is free from such imperfections challenge you to be more present in your worship today?
?
Is there an area of your life where you act as if something is happening against your will (like the passage says is impossible for Allah)? How can you surrender that to Allah's perfect will?
?
If Allah never forgets or is heedless, why do you forget His favors so quickly? How can you train your heart to remember Him constantly?

Refuting Causality and Naturalism: Affirming Allah's Absolute Will
Refutes causality and naturalism as opposites of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why causal determinism and naturalism are impossible for Allah, and how they contradict His will.

Core Idea
Allah is not a blind cause or a nature; He creates with will and without intermediaries influencing.

The final part of the passage addresses two philosophical doctrines that compromise divine will: causality (ta'līl) and naturalism (ṭab'). Sanusi explains why they are impossible and reaffirms that Allah alone is the effective agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the causality argument
Read the section on ta'lil, where Allah would be a necessary cause like the finger causing the ring's movement. Note the refutation.
Causality denies choice and conditions.
2
Read the naturalism argument
Read the section on tab', where Allah would be a nature like fire causing burning. Note the condition dependency.
Naturalism also denies choice but requires conditions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Both are negated as opposites of will. Recognize that Allah's power is direct and without intermediaries.
The phrase 'all of this is the opposite of will' ties it back to irāda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Opposites of Divine Attributes
Enumeration of opposites of divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which attributes are impossible for Allah and their corresponding opposites, and why these opposites are incompatible with divine perfection.

Core Idea
Every divine perfection excludes its opposite; Allah is free from ignorance, death, muteness, blindness, and incapability.

In this passage, the author lists the specific opposites that cannot be attributed to Allah. Each opposite negates a necessary attribute of perfection. Understanding these opposites helps clarify the boundaries of divine attributes and reinforces Allah's absolute perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the concise enumeration style.
The passage lists five opposites; the last is qualified by the theory of states.
2
Identify each opposite and its counterpart
For each opposite, find the necessary attribute it negates: ignorance vs. knowledge, death vs. life, muteness vs. speech, blindness vs. sight, incapability vs. power.
3
Note the special case of incapability
The author mentions 'كونه عاجزاً' (His being incapable) as an opposite of power, but adds 'على القول بالأحوال' (according to the opinion of states). This indicates a theological nuance regarding the nature of attributes.
This point is based on the theory of al-ahwal, which is not universally accepted.
4
Reflect on divine perfection
Consider why these opposites are impossible for Allah: because each attribute of perfection is necessarily existent in Allah, so its opposite cannot exist. This affirms Allah's absolute perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهل؛ وما في معناه؛ كالظنّ والشكّ والوهم والنوم، وهذا ضد العلم. والموتُ؛ وهو ضدُّ الحياة. والبَكَمُ النفسي؛ وهو ضدُّ الكلام. والعمى؛ وهو ضدّ البصر. وكونه عاجزاً ... إلخ على القول بالأحوال.
And ignorance; and what is in its meaning: like suspicion, doubt, false estimate, and sleep; this is opposite to knowledge. And death; it is opposite to life. And psychological muteness; it is opposite to speech. And blindness; it is opposite to sight. And His being incapable... etc. according to the opinion of states (al-ahwal).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage enumerates the opposites of the necessary divine attributes. Each attribute of perfection (knowledge, life, speech, sight) has an opposite (ignorance, death, muteness, blindness) which is impossible for Allah. The author also mentions 'كونه عاجزاً' (His being incapable) as the opposite of power, but this is based on the theory of states (al-ahwal). The enumeration shows that divine perfection entails the impossibility of these defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Psychological muteness means simply not speaking.
Correction: It refers to the absence of the attribute of speech as a state, not just the act of speaking.

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely perfect and free from all opposites of His necessary attributes, such as ignorance, death, muteness, blindness, and incapability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You will one day die, but Allah is the ever-Living. How does this stark contrast reshape your priorities and your attachment to this temporary life? Will you live today more conscious of that reality?
?
You have experienced muteness, blindness, or weakness in some form. This lesson reminds you that Allah is free from all such limitations. When you feel your own inadequacies, do you turn to Allah as the One who perfectly possesses what you lack? What concrete action can you take this week to rely on His power instead of your own?
