Definition of Tawfiq and Scholarly Disagreement
Defines tawfiq and presents three scholarly interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of tawfiq and the three main scholarly interpretations regarding its meaning.

Core Idea
Tawfiq is not just creating capacity—scholars disagree on exactly what it entails.

The divine attribute al-Muwaffiq (the Granter of Success) is derived from tawfiq. This lesson explores its linguistic meaning, its technical definition, and the significant differences among key Sunni authorities on what tawfiq truly is.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the structure: definition, then disagreement, then three positions, then contrast with Mu'tazila.
The passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the linguistic definition (harmonizing) and the technical definition (creating capacity for obedience).
3
Understand the three interpretations
Locate the positions of Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ash'ari, and Abu Hanifa. Note how each differs in what 'capacity for obedience' means.
Al-Ash'ari says it is the creation of the act itself.
4
Contrast with Mu'tazila view
Read the final part about shirk according to Mu'tazila and their rejection of kasb. Understand how this differs from the Sunni view of tawfiq.
Mu'tazila equate creation and acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُوَفِّقٌ) معطوفٌ على (خالق) بحرف عطف مقدَّرٍ ، كما أشار إليه الشارح، حيث قال: (وموفق) فقدر حرف العطف، و(موفق) مأخوذ من التوفيق، وهو لغة: التأليف بين الأشياء، وشرعاً خلق قدرة الطاعة في العبد. وهل يحتاج لقولهم : وتسهيل سبيل الخير إليه أو قولهم : والداعية إليها ؛ أي: الميل النفساني إلى الطاعة، أو لا يحتاج لذلك؟ خلافٌ مبني على الخلاف في تفسير قدرة الطاعة : ففسرها إمام الحرمين بسلامة الأسباب والآلات والمراد من الأسباب الأشياء التي تكون حاملة على الفعل، والمراد من الآلات الأشياء التي يحصل بها الإعانة على الفعل، وقال الشيخ الأشعري : التوفيق هو خلق عين الطاعة، وسلامة الأسباب والآلات تصحح التكليف. قال أبو حنيفة : ( ولم يجبر أحداً من خلقه على الكفر ولا على الإيمان، والمعنى: أن الله لا يخلق الطاعة والمعصية في قلب العبد بطريق الجبر والغلبة، بل يخلقهما مقروناً باختيار العبد وحبه . انتهى . واعلم أن الإشراك عند المعتزلة : هو أن يعتقد لغير الله أثراً فوق ما وهبه الله من الأسباب الظاهرة، وأنَّ لشيء ما سلطاناً على ما خرج عن قدرة المخلوقين، فلا كسب عند المعتزلة، بل الكسب والخلق شيء واحد.
His statement: 'Al-Muwaffiq' is coordinated with 'al-Khaliq' by an implied coordinating conjunction, as the commentator indicated where he said: 'And Muwaffiq' — so he supplied the conjunction. And 'Muwaffiq' is derived from al-tawfiq, which linguistically means harmonizing between things, and technically means creating the capacity for obedience in the servant. And does it need [the addition of] 'facilitating the path of good to him' or 'the motivating factor towards it' i.e., the psychological inclination towards obedience, or does it not need that? There is a disagreement built upon the disagreement in interpreting the 'capacity for obedience': Imam al-Haramayn interpreted it as soundness of causes and instruments — the causes meaning the things that impel to the action, and the instruments meaning the things by which assistance in the action is obtained. And al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari said: al-tawfiq is the creation of the very act of obedience, and the soundness of causes and instruments validates the obligation (taklif). Abu Hanifa said: 'He did not compel anyone of His creation to disbelief nor to faith, meaning that Allah does not create obedience and disobedience in the servant's heart by way of coercion and domination, but creates them accompanied by the servant's choice and love.' End. And know that shirk according to the Mu'tazila is to believe that something other than God has an effect beyond what God has granted of apparent causes, and that something has authority over what is beyond the capacity of creatures. So there is no kasb according to the Mu'tazila; rather kasb and creation are one and the same.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute 'al-Muwaffiq' (the Granter of success) as derived from tawfiq. Linguistically, tawfiq means harmonizing; technically (shar'an), it means creating in the servant the capacity for obedience. The author then notes a disagreement: whether the definition should include additional elements like 'facilitating the path of good' or 'providing the motivating inclination'. This disagreement stems from different interpretations of 'capacity for obedience'. Three positions are given: (1) Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) interprets it as soundness of causes (factors impelling action) and instruments (means of assistance). (2) Al-Ash'ari interprets tawfiq as the creation of the very act of obedience itself, while the soundness of causes/instruments is what makes the obligation valid. (3) Abu Hanifa emphasizes that God does not compel obedience or disbelief but creates them along with the servant's choice and love. Finally, the author contrasts with the Mu'tazila, noting that their understanding of shirk includes attributing independent effect to anything other than God, and they reject the concept of kasb, equating acquisition with direct creation.

Key Terms
التوفيق
قدرة الطاعة
سلامة الأسباب والآلات
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the definition of tawfiq as 'creating capacity for obedience' is universally agreed upon among Sunni scholars.
Correction: In fact, there is significant disagreement: al-Juwayni interprets it as soundness of causes/instruments, al-Ash'ari as creation of the act itself, and Abu Hanifa emphasizes non-compulsion with choice.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is al-tawfiq?
A) The creation of the capacity for obedience.
B) The soundness of causes and instruments.
C) The creation of the very act of obedience.
D) Not compelling the servant to obedience.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of tawfiq is debated among major Sunni scholars, with interpretations ranging from creating capacity to creating the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish an act of obedience, do you truly believe that the capacity came from Allah alone, or do you silently credit your own resolve? Read the definition of tawfiq as “creating capacity”—does your heart acknowledge that without His tawfiq, you would have no ability at all? Commit to starting each act of worship by saying “لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله” as a confession of your absolute dependence.
?
Abu Hanifa emphasized that Allah creates obedience accompanied by your choice and love. Reflect on your last sin: Did you blame it on your circumstances or on Allah's decree? According to this lesson, Allah does not compel you to sin nor to faith. How does this truth challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices today?
?
The Mu'tazila considered ascribing any effect to something other than Allah as shirk. In your daily life, do you ever attribute outcomes—even small ones—to your own skill, luck, or the actions of others, forgetting that they are merely causes granted by Allah? Confess one area where you need to repent of this subtle shirk and realign your tawhid.

Ash'ari Proofs for God Creating Human Actions
Enumerates three Ash'ari proofs for divine creation of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs the Ash'aris use to argue that God directly creates human actions, including rational and textual arguments.

Core Idea
Human actions are possible things; every possible thing is created by God alone.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents three concise proofs — two rational and one scriptural — that establish the Ash'ari position on human action. These proofs respond to those who claim that humans independently create their own deeds. By grounding the argument in both reason and revelation, the Ash'ari school demonstrates that all actions, without exception, are directly created by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the enumeration of three distinct proofs.
The proofs are numbered 1, 2, and 3 in the Arabic.
2
Understand the first proof: possibility
The first proof states that the servant's action is possible (mumkin), and every possible thing depends on absolute power. Reflect on why a possible thing cannot exist by itself.
This is a rational proof grounded in contingency.
3
Understand the second proof: divine knowledge
The second proof notes that the servant is ignorant of the details of his action, while the creator must know all details. Ask yourself: Who has perfect knowledge of every action?
This proof connects omniscience with creation.
4
Reflect on the textual proofs
The third proof cites two Qur'anic verses. Read them and consider how they directly attribute creation to God.
Verse 37:96 states 'Allah created you and what you do.'
5
Synthesize the three proofs
Consider how the rational and textual proofs together refute the idea that humans create their own actions. Why does the Ash'ari school insist on direct divine creation?
These proofs are standard in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أدلة الأشاعرة في خلق أفعال العباد الله تعالى دون واسطة : ١ . فعلُ العبد ممكن، وكل ممكن يستند إلى القدرة المطلقة ٢. جهل العبد بتفاصيل فعله، والقاعدة تقول : خالقُ الشيء عالم به . .٣ الأدلة النقلية : قال تعالى: ﴿وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصَّافات : ٩٦] ، قال تعالى : ومَل مِنْ خَلِقٍ غير الله [فاطر: ٣].
And among the proofs of the Ash'aris for [the fact that] the actions of servants are created by God, the Exalted, without intermediary: 1. The servant's action is possible (mumkin), and every possible thing depends on absolute power. 2. The servant's ignorance of the details of his action, and the rule is: The creator of a thing is knowledgeable of it. 3. Textual proofs: He said, the Exalted: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Qur'an 37:96). He said, the Exalted: 'Is there any creator other than Allah?' (Qur'an 35:3).
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage enumerates three proofs used by the Ash'aris to argue that human actions are directly created by God without any intermediary. The first is a rational proof: a human action is a possible entity (mumkin), and every possible entity must be brought into existence by the absolute power of God (al-qudra al-mutlaqa), since contingent things cannot cause themselves. The second proof: a human is ignorant of the full details of his own action (e.g., the precise neurological and physiological processes), whereas the creator of a thing must have complete knowledge of it; therefore, since God knows all details, He is the true creator. The third proof is textual: Qur'an 37:96 explicitly states that God created both humans and what they do, and Qur'an 35:3 rhetorically asks if there is any creator besides God, implying that all actions are created by Him alone. These proofs are presented succinctly, without elaboration, as standard Ash'ari arguments against those who attribute creative power to humans.

Key Terms
فعل العبد ممكن
القدرة المطلقة
جهل العبد بتفاصيل فعله
الخلق والإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proofs rely solely on transmitted texts without rational arguments.
Correction: This passage shows that the Ash'aris use both rational proofs (possibility and divine knowledge) and textual proofs from the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a rational proof mentioned for God creating human actions?
A) The servant's action is eternal.
B) The servant's action is possible and depends on absolute power.
C) The servant's action is obligatory.
D) The servant's action is identical to God's action.

Key Takeaway
The three proofs—rational (possibility and knowledge) and textual—demonstrate that God directly creates human actions without any human creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that Allah created that action? How does that affect your feeling of guilt and your turning to Him in repentance? Will you now, in your next moment of weakness, pause and say 'Allah is the Creator of this action; I am only the locus of acquisition'?
?
Are you content with knowing only the surface of your actions while Allah knows every detail? How does that humble you when you think you have control? Take a moment to ask Allah to grant you sincerity in all you do, knowing that He alone creates and knows.
?
When you witness injustice, do you rush to accuse Allah of creating evil? Or do you trust His wisdom? Write a resolve to submit to His decree in hardship starting today.

Three Proofs of the Ash'aris for the Creation of Actions
Enumeration of three proofs for divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs the Ash'aris use to argue that God directly creates human actions, including the contingency argument, the ignorance argument, and the scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
God creates every action; our ignorance and contingency prove it.

The Ash'ari school holds that every human action is directly created by God without intermediaries. To support this, they offer two rational proofs and one scriptural proof. In this lesson, we will examine each proof as presented in a concise footnote from a classical commentary, and see how they together reinforce the doctrine of divine omnipotence while preserving human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three numbered proofs: two rational and one textual. Try to summarize each in your own words before reading the commentary.
The proofs are listed in order: 1) contingency, 2) ignorance, 3) Quranic verses.
2
Understand the first rational proof (contingency)
Focus on the first proof: human actions are contingent (possible in themselves), and every contingent thing requires a sufficient cause. God's absolute power is that cause. This establishes that the action cannot be self-caused or caused by the servant alone.
Contingency (imkān) is a key concept in kalam: whatever is not necessary in itself needs a cause.
3
Understand the second rational proof (ignorance)
The second proof argues that the servant is ignorant of many details of his own action (e.g., neural processes). But the creator of a thing must know it fully. Since the servant lacks full knowledge, he cannot be the creator; therefore God is the creator.
This proof relies on the principle: 'The creator of something knows it completely'.
4
Understand the textual proof (Quranic verses)
The third proof cites two Quranic verses: 'And God created you and what you do' (37:96) and 'And you have no creator other than God' (35:3). These verses directly state that God creates human actions.
These verses are often used in debates on free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أدلة الأشاعرة في خلق أفعال العباد الله تعالى دون واسطة : ١ . فعلُ العبد ممكن، وكل ممكن يستند إلى القدرة المطلقة ٢. جهل العبد بتفاصيل فعله، والقاعدة تقول : خالقُ الشيء عالم به . ٣. الأدلة النقلية : قال تعالى: ﴿وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصَّافات : ٩٦] ، قال تعالى : ﴿وَمَا لَكُمْ مِنْ خَالِقٍ غَيْرُ اللَّهِ﴾ [فاطر: ٣].
And among the proofs of the Ashʿaris for the creation of the actions of servants by God Most High without intermediary: 1. The action of the servant is contingent (mumkin), and every contingent is dependent on absolute power. 2. The servant's ignorance of the details of his action, and the rule states: the creator of a thing is knowledgeable of it. 3. Scriptural evidence: He said Most High: {And God created you and what you do} [al-Ṣāffāt: 96]; He said Most High: {And you have no creator other than God} [Fāṭir: 3].
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of three proofs, likely from a footnote, supporting the Ashʿari position that God creates human actions directly without any intermediary (like secondary causation). The first proof is rational: the servant's action is contingent (mumkin), meaning it is possible in itself, and every contingent requires a cause; the only sufficient cause is God's absolute power. The second proof is also rational: the servant is ignorant of many details of his own action (e.g., the exact neural processes), yet the creator must know all details of what He creates; since the servant does not have full knowledge, the servant cannot be the creator; therefore God is the creator. The third proof is textual: two Quranic verses are cited. The first, from Sūrat al-Ṣāffāt 37:96, states that God created both the people and their actions. The second, from Sūrat Fāṭir 35:3, implies that there is no creator besides God. These proofs together are meant to establish that human actions are directly created by God, a key Ashʿari doctrine in the debate on free will and predestination.

Key Terms
فعل العبد ممكن
القدرة المطلقة
جهل العبد
خالق الشيء عالم به
الأدلة النقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs deny human agency and imply fatalism, making humans mere puppets.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that while God creates the action, humans 'acquire' (kasb) the act, meaning they intend and perform it, thus bearing responsibility. The proofs argue that the act's existence depends on God's power, not that humans have no role.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three proofs mentioned for the Ash'ari position on the creation of actions?
A) The action of the servant is contingent and needs absolute power.
B) The servant is ignorant of the details of his action, but the creator is all-knowing.
C) The Quran says 'God created you and what you do'.
D) The servant feels his action is free, therefore he is the creator.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris use two rational proofs (contingency and ignorance) and one scriptural proof to show that God directly creates all human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God creates every detail of your actions – even the ones you regret – how does that change the way you view your mistakes? Will you turn to Him for help in changing, or will you remain trapped in self-blame?
?
You are often ignorant of the full reality of your own motives and the consequences of your actions. Does that realization humble you enough to seek God's guidance in every decision, even the small ones?
?
The Ash'aris say you 'acquire' what God creates. Does that distinction give you comfort that you are still responsible, or does it make you question whether you truly have any control? How will you live differently knowing that your actions are both created by God and yours to own?

Divine Guidance and Forsaking: Tawfiq and Khidhlan
Explains divine guidance as tawfiq and forsaking as khidhlan with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of tawfiq (divine guidance) and khidhlan (forsaking) as acts of Allah, and how they relate to His will.

Core Idea
Tawfiq and khidhlan are both created by Allah according to His will.

This lesson unpacks two key divine attributes mentioned in the Jawharah: tawfiq (granting success) and khidhlan (forsaking). The author explains that both are acts of Allah tied to His will, not mere metaphors. Understanding these terms is essential for grasping the Islamic view of divine sovereignty over human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text from the poem and its commentary once, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first tawfiq, then khidhlan.
2
Understand the grammatical analysis of tawfiq
Focus on how the commentary explains the phrase 'li-man arada an yasil'. Note the grammatical roles and what the pronouns refer to.
The phrase means God grants success to the person He desires to bring near.
3
Learn the definition of khidhlan
Read the linguistic and legal definitions of khidhlan. Note the two views on tawfiq that affect the definition of khidhlan.
Legally, it is the creation of disobedience or the power for it.
4
Compare tawfiq and khidhlan
Observe how both are explained as acts of creation by Allah, each tied to His will. Note the parallel structure in the poem lines.
Both are opposed: tawfiq brings near, khidhlan keeps distant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لِمَنْ أَرَادَ أَنْ يَصِلْ) أي : للذي أراد وصوله لرضاه ومحبته، فـ (أن) والفعل في تأويل مصدر مفعول (أراد)، والجار والمجرور متعلق بـ (موفق)، وضمير (أراد) عائد على الله تعالى، وضمير يصل عائد على (من)، فالمعنى: أن الله موفق للشخص الذي أراد الله أن يصل؛ لرضاه عنه ومحبته له.
٤٦ - وَخَاذِلٌ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ بُعْدَهُ وَمُنْجِزْ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ وَعْدَهُ
وقوله: (وخَاذِلٌ) من الخِذْلان، ومعناه لغةً: ترك النُّصْرة والإعانة، وشرعاً: خَلْقُ المعصية في العبد والداعية إليها، أو خَلْقُ قدرة المعصية على الرأيين في التوفيق. وقوله: (لِمَنْ أَرَادَ بُعْدَهُ)؛ أي: للذي أراد بُعْدَهُ عن رضاه ومحبته، كما تقدَّم في نظيره.
His saying: 'For whom He desired that he should arrive' means: for the one He desired his arrival to His pleasure and love. So 'an' and the verb are in the interpretation of a verbal noun as the object of 'arada', and the prepositional phrase is attached to 'muwaffiq', and the pronoun of 'arada' refers back to God Most High, and the pronoun of 'yasal' refers back to 'man'. So the meaning is: God is granting success to the person whom God desired that he should arrive, out of His pleasure with him and His love for him. 46. 'And a forsaker for whom He desired his distance, and a fulfiller for whom He desired His promise.' And His saying: 'and a forsaker' is from khidhlān, and its meaning linguistically is: leaving off support and assistance, and in sacred law: the creation of disobedience in the servant and the call to it, or the creation of the power of disobedience according to the two views on tawfīq. And His saying: 'for whom He desired his distance' means: for the one He desired his distance from His pleasure and love, as was mentioned previously in its parallel.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage defines two key divine acts: tawfiq (success-granting) and khidhlan (forsaking). It begins by explaining the phrase 'li-man arada an yasil' from the poem: it refers to the person God wants to bring near to His pleasure and love. The grammatical analysis clarifies that 'yasil' is the object of 'arada', and the preposition 'li' relates to 'muwaffiq'. The meaning is that God grants success to that person. Then it turns to the line 'wa-khadhilun li-man arada bu'dahu' – 'and a forsaker for whom He desired distance.' It defines khidhlān first linguistically as abandoning support, then in sacred law as creating disobedience and the inclination toward it in the servant, or creating the ability to commit disobedience, depending on the two views of tawfiq. The 'distance' in the line is explained as distance from God's pleasure and love, mirroring the earlier explanation for guidance. The passage thus establishes that both guidance and forsaking are created acts of God, tied to His will.

Key Terms
توفيق
خذلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khidhlan means God actively abandons the servant without any action from Him.
Correction: Khidhlan is the creation of disobedience or the power for it in the servant, not mere abandonment. It is an act of creation tied to God's will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of khidhlan in the context of divine actions?
A) God leaving the servant without help.
B) Creating sin and the inclination towards it in the servant.
C) Withholding reward from the disobedient.
D) Preventing the servant from doing good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq and khidhlan are both created by Allah according to His will: tawfiq brings the servant near through obedience, khidhlan distances the servant through disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If your success in obedience is entirely due to Allah's tawfiq, created in you according to His will, what does that imply about your pride in your good deeds? How can you cultivate true humility today?
?
The lesson states that both tawfiq and khidhlan are from Allah's will. Are you more afraid of falling into disobedience (khidhlan) or more hopeful of being guided (tawfiq)? What concrete supplication will you make tonight to beseech Allah for tawfiq and protection from khidhlan?

God's Promise and Threat in Ash'ari Theology
States that God's promise is unbreakable while threat may be broken due to divine generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between God's promise (which must be fulfilled) and His threat (which may be waived out of generosity), and the rational reasoning behind this stance.

Core Idea
God's promise is unbreakable, but His threat may be broken as an act of generosity.

This lesson examines a key Ash'ari doctrine concerning God's promise and threat. The passage explains that God's promise of Paradise to believers is absolutely binding in sacred law, while His threat of punishment may be withheld due to His mercy and generosity. We will explore the reasoning based on Quranic verses and the logical distinction between breaking a promise (a defect) and breaking a threat (an act of grace).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the full meaning.
Pay attention to the poetic lines at the end, which illustrate the concept.
2
Identify key terms: wa'd, wa'id, khalaf
These three terms are central. 'Wa'd' means promise, 'wa'id' means threat, and 'khalaf' means breaking or failing to fulfill.
Understand that 'khalaf' is used differently for promise and threat.
3
Understand the promise argument
The passage argues that God's promise to believers of Paradise cannot fail, citing Quranic verses (30:6, 3:9). If it failed, that would imply lying and foolishness in God, which is impossible.
The argument uses a conditional proof: if the promised reward were withheld, it would entail defect.
4
Understand the threat argument
Unlike the promise, breaking a threat is permissible. The reason is that it is not a defect but an act of generosity. The passage quotes a poet to show that breaking a threat can be praiseworthy.
Note that this is specifically the Ash'ari view; the Maturidis are mentioned as agreeing on the promise part.
5
Contrast promise and threat
Reflect on why breaking a promise is a defect while breaking a threat is not. The key is that a promise creates an obligation, while a threat does not in the same way.
This distinction preserves both God's truthfulness and His mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمُنْجِزُ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ وَعْدَهُ) أي : ومُعْط للذي أراد به خيراً ما وعده به على لسان نبيه أو في كتابه فمفعول ((أراد) محذوف، و(وعده) مفعولُ (منجز)، والمراد به الموعود به، وأشار المصنف بذلك إلى أن وعد الله المؤمنين الجنة لا يتخلف شرعاً قطعاً؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ اللَّهِ لَا يُخْلِفُ اللهُ وَعْدَهُ [الروم: ٦]، وإن اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٩]؛ أي: الوعد كما قاله بعض المفسرين، فلو تخلَّفَ إعطاء الموعود به لزم الكذب والسفه والخُلْفُ، واللازم باطل فكذا الملزوم، فالخُلْفُ في الوعد نقص يجب تنزيه الله عنه، وهذا متفق عليه عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية. وأمَّا الوعيد فيجوز الخُلْف فيه عند الأشاعرة؛ لأن الخُلْفَ فيه لا يُعدُّ نقصاً، يُعد كرماً يُمتدح به، كما يشير له قول الشاعر: [من الطويل]
بل
سقمي في الحب عافيتي ووجودي من الهوى عدمي
وإني وإن أوعدته أو وعدته لمخلف إيعادي ومنجز موعدي
His saying: 'And a fulfiller for whom He desired His promise' means: a giver to the one He desired good for, what He promised him by the tongue of His prophet or in His book. So the object of 'arada' is omitted, and 'wa'dahu' is the object of 'munjiz', and what is meant is the promised thing. The author indicated by this that the promise of God to the believers of Paradise does not fail definitively in sacred law, due to His saying: 'The promise of God – God does not break His promise' [30:6], and 'Indeed God does not break the appointment' [3:9]; i.e., the promise as some commentators said. For if giving the promised thing failed, lying, foolishness, and breaking would follow, and the necessary is false, so likewise the necessitator. So breaking in the promise is a deficiency that God must be exonerated from, and this is agreed upon among the Ash'aris and Maturidis. As for the threat, breaking it is permissible among the Ash'aris because breaking in it is not considered a deficiency; it is considered generosity that is praised, as the poet's saying indicates: [from at-tawil] 'Nay, my illness in love is my health and my existence from passion is my non-existence; and I, even if I threaten him or promise him, am breaking my threat and fulfilling my promise.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage explains the line 'wa-munjiz li-man arada wa'dahu' from the poem. It first interprets 'munjiz' as a giver, and clarifies that the verb 'arada' has an implied object, with 'wa'dahu' as the direct object of 'munjiz'. The author then states that this line indicates that God's promise of Paradise to believers absolutely never fails in sacred law, citing Quranic verses (30:6 and 3:9) that God does not break His promise. The reasoning: if the promised thing were not given, it would entail lying, foolishness, and breach – all impossible for God. Hence, breaking a promise is a defect from which God must be exonerated. This is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. However, regarding the threat (wa'id), the Ash'ari position is that breaking it is permissible because it is not considered a defect but an act of generosity worthy of praise. The passage then quotes two poetic lines: 'My illness in love is my health... and I, even if I threaten him or promise him, am breaking my threat and fulfilling my promise.' This poetry illustrates the point that breaking a threat can be seen as noble, whereas breaking a promise is not.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Breaking a threat implies that God lied or is untruthful.
Correction: The passage clarifies that breaking a threat is not considered a defect; rather, it is an act of generosity and thus does not entail lying or untruthfulness.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view expressed, why is breaking the threat not considered a defect?
A) Because the threat is conditional on the servant's sin.
B) Because God cannot break His word.
C) Because it is seen as an act of generosity.
D) Because the threat is not a promise.

Key Takeaway
God's promise must be fulfilled; His threat may be waived as generosity—this distinction is central to Ash'ari theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God's threat of punishment is not necessarily binding and may be waived out of His generosity. Does this make you more hopeful and grateful, or does it tempt you to take sin lightly? Examine your heart: are you using God's mercy as a license to disobey, or as a reason to repent more sincerely?
?
If you were certain that God would never break His promise of Paradise to believers, would that certainty change how you live today? What specific actions would you do differently if you truly believed that His promise is absolutely unbreakable?
?
When you sin, do you expect God's mercy to override His threat as a matter of entitlement, or do you stand in awe of His generosity? This lesson teaches that breaking the threat is a favor, not a right. How does this distinction call you to humility and gratitude?

The First Objection: Lying in God's Threat and Its Answer
First objection and answer regarding lying in divine threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection of lying in God's threat hinges on implicit conditionality in generous speech.

Core Idea
A generous threat is implicitly conditional; a promise is not.

The first objection against allowing God to break His threat is that it would entail lying. This lesson presents the objection and the Ash'ari answer, which distinguishes between how threats and promises are understood from a generous person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation.
The passage is concise; note the two parts.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Locate the statement of the objection and the beginning of the answer. Notice the key term 'kadhib' (lying).
3
Understand the distinction between threat and promise
The answer centers on the difference: threats are implicitly conditional for a generous person, promises are decisive. Reflect on why this matters.
This is the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اعترض جواز تخلف الوعيد بلزوم مفاسد كثيرة:
منها الكذب في خبره تعالى وقد قام الإجماع على تنزه خبره تعالى عن الكذب . ومنها تبدلُ القول، وقد قال تعالى : مَا يُبَدِّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى [ق: ٢٩] . ومنها تجويز عدم خلود الكفار في النار، وهو خلاف ما قامت عليه الأدلة القطعية من خلودهم فيها .
وأجيب عن الأول : بأن الكريم إذا أخبر بالوعيد فاللائق بكرمه أن يبني إخباره به على المشيئة، وإن لم يصرح بها ، فإذا قال الكريم : لأعذبن زيداً مثلاً، فنيّته إن شئت، بخلاف الوعد ؛ فإن اللائق بكرمه أن يبني إخباره به على الجزم.
وعن الثاني : بأنَّ الممنوع إنَّما هو تبدلُ القول في وعيد الكفار أو من لم يرد الله عفواً، فالآية أعني قوله : مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى ﴾ [ق: ٢٩] محمولةٌ على ذلك.
وعن الثالث : بأنَّ جواز تخلف الوعيد فيما إذا كان وارداً فيما يجوز العفو عنه، فلا ينافي خلود الكفار في النار ؛ فإنَّه لا يجوز العفو عن الكفر، قال الله تعالى : إنَّ الله لا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ [النساء: ٤٨]، وهذه الآية مقيدة لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَميعاً [الزمر: ٥٣].
And it was objected against the permissibility of breaking the threat by entailing many corruptions: among them, lying in His report, and the consensus has been established on the exoneration of His report from lying. And among them, the change of speech, and He said: 'No change is there in the word from Me' [Q. 50:29]. And among them, permitting the non-eternity of disbelievers in the Fire, which contradicts what the decisive proofs have established of their eternity therein. It was answered concerning the first: that the generous one, when he informs of a threat, fitting for his generosity is that he bases his informing of it on will, even if he does not explicitly state it. So if the generous one says: 'I will punish Zayd,' for example, his intention is 'if I will,' unlike the promise; for the fitting thing for his generosity is that he bases his informing of it on decisiveness. Concerning the second: that what is forbidden is only the change of speech regarding the threat against disbelievers or those for whom God has not willed pardon. So the verse, namely His saying: 'No change is there in the word from Me' [50:29], is restricted to that. Concerning the third: that the permissibility of breaking the threat applies when it is issued regarding what is permissible to pardon, so it does not contradict the eternity of disbelievers in the Fire, for pardon of disbelief is not permissible. God said: 'Indeed, God does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [4:48], and this verse restricts His saying: 'Indeed, God forgives all sins' [39:53].
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised against the Ash'ari view that God may break His threat, along with their answers. The first objection is that breaking a threat would entail lying in God's report, which is impossible by consensus. The answer: when a generous person makes a threat, it is fitting for his generosity that his statement is implicitly conditional on his will, even if not explicitly stated. For example, saying 'I will punish Zayd' is understood as 'if I will'; this differs from a promise, which is stated with decisiveness. The second objection is that it would involve a change of speech, contradicted by Q. 50:29. The answer: that verse is restricted to threats against disbelievers or those God does not pardon; it does not forbid all changes. The third objection is that allowing broken threats could lead to non-eternal punishment for disbelievers, contradicting definitive proofs. The answer: permissibility applies to cases where pardon is allowed; disbelief is not pardonable, so the eternity of disbelievers remains firm. The passage cites Q. 4:48 (God does not forgive shirk) and Q. 39:53 (God forgives all sins) to show that the general verse is restricted by the specific one. Thus, the answers defend the Ash'ari position without undermining divine justice or truthfulness.

Key Terms
كذب
مشيئة
وعد
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God threatens punishment and does not carry it out, then God lied.
Correction: The threat is implicitly conditional, as is fitting for a generous person, so not carrying it out is not a lie.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how should a generous person's threat be understood?
A) As absolute and definite
B) As conditional on will
C) As a promise
D) As a joke

Key Takeaway
A threat from a generous being is implicitly conditional, so non-fulfillment is not lying.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you despair of God's mercy because you think His threat of punishment is unconditional? How does this lesson's distinction between threat and promise reshape your hope in repentance?
?
Are there promises you have made to Allah that you treat lightly, assuming they are conditional like a threat? What specific promise do you need to reaffirm today?
?
If you were a generous person, how would your threats differ from your promises? How can you apply the lesson's principle to your own speech and character?

The Second Objection: Change of Speech and Its Answer
Second objection and answer regarding change of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection based on Q. 50:29 ('No change in the word') is restricted to threats against disbelievers and those not pardoned.

Core Idea
Q. 50:29 forbids change only in specific threats, not all.

The second objection claims that allowing God to break His threat would involve a change of speech, contradicted by Q. 50:29. This lesson explains how the answer limits the verse's application.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the verse (50:29) cited as evidence.
The verse is 'Ma yubaddalu al-qawl'.
2
Understand the answer's restriction
The answer says the verse applies only to threats against disbelievers or those God does not pardon. See how this limits the objection.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the answer restricts the verse. This protects God's truthfulness while allowing for mercy.
Think about the context of the verse in Surah Qaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

منها الكذب في خبره تعالى وقد قام الإجماع على تنزه خبره تعالى عن الكذب . ومنها تبدلُ القول، وقد قال تعالى : مَا يُبَدِّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى [ق: ٢٩] . ومنها تجويز عدم خلود الكفار في النار، وهو خلاف ما قامت عليه الأدلة القطعية من خلودهم فيها .

وعن الثاني : بأنَّ الممنوع إنَّما هو تبدلُ القول في وعيد الكفار أو من لم يرد الله عفواً، فالآية أعني قوله : مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى ﴾ [ق: ٢٩] محمولةٌ على ذلك.

And among them, the change of speech, and He said: 'No change is there in the word from Me' [Q. 50:29]. And among them, permitting the non-eternity of disbelievers in the Fire, which contradicts what the decisive proofs have established of their eternity therein. ?

?

?
Think of a specific sin you have committed and secretly hoped God would 'change His word' to excuse you. How does this lesson's teaching that God's threats are real but conditional affect your hope for forgiveness? Will you now repent sincerely, or continue assuming leniency without change?

The Third Objection: Non-Eternity of Disbelievers and Its Answer
Third objection and answer regarding eternity of disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the permissibility of breaking threats does not contradict the eternity of disbelievers in Hell, because disbelief is not pardonable.

Core Idea
Pardon is not possible for disbelief, so disbelievers' eternity remains firm.

The third objection worries that if God can break threats, then disbelievers might not be eternally punished. This lesson shows how the answer restricts the permissibility to cases where pardon is allowed, and disbelief is not such a case.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about disbelievers' eternity
Read the Arabic and translation. The objection claims that allowing broken threats implies non-eternity for disbelievers.
2
Study the answer using Q. 4:48
The answer says permissibility only applies where pardon is possible. It cites Q. 4:48 which says God does not forgive shirk. This shows that disbelief is not pardonable.
Note how Q. 4:48 restricts Q. 39:53.
3
See how the two verses harmonize
Q. 39:53 says God forgives all sins. Q. 4:48 excludes shirk. Together, they show that forgiveness is for sins less than shirk, preserving the eternity of disbelievers.
This is a key principle in understanding divine promises and threats.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objections to Threat Divergence and Their Answers
Al-Bijuri lists objections and gives three Ash'ari answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections raised against the permissibility of God not fulfilling His threat and how they are answered within the Ash'ari framework.

Core Idea
Divine threats are conditional on will; promises are unconditional.

The permissibility of threat divergence (takhalluf al-wa'id) was contested by some theologians who raised logical and scriptural objections. Al-Bijuri presents these objections and offers Ash'ari responses that preserve God's truthfulness while affirming His generosity. This lesson walks through the objections and their answers, grounding them in transmitted evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and list the three harms that objectors claim follow from threat divergence: lying in God's report, change of statement, and allowing non-eternal punishment of disbelievers.
Each objection is introduced by 'ومنها'.
2
Study the first answer
Notice how the answer distinguishes between threat and promise based on divine generosity: threats are conditional on will, promises are certain. The hadith of the Prophet reinforces this.
The key term is 'الكرم' – generosity.
3
Analyze the second answer
See how the Quranic verse about no change of statement is restricted to threats against disbelievers or the unforgiven, not threats in general.
The principle of specification (takhsis) by context.
4
Examine the third answer
Understand that threat divergence only applies where forgiveness is possible (e.g., sins less than shirk). The eternity of disbelievers remains firm.
Verse 4:48 restricts 39:53.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنِّي وَإِنْ أَوْعَدْتُهُ أَو وَعَدْتُهُ لَمُخْلِفُ إِيعَادِي وَمُنْجِزُ مَوْعِدِي وقد اعترض جواز تخلف الوعيد بلزوم مفاسد كثيرة: منها الكذب في خبره تعالى وقد قام الإجماع على تنزه خبره تعالى عن الكذب . ومنها تبدلُ القول، وقد قال تعالى : مَا يُبَدِّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى [ق: ٢٩] . ومنها تجويز عدم خلود الكفار في النار، وهو خلاف ما قامت عليه الأدلة القطعية من خلودهم فيها . وأجيب عن الأول : بأن الكريم إذا أخبر بالوعيد فاللائق بكرمه أن يبني إخباره به على المشيئة، وإن لم يصرح بها ، فإذا قال الكريم : لأعذبن زيداً مثلاً، فنيّته إن شئت، بخلاف الوعد ؛ فإن اللائق بكرمه أن يبني إخباره به على الجزم. قال : «مَنْ وعده الله على عمل ثواباً فهو مُنجز له، ومَنْ أوعده على عمل فهو بالخيار إن شاء عذبه، وإن شاء غفر له (۱) وعن الثاني : بأنَّ الممنوع إنَّما هو تبدلُ القول في وعيد الكفار أو من لم يرد الله عفواً، فالآية أعني قوله : مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَيَّ ﴾ [ق: ٢٩] محمولةٌ على ذلك. وعن الثالث : بأنَّ جواز تخلف الوعيد فيما إذا كان وارداً فيما يجوز العفو عنه، فلا ينافي خلود الكفار في النار ؛ فإنَّه لا يجوز العفو عن الكفر، قال الله تعالى : إنَّ الله لا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ النِّسَاء: ٤٤٨، وهذه الآية مقيدة لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَميعاً (الزمر: ٢٥٣ . وذهبت الماتريدية إلى أنه يمتنع تخلُّفُ الوعيد كما يمتنع تخلُّفُ الوعد، ولا يرد على ذلك أن الوعيد يتخلَّفُ في المؤمن المغفور له ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة بعموم الوعيد مُخْرَجٌ منها المؤمنُ المغفور له، وأما غير المغفور له .. فلا بد من نفوذ الوعيد فيه؛ فقولهم : لا بدَّ من إنفاذ الوعيد فيه ولو في واحد الآتي في قوله : وواجب تعذيب بعض ارتكب كبيرة .... إلى آخره . إنَّما يظهر على كلام الماتريدية، وينبني على الخلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية أنَّه يصح على قول الأشاعرة أن تقول: اللهم اغفر لجميع المؤمنين جميعَ ذنوبهم، ولا يصح ذلك على كلام الماتريدية، فظهر أن الخلاف حقيقي، وإن جعله بعضهم لفظيا، فتدبر. (۱) رواه البزار كما في كشف الأستار (۳۲۳۵)، وأبو يعلى (٣٣١٦) ، والطبراني في «الأوسط» كما في مجمع الزوائده (۲۱۱/۱۰).
And when I threaten him or promise him, I am one who fails to fulfill my threat and fulfills my promise. The permissibility of threat divergence has been objected to on the basis of many harms: Among them is lying in His report, and consensus has been established that His report is free from lying. And among them is change of statement, and He said: 'There is no change of statement with Me.' And among them is permitting that the disbelievers not be eternally in the Fire, contrary to the definitive evidence of their eternity therein. And it was answered regarding the first: that when the Generous One reports a threat, the fitting thing according to His generosity is that He builds His report upon the will, even if He does not explicitly state it. So when the Generous One says, 'I will punish Zayd, for example,' His intention is 'if I will,' contrary to the promise; for the fitting thing according to His generosity is that He builds His report upon certainty. He said: 'Whoever God promises a reward for an action, He fulfills it; and whoever He threatens for an action, He has the choice: if He wills, He punishes him, and if He wills, He forgives him.' (1) And regarding the second: that what is prohibited is only the change of statement in the threat of disbelievers or those for whom God has not willed forgiveness. So the verse, meaning His statement: 'There is no change of statement with Me' is interpreted as applying to that. And regarding the third: that the permissibility of threat divergence is when it occurs concerning that for which forgiveness is permissible. So it does not negate the eternity of disbelievers in the Fire; for forgiveness of disbelief is not permissible. God said: 'Indeed, God does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' This verse is a restriction of His statement: 'Indeed, God forgives all sins.' And the Maturidis held that the non-fulfillment of the threat is impossible just as the non-fulfillment of the promise is impossible. And it is not objected to that the threat is not fulfilled for the forgiven believer; because the verses that came with the generality of the threat are exempted from the forgiven believer. But as for the unforgiven, the threat must be carried out in him. So their saying: 'Carrying out the threat in him is necessary, even if in one [person]'—which comes in his statement: 'And it is obligatory to punish some who commit major sins' up to the end—only appears according to the Maturidi view, and it is built on the difference between the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. On the Ash'ari view it is valid to say: 'O God, forgive all believers all their sins,' while that is not valid on the Maturidi view. So it appears that the difference is real, even if some have made it verbal. So ponder. (1) Reported by al-Bazzar as in Kashf al-Astar, and Abu Ya'la, and al-Tabarani in al-Awsat as in Majma' al-Zawa'id.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the permissibility of God not carrying out His threat of punishment (وعيد) while always fulfilling His promise (وعد). It begins by stating an objection: that allowing threat divergence leads to several problems. The first problem is that it would imply lying in God's report, but consensus holds God's report is truthful. The second is that it would involve a change of statement, contradicting the Quranic verse 'There is no change of statement with Me' (Qaf 50:29). The third is that it might allow that disbelievers are not eternally punished, contradicting definitive proofs of their eternal punishment. The commentator then gives three answers. For the first: when a generous person issues a threat, it is fitting that his intention be conditional on his will, even if not stated—unlike a promise, which is made in earnest. A hadith is cited to support this difference. For the second: the verse prohibiting change of statement is confined to threats against disbelievers or those for whom God has not willed forgiveness. For the third: threat divergence applies only where forgiveness is possible (i.e. for sins other than disbelief), so it does not affect the eternity of disbelievers whose punishment forgiveness cannot be waived. The Quranic verse 'God does not forgive shirk...' (An-Nisa 4:48) restricts the general verse 'God forgives all sins' (Az-Zumar 39:53). Next, the Maturidi position is introduced: they hold that threat divergence is impossible, just as promise divergence is impossible. They respond to the objection about forgiven believers by saying that the general threat verses are understood to exclude such believers. For the unforgiven, punishment must occur. This leads to a real difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis: on the Ash'ari view, one can pray for forgiveness for all believers collectively, while on the Maturidi view, one cannot, because the threat must be carried out on some. The passage concludes by affirming that the difference is substantive, not merely verbal, and urges reflection.

Key Terms
الوعيد
الوعد
تخلف الوعيد
الكرم
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that threat divergence implies God lies or contradicts scripture.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer shows that threats are conditional on divine will, so God is truthful, and scriptural restrictions prevent contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the Generous One issues a threat, what is His underlying intention?
A) He will certainly carry it out.
B) It is conditional on His will (إن شئت).
C) It is a test for the listener.
D) It applies only to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Divine threats are conditional on God's will, preserving truthfulness while allowing forgiveness for sins less than shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you assume God will forgive you because of His mercy, or do you truly fear His threat of punishment? How does the conditionality of threats in this lesson change your sense of accountability?
?
This lesson teaches that God's promises are unconditional but His threats are conditional on His will. Does that make you more confident in His rewards, or does it make you uneasy about your own salvation? How should this affect the way you seek forgiveness?
?
If God can choose not to punish a sin, why do you think He still warns us of punishment? What does that tell you about His love and justice, and how should that motivate you to take both His threats and promises seriously?

The Ash'ari-Maturidi Dispute on Threat Divergence
Al-Bijuri contrasts Maturidi view and shows the real difference with Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that threat divergence is impossible and how it leads to a real difference with the Ash'aris regarding prayer for forgiveness and the necessity of punishment for some sinners.

Core Idea
Maturidis require threat fulfillment; Ash'aris allow forgiveness even without punishment.

After presenting the Ash'ari view, Al-Bijuri introduces the Maturidi stance that God cannot fail to carry out His threat, just as He cannot fail His promise. This leads to a practical difference: on the Ash'ari view, one can pray for all believers' forgiveness; on the Maturidi view, some must be punished. The passage asserts that the difference is substantive, not merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maturidi position
Note that Maturidis hold it is impossible for God to not fulfill His threat, similar to promise. They argue threat verses are general but exclude forgiven believers.
The key phrase: 'يمتنع تخلف الوعيد كما يمتنع تخلف الوعد'.
2
See how they handle forgiven believers
They say general threat verses are exempted for forgiven believers; for the unforgiven, punishment must occur.
This leads to the practical difference with Ash'aris.
3
Identify the practical consequence
On Ash'ari view, you can pray 'O God forgive all believers all sins.' On Maturidi view, you cannot, because some must be punished.
Al-Bijuri says this shows the difference is real.
4
Reflect on the nature of the disagreement
The passage concludes that the difference is genuine (حقيقي), not just verbal, urging careful study.
Some earlier scholars had called it verbal, but Al-Bijuri disagrees.

?

?

?

Felicity and Misery: Predestined in Eternity and Unchanging
Al-Bijuri explains the verse about predestined felicity and misery and their immutability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that felicity and misery are predetermined in God's eternal knowledge and that this predestination is immutable, with the final end indicating the eternal decree.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge determines each person's final end, which never changes.

In this passage from his commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid, Imam al-Bijuri explains the meaning of a key verse about predestination. He clarifies that the felicity of the believer and the misery of the disbeliever are not based on their present state but on God's eternal knowledge of their final outcome. This understanding resolves apparent contradictions between human actions and divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation to get a sense of the passage's flow and meaning.
Pay attention to the grammatical parsing mentioned in the commentary.
2
Understand key terms
Focus on the meanings of 'fawz' (triumph/salvation), 'sa'ada' (felicity), 'shaqawa' (misery), and 'azal' (eternity without beginning). These are central to the argument.
Al-Bijuri defines 'fawz' as 'al-najah wa al-zafar bi al-khair' (salvation and attainment of good).
3
Grasp the predestination principle
Notice that predestination is based on God's eternal knowledge, not the current state of faith or disbelief. The phrase 'indahu' (with Him) refers to His knowledge.
This distinguishes between the present attribute (iman/kufr) and the eternal decree.
4
Reflect on immutability and final end
Consider why the decree does not change: if it did, God's knowledge would change, which is impossible. Then see how the final end (death state) indicates the eternal decree.
The passage concludes that whoever dies in faith was predestined as felicitous, even if they had disbelief before.

السعادة والشقاوةُ مُقدرانِ في الأزلِ] ٤٧ - فَوْزُ السَّعِيدِ عِنْدَهُ فِي الأَزَلِ كَذَا الشَّقِيُّ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَنْتَقِلِ قوله: (فَوْزُ السَّعِيدِ عِنْدَهُ فِي الأَزلِ) (فوز) مبتدأ، وفي الأزل) متعلّق بمحذوف خبر، والظرف المضاف للضمير العائد على الله تعالى متعلق بمحذوف حالٍ، والتقدير : فوز السعيد مقدَّر في الأزل حال كونه سابقاً عنده تعالى؛ أي: في عِلْمِه، فالمراد من العندية: العلم. و(الفوزُ) النجاة والظفر بالخير كما في القاموس»، و(الأزل) عبارةٌ عن عدم الأولية، أو عن استمرار الوجود في أزمنة مقدَّرة غير متناهية في جانب الماضي، وإنَّما قلنا : (مقدرة)؛ لأنه لا أزمنة في الأزل، فهي مقدَّرة لا محققة. وقوله : كَذَا الشَّقِيُّ ؛ أي: شقاؤه عنده في الأزل مثل فوز السعيد، فليس كلٌّ مِنْ فوز السعيد، وشقاء الشقي باعتبار الوصف القائم به في الحال من الإيمان في الأول والكفر في الثاني، بل باعتبار ما سبق أزلاً في علمه تعالى . وقوله : (ثُمَّ لَمْ يَنْتَقِلِ) أي : لم يتحول كلُّ واحدٍ من السعيد والشقي عما سبق أزلاً في علمه تعالى . فالسعيد لا ينقلب شفيًّا، وبالعكس، وإلا لزم انقلاب العلم جهلاً، وهو بديهي الاستحالة. فالسعادة والشقاوة مقدرتان في الأزل لا يتغيران ولا يتبدلان؛ لأنَّ السعادة هي الموتُ على الإيمان باعتبار تعلق علم الله أزلاً بذلك، والشقاوة هي الموت على الكفر بذلك الاعتبار، فالخاتمة تدلُّ على السابقة، فإن ختم له بالإيمان دلَّ على أنه في الأزل كان من السعداء، وإن تقدَّمه كفر، وإن خُتم له بالكفر دلَّ على أنَّه فى الأزل كان من الأشقياء، وإن تقدَّمه إيمان، كما يدلُّ له حديث
[Felicity and misery are predestined in eternity.] 47 - The triumph of the felicitous is with Him in eternity; likewise the miserable; then it does not change. His statement: (The triumph of the felicitous is with Him in eternity) - 'Triumph' is the subject, and 'in eternity' is a predicate attached to a deleted word, and the adverb added to the pronoun referring to God is attached to a deleted circumstantial accusative. The estimation is: The triumph of the felicitous is predestined in eternity while being precedent with Him - i.e. in His knowledge. So the intended meaning of 'with' is knowledge. And 'triumph' means salvation and attainment of good, as in al-Qamus. And 'eternity' is an expression for lack of beginning, or for the continuation of existence in imagined infinite times on the past side. We only say 'imagined' because there are no times in eternity; they are imagined, not realized. And His statement: 'Likewise the miserable' means his misery is with Him in eternity like the triumph of the felicitous. So each of the triumph of the felicitous and the misery of the miserable is not by virtue of the description existing in the present state of faith in the first and disbelief in the second, but by virtue of what was previously eternal in His knowledge. And His statement: 'Then it does not change' means that neither the felicitous nor the miserable changes from what was previously eternal in His knowledge. So the felicitous does not turn into a miserable, and vice versa, otherwise it would require the change of knowledge into ignorance, which is self-evidently impossible. So felicity and misery are predestined in eternity and do not change nor alter; because felicity is dying upon faith by virtue of God's knowledge being eternally attached to that, and misery is dying upon disbelief by that same virtue. So the final end indicates the precedent state. Whoever ends with faith indicates that he was in eternity from among the felicitous, even if disbelief preceded him; and whoever ends with disbelief indicates that he was in eternity from among the miserable, even if faith preceded him, as is indicated by the hadith...
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the verse from the poem that states the predestination of felicity and misery in eternity. The commentator begins by parsing the verse grammatically. 'Fawz al-sa'id 'indahu fi al-azal' — 'the triumph of the felicitous is with Him in eternity' — he notes that 'fawz' is the subject, and 'fi al-azal' is the predicate, with the adverb 'indahu' (with Him) being a circumstantial. The implied meaning is that the triumph is predestined in eternity, already known to God. The word 'fawz' means salvation and attainment of good, and 'azal' means timelessness without beginning, with imaginary times on the past side. He clarifies that the phrase 'with Him' refers to God's knowledge. The next phrase 'kadha al-shaqiy' means the misery of the miserable is similarly predetermined in eternity. Importantly, this predestination is not based on the current state of faith or disbelief but on God's eternal knowledge of the outcome. The phrase 'thumma lam yantaqil' means that the state of felicity or misery does not change: a felicitous person never becomes miserable, and vice versa, because that would imply a change in God's knowledge, which is impossible. The commentator then summarizes: felicity and misery are fixed in eternity; felicity is dying upon faith as known by God from eternity, and misery is dying upon disbelief as known by God. The final end (the death state) indicates the eternal predestination: if one dies in faith, even if preceded by disbelief, he was from the felicitous; if one dies in disbelief, even if preceded by faith, he was from the miserable. The passage ends by referencing a hadith that supports this.

Key Terms

الشقاوة
الأزل
الفوز
العلم الأزلي
الخاتمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that predestination means present faith or disbelief determines the outcome; the passage clarifies it is based on God's eternal knowledge of the final end, not the current state.
Correction: According to this passage, felicity and misery are predestined in eternity based on God's knowledge of the final end. The present state of faith or disbelief does not directly determine predestination; rather, the final end reveals the eternal decree.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the term 'fawz' mean?
A) Predestination
B) Salvation and attainment of good
C) The final end
D) God's knowledge

Key Takeaway
Felicity and misery are fixed by God's eternal knowledge and do not change; the final end reveals the eternal decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your final end is already eternally known to God, does that make you feel complacent or more urgent about your actions? What specific change in your daily habits would show that you truly believe this truth?
?
Think of someone you know who lives in sin but you hope will die in faith. How does this lesson challenge you to pray or act for them differently, knowing that their end is already fixed in God's knowledge?
?
The lesson says the final end reveals the eternal decree. If you were to die tonight, what would your death reveal about your eternal state—and does that thought terrify you enough to change something today?

Ash'ari and Maturidi Definitions of Happiness and Misery
Enumeration of Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions and their consequence on saying 'if Allah wills'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) differ and why this leads to different rulings on saying 'I am a believer, if Allah wills'.

Core Idea
Happiness and misery are defined as future or present states, affecting whether we can suspend them with 'if Allah wills'.

This passage introduces two major theological schools within Sunni Islam—the Ash'aris and the Maturidis—and their distinct definitions of spiritual happiness and misery. At first glance, the difference seems subtle, but it has direct implications for a common phrase used by Muslims: 'I am a believer, if Allah wills.' Understanding this distinction is key to grasping why scholars have differed on the permissibility of that statement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions
Read the passage carefully. The Ash'aris define happiness as dying upon faith (future state), while the Maturidis define it as faith in the present. Misery is the opposite.
2
See the effect on 'if Allah wills'
Notice that according to Ash'aris, saying 'if Allah wills' about being a believer is valid because the future is uncertain. For Maturidis, it is not valid because present faith is already actual.
3
Explore the commentary's clarification
The commentary points out that the text's claim about the consequence is not immediately apparent. It explains why the ruling follows from each school's definition.
4
Consider the additional imam-based classification
The passage also mentions that Shafi'i permitted, Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited, and some Malikis obliged the phrase, provided no doubt is intended.
This adds another dimension to the discussion.

و خوفُ العامة من الخاتمة، وخوف الخاصة من السابقة، وهو هذا ما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة . هي أشد وإن تلازما وذهبت الماتريدية إلى أن السعادة الإيمان في الحال، والشقاوة هي الكفر كذلك ؛ فالسعيد هو المؤمنُ في الحال، وإذا مات على الكفر فقد انقلب شقيا بعد أن كان سعيداً، والشقي هو الكافر في الحال، وإذا مات على الإيمان فقد انقلب سعيداً بعد أن كان شقيا . الخلافُ في قول القائل: (أنا مؤمنٌ إن شاء الله)] ويترتب على الخلاف (٢) بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية أنه يصح أن تقول: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله على قول الأشاعرة، وأنَّه لا يصح ذلك على الثاني. وحكى بعضُهم في ذلك خلافاً على غير هذا الوجه حيث قال: جوزه الشافعي، ومنعه مالك وأبو حنيفة، وقال بعضُ أتباع مالك بوجوبه، وذلك إن لم يرد الشك(٣) قوله: (ويترتب على الخلاف إلخ) هذا الترتيب غير ظاهر بل الظاهر صحة أن يقال : سعيد إن شاء الله على الأول، وعدم صحة ذلك على الثاني ؛ لأن السعادة عند الأشاعرة: هي الموت على الإيمان، فهي مستقبلة، فيصح تعليقها. وعند الماتريدية : هي الإيمان الحالي؛ أي: الحاصل بالفعل، والحاصل بالفعل لا يعلق والخلاف في تعليق الإيمان وعدمه إنما هو بين الأئمة، هذا ما ظهر . انتهى أجهوري. قوله : (وذلك إن لم يرد الشك ... إلخ)، الشك الممنوع أن يتردد في أنه هل يستمر على الإيمان أو يقطعه، وأما لو تردد الآن في أنه هل يكون مؤمناً عند الموت أو لا، فهذا شك غير ممنوع؛ لأن الخاتمة مجهولة . والحاصل أن الآتي بالمشيئة، إما أن يقصد التبرك، وإما أن يقصد التعليق ومن لازمه الشك، وإما أن يطلق، وللتعليق باعتبار الشك اللازم صورتان؛ لأنه تارةً يشك في أنه هل يستمر على الإيمان أو يقطعه، وتارة يشك هل يكون مؤمناً عند الموت أو لا ، فإن قصد التبرك جازت المشيئة بلا خلاف وإن قصد التعليق مع الصورة الأولى للشك امتنع بلا خلاف، فتبين أن محل الخلاف ما لو أطلق أو قصد التعليق مع صورة الشك الثانية. هذا ما ظهر. انتهى أجهوري. وقد نظم بعضُ الأفاضل حاصل هذا، فقال: [من الرجز] مَنْ قَالَ: إِنِّي مُؤْمِنٌ يَمْنَع مِنْ مَقَالِهِ إِنْ شَاءَ رَبِّيْ) يَا فَطِنْ وَذَا لِمَالكِ، وَبَعْضُ تَابِعِيه يُوجِبُ أَنْ يَقُولَ هَذَا يَا نَبِية وَمِثْلُ مَا لِمَالِكِ للحَنَفِي والشَّافِعِي جَوْزَ هَذَا فَاعْرِفِ وَامْنَعْهُ إِجْمَاعاً إِذَا أَرَادَ بِهْ الشَّكَ فِي إِيْمَانِهِ يَا مُنْتَبِهْ كَعَدَمِ المَنْعِ إِذَا بِهِ يُرَادْ تَبَرُّكُ بِذِكْرِ خَالِقِ العِبَادُ فَالخُلْفَ حَيْثُ لَمْ يُرِدْ شَكًا وَلَا تَبَرُّكاً فَكُنْ بِذَا مُحْتَفِلَا وبالجملة فالخلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية لفظي ; لأنَّهم اختلفوا في المراد من لفظ السعادة، ولفظ الشقاوة مع الاتفاق في الأحكام.
And the fear of the common people is of the final end, and the fear of the elite is of the prior decree, and this is what the Ash'aris held. It is more intense although they are inseparable. And the Maturidis held that happiness is faith in the present, and misery is disbelief likewise; so the happy one is the believer in the present, and if he dies upon disbelief, he becomes miserable after having been happy, and the miserable one is the disbeliever in the present, and if he dies upon faith, he becomes happy after having been miserable. The dispute is concerning the saying of one who says: 'I am a believer, if Allah wills.' And it follows from the dispute between the Ash'aris and Maturidis that it is permissible to say: 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' according to the Ash'aris, and that it is not permissible according to the second. And some narrated on that a dispute on a different basis, where he said: Al-Shafi'i permitted it, and Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited it, and some followers of Malik made it obligatory, and that is if he does not intend doubt. His saying: 'And it follows from the dispute etc.' This conclusion is not apparent; rather the apparent is the correctness of saying 'happy if Allah wills' according to the first, and the incorrectness of that according to the second; because happiness according to the Ash'aris is dying upon faith, so it is future, so it is valid to suspend it. According to the Maturidis it is present faith, i.e., actually attained, and what is actually attained cannot be suspended, and the dispute about suspending faith or not is only between the imams. This is what appeared. End of al-Ujhuri. His saying: 'and that if he does not intend doubt' etc. The prohibited doubt is that he is uncertain whether he will persist in faith or abandon it. As for if he is uncertain now whether he will be a believer at death or not, this is not prohibited, because the final end is unknown. The summary is that the one who uses the conditional particle intends either blessing (tabarruk) or suspension (ta'liq) with its necessary doubt, or he uses it absolutely. And for suspension with respect to the necessary doubt there are two forms: sometimes he doubts whether he will persist in faith or abandon it, and sometimes he doubts whether he will be a believer at death or not. If he intends blessing, the conditional is permitted without dispute; if he intends suspension with the first form of doubt, it is prohibited without dispute; so it becomes clear that the locus of dispute is when he uses it absolutely or intends suspension with the second form of doubt. This is what appeared. End of al-Ujhuri. And some virtuous ones versified the conclusion, saying (in rajaz meter): Whoever says 'I am a believer' is prevented from his saying 'if my Lord wills', O intelligent one, and this (attributed) to Malik, and some of his followers oblige him to say this, O wise one, and like (the view) of Malik for the Hanafi, and al-Shafi'i permitted this, so know, and prohibit it unanimously if he intended by it doubt in his faith, O attentive one, like the absence of prohibition if by it blessing is intended through mentioning the Creator of servants. So the disagreement where he does not intend doubt nor blessing, so be careful about that. In sum, the dispute between the Ash'aris and Maturidis is verbal; because they disagreed about the meaning of the word 'happiness' and the word 'misery' while agreeing on the rulings.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the theological issue of saying 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' and its connection to the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa). The commentary begins by stating that the common people fear the final end of life (khatima), while the elite fear the prior decree (sabiqa), which is the Ash'ari position. The Ash'aris hold that happiness consists in dying upon faith, and misery in dying upon disbelief, so they are future states. The Maturidis, however, hold that happiness is faith in the present, and misery is disbelief in the present; thus a person can change from happy to miserable or vice versa depending on their state at death. It follows from this difference that for the Ash'aris it is valid to say 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' because the future state of happiness is uncertain and can be suspended, while for the Maturidis it is not valid because present faith is already actual and cannot be suspended. The passage then reports a different classification attributed to the imams: al-Shafi'i permitted it, Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited it, and some Malikis made it obligatory, provided that the speaker does not intend doubt. The commentary clarifies that the apparent ruling differs from what is stated, explaining that according to the Ash'aris happiness is future, so suspending it is valid, while for the Maturidis it is present, so it cannot be suspended. It then discusses the different intentions in using the conditional: blessing (tabarruk), suspension (ta'liq) which entails doubt, or absolute use. Two forms of doubt are distinguished: doubt about persistence in faith until death, and doubt about whether one will have faith at death. The first type makes suspension prohibited by consensus, the second is not prohibited. The dispute is only when the speaker uses the conditional absolutely or with the second type of doubt. The passage ends with a verse summarizing the views and concludes that the dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis is verbal, since they agree on the rulings but differ on the definitions of happiness and misery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'If Allah wills,' do you realize that your school of thought might prohibit it? How does this challenge your daily speech and your understanding of your own faith? Will you investigate your own position or simply follow custom?
?
According to the Maturidis, you are happy if you are a believer right now. Does this make you complacent or spur you to ensure you die upon faith? Examine your heart: do you fear your final end or you fear what Allah decreed for you before birth? Which fear dominates your worship?
?
The elite fear the prior decree more than the final end. Do you truly fear Allah's decree over your life? How does that fear affect your reliance on Him? Commit today to focusing on your present state of faith while trusting His mercy for your end.

The Permissibility of 'If Allah Wills' According to Intent and the Verbal Nature of the Dispute
Analysis of intentions behind 'if Allah wills' and conclusion that the Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different intentions behind using the phrase 'if Allah wills' and why the overall dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis is ultimately verbal.

Core Idea
The phrase 'if Allah wills' can have two intentions: blessing or suspension, and only one type of doubt is prohibited.

This passage delves deeper into the practical ruling on saying 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' by analyzing the speaker's intention. It distinguishes between blessing (tabarruk) and suspension (ta'liq), which entails doubt, and further divides doubt into two forms. The discussion culminates in the striking conclusion that the entire dispute between the Ash'aris and Maturidis is verbal—a difference in wording, not in substance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two intentions
The passage explains that the speaker may intend blessing (tabarruk) or suspension (ta'liq), or use the phrase absolutely. The intention matters for the ruling.
2
Distinguish the two forms of doubt
One form of doubt is about whether one will persist in faith until death; the other is about whether one will have faith at death. Only the first makes suspension prohibited by consensus.
3
Identify the locus of dispute
The disagreement among scholars occurs only when the speaker uses the phrase absolutely or intends suspension with the second form of doubt.
4
Reflect on the verbal nature of the dispute
The passage concludes that the Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute is verbal because they agree on the rulings but differ on the definitions of the terms 'happiness' and 'misery'.
