على الإيمان أو يقطعه، وتارة يشك هل يكون مؤمناً عند الموت أو لا ، فإن قصد التبرك جازت المشيئة بلا خلاف وإن قصد التعليق مع الصورة الأولى للشك امتنع بلا خلاف، فتبين أن محل الخلاف ما لو أطلق أو قصد التعليق مع صورة الشك الثانية. هذا ما ظهر. انتهى أجهوري. وقد نظم بعضُ الأفاضل حاصل هذا، فقال: [من الرجز] مَنْ قَالَ: إِنِّي مُؤْمِنٌ يَمْنَع مِنْ مَقَالِهِ إِنْ شَاءَ رَبِّيْ) يَا فَطِنْ وَذَا لِمَالكِ، وَبَعْضُ تَابِعِيه يُوجِبُ أَنْ يَقُولَ هَذَا يَا نَبِية وَمِثْلُ مَا لِمَالِكِ للحَنَفِي والشَّافِعِي جَوْزَ هَذَا فَاعْرِفِ وَامْنَعْهُ إِجْمَاعاً إِذَا أَرَادَ بِهْ الشَّكَ فِي إِيْمَانِهِ يَا مُنْتَبِهْ كَعَدَمِ المَنْعِ إِذَا بِهِ يُرَادْ تَبَرُّكُ بِذِكْرِ خَالِقِ العِبَادُ فَالخُلْفَ حَيْثُ لَمْ يُرِدْ شَكًا وَلَا تَبَرُّكاً فَكُنْ بِذَا مُحْتَفِلَا وبالجملة فالخلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية لفظي ; لأنَّهم اختلفوا في المراد من لفظ السعادة، ولفظ الشقاوة مع الاتفاق في الأحكام.
And the fear of the common people is of the final end, and the fear of the elite is of the prior decree, and this is what the Ash'aris held. It is more intense although they are inseparable. And the Maturidis held that happiness is faith in the present, and misery is disbelief likewise; so the happy one is the believer in the present, and if he dies upon disbelief, he becomes miserable after having been happy, and the miserable one is the disbeliever in the present, and if he dies upon faith, he becomes happy after having been miserable. The dispute is concerning the saying of one who says: 'I am a believer, if Allah wills.' And it follows from the dispute between the Ash'aris and Maturidis that it is permissible to say: 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' according to the Ash'aris, and that it is not permissible according to the second. And some narrated on that a dispute on a different basis, where he said: Al-Shafi'i permitted it, and Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited it, and some followers of Malik made it obligatory, and that is if he does not intend doubt. His saying: 'And it follows from the dispute etc.' This conclusion is not apparent; rather the apparent is the correctness of saying 'happy if Allah wills' according to the first, and the incorrectness of that according to the second; because happiness according to the Ash'aris is dying upon faith, so it is future, so it is valid to suspend it. According to the Maturidis it is present faith, i.e., actually attained, and what is actually attained cannot be suspended, and the dispute about suspending faith or not is only between the imams. This is what appeared. End of al-Ujhuri. His saying: 'and that if he does not intend doubt' etc. The prohibited doubt is that he is uncertain whether he will persist in faith or abandon it. As for if he is uncertain now whether he will be a believer at death or not, this is not prohibited, because the final end is unknown. The summary is that the one who uses the conditional particle intends either blessing (tabarruk) or suspension (ta'liq) with its necessary doubt, or he uses it absolutely. And for suspension with respect to the necessary doubt there are two forms: sometimes he doubts whether he will persist in faith or abandon it, and sometimes he doubts whether he will be a believer at death or not. If he intends blessing, the conditional is permitted without dispute; if he intends suspension with the first form of doubt, it is prohibited without dispute; so it becomes clear that the locus of dispute is when he uses it absolutely or intends suspension with the second form of doubt. This is what appeared. End of al-Ujhuri. And some virtuous ones versified the conclusion, saying (in rajaz meter): Whoever says 'I am a believer' is prevented from his saying 'if my Lord wills', O intelligent one, and this (attributed) to Malik, and some of his followers oblige him to say this, O wise one, and like (the view) of Malik for the Hanafi, and al-Shafi'i permitted this, so know, and prohibit it unanimously if he intended by it doubt in his faith, O attentive one, like the absence of prohibition if by it blessing is intended through mentioning the Creator of servants. So the disagreement where he does not intend doubt nor blessing, so be careful about that. In sum, the dispute between the Ash'aris and Maturidis is verbal; because they disagreed about the meaning of the word 'happiness' and the word 'misery' while agreeing on the rulings.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the theological issue of saying 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' and its connection to the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa). The commentary begins by stating that the common people fear the final end of life (khatima), while the elite fear the prior decree (sabiqa), which is the Ash'ari position. The Ash'aris hold that happiness consists in dying upon faith, and misery in dying upon disbelief, so they are future states. The Maturidis, however, hold that happiness is faith in the present, and misery is disbelief in the present; thus a person can change from happy to miserable or vice versa depending on their state at death. It follows from this difference that for the Ash'aris it is valid to say 'I am a believer, if Allah wills' because the future state of happiness is uncertain and can be suspended, while for the Maturidis it is not valid because present faith is already actual and cannot be suspended. The passage then reports a different classification attributed to the imams: al-Shafi'i permitted it, Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited it, and some Malikis made it obligatory, provided that the speaker does not intend doubt. The commentary clarifies that the apparent ruling differs from what is stated, explaining that according to the Ash'aris happiness is future, so suspending it is valid, while for the Maturidis it is present, so it cannot be suspended. It then discusses the different intentions in using the conditional: blessing (tabarruk), suspension (ta'liq) which entails doubt, or absolute use. Two forms of doubt are distinguished: doubt about persistence in faith until death, and doubt about whether one will have faith at death. The first type makes suspension prohibited by consensus, the second is not prohibited. The dispute is only when the speaker uses the conditional absolutely or with the second type of doubt. The passage ends with a verse summarizing the views and concludes that the dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis is verbal, since they agree on the rulings but differ on the definitions of happiness and misery.

Correction: The text concludes that the Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute is verbal; they agree on the rulings but define the terms differently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is saying 'if Allah wills' prohibited by consensus?
A) When intending blessing
B) When intending suspension with doubt about persistence in faith
C) When intending suspension with doubt about state at death
D) When using the phrase absolutely

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis over happiness and misery is ultimately verbal, as they agree on the practical rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'if Allah wills' about your own faith, do you feel a genuine desire for blessing, or a creeping fear that you might not endure? What does your intention reveal about your trust in Allah's preservation?
?
Are you more anxious about your state at the moment of death than about your present commitment to faith? How does this lesson's distinction between the two doubts expose where your heart truly lies?
?
The scholars disagreed over words but agreed on actions. Where in your life do you fight over theological phrasing while neglecting practical obedience? What would it cost you to let go of the need to be verbally 'right' and instead focus on what you actually do?

The Servant's Agency: Inwardly Compelled, Outwardly Free
Author justifies corrected version and states the servant's dual nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'aris affirm that humans are inwardly compelled yet outwardly free, and how this resolves the problem of moral responsibility.

Core Idea
The servant has no real effectiveness; he is compelled inwardly but chooses outwardly.

This passage explains why the corrected version of a verse is superior, and it culminates in the classic Ash'ari formulation: the servant is majbūr bāṭinan, mukhtār ẓāhiran. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Ash'ari qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read its literal translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through before analyzing.
Notice the technical terms like istidrāk and kash.
2
Understand the role of 'istidrāk'
The author argues that the corrected version needs no istidrāk. Istidrāk is a rhetorical device used to correct a false impression. Understand how it works with the examples given.
3
Compare the two versions
The author compares the corrected and the circulating versions. Note the advantage of the circulating version: explicit mention of 'bi-hi' and better meter.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The passage ends with the clear Ash'ari position: the servant has no effectiveness, is inwardly compelled, outwardly free. Reflect on how this balances divine omnipotence and human accountability.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه الأحسنيَّةِ : أنَّه لا محل للاستدراك ؛ فإنَّه يُساق) لدفع ما يتوهم ثبوته، أو لإثبات ما يتوهم نفيه ؛ كما في قولهم : زيد شجاعٌ لكنَّه ليس بكريم، وكما في قولهم : زيد جبانٌ لكنَّه كريمٌ، وهنا لا يتوهم ثبوت التأثير من التعبير بالكسب؛ لأن اصطلاحهم أنَّ الكسب لا تأثير فيه؛ إلا أن يُقال: ربَّما يُتوهم أنه يؤثر في مكسوبه، وقد يُقال : المتداولة أحسن ؛ لما فيها من التصريح بلفظ (به) ؛ والمعنى (عليه)، ولو صرّح به على النسخة المصححة لم يستقم الوزن، نعم يُحتاج في رجز المتداولة لتسكين راء (يؤثر) . والألف في قوله : (فَلْتعرفا أو فَاعْرِفَا بدل من نون التوكيد الخفيفة في الوقف. وبالجملة فليس للعبد تأثير ما ، فهو مجبور باطناً، مختار ظاهراً .
The aspect of superiority is that there is no place for correction (istidrāk); for it (the verse) is brought to repudiate what is mistakenly thought to be established, or to establish what is mistakenly thought to be negated — as in their saying: 'Zayd is brave but he is not generous,' and as in their saying: 'Zayd is cowardly but he is generous.' Here, no one mistakenly thinks that effectiveness is established from the expression of kash, because their convention is that kash has no effectiveness — unless it is said: perhaps one might mistakenly think that it influences its acquired object. And it may be said: the circulating version is better, because it contains the explicit mention of the word 'bi-hi' (with it) and the meaning is 'upon it' (ʿalayhi). And if it were explicitly stated according to the corrected version, the meter would not be correct. Yes, in the rajaz of the circulating version, one needs to stop (sukūn) the raa of 'yuʾthir'. And the alif in his saying: 'fal-taʿrifā' or 'faʿrifā' substitutes for the light nun of emphasis in pause. In summary, the servant has no effectiveness at all; so he is inwardly compelled (majbūr) and outwardly free (mukhtār).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the corrected verse. The author argues that the corrected version is superior because it avoids the need for a corrective (istidrāk) — a logical device used to cancel a false impression. In the circulating version, the word 'lā yuʾaththir' might give the impression that the servant could affect but does not, requiring a corrective. The corrected version denies effectiveness outright ('wa-lam yakun muʾaththiran'), so no corrective is needed. He then compares with examples like 'Zayd is brave but not generous' where 'but' corrects a potential misconception. He also notes that the circulating version has advantages: it explicitly mentions 'bi-hi' (with it) and fits the meter better after adjusting the pronunciation. The passage concludes with the central Ash'ari position: the servant has no real effectiveness; he is inwardly compelled (since God creates all acts) but outwardly appears to choose (because he has kash and is held responsible).

Key Terms

اصطلاح
مجبور باطناً
مختار ظاهراً
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'inwardly compelled' negates moral responsibility, but the Ash'aris hold that outward choice (kash) is sufficient for accountability.
Correction: The passage affirms that the servant is outwardly free (mukhtār ẓāhiran), meaning he experiences choice and is responsible, even though God creates his actions.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion about the servant's role in his actions?
A) He creates his own actions with full effectiveness
B) He has no effectiveness, being inwardly compelled and outwardly free
C) He is completely free without any divine involvement
D) He shares effectiveness with God

Key Takeaway
The servant has no real causal effectiveness; his actions are created by God, but he is accountable through his acquisition (kash) and outward choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your worst sin, do you truly believe that God created that action while you are still accountable? How does this change the way you seek forgiveness—do you blame God, yourself, or both? Be honest: what does your heart feel when you read 'the servant has no effectiveness at all'?
?
Think of a recent success you had. Did you inwardly attribute it to your own skill or effort? According to this lesson, what is the reality? How should this reshape your gratitude and humility tonight?
?
If you are inwardly compelled but outwardly free, where is the line between accepting God's decree and striving to obey? In a specific temptation you face now, how does the concept of kash (acquiring the action without causal power) affect your decision to act or refrain? What concrete step will you take today to live out this 'outward freedom' while relying solely on God?

Pure Grace and Pure Justice in Divine Action
Al-Ajhuri defines pure grace and pure justice and refutes philosophers and Mu'tazilites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of pure grace and pure justice in divine action and how Ahl al-Sunna refute the views of philosophers and Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
God's reward is pure grace; punishment, pure justice—neither is obligatory upon Him.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid unpacks two foundational principles of Ash'ari theology: that God rewards the obedient purely out of grace, not necessity, and that He punishes the disobedient out of pure justice, not obligation. These terms are carefully defined to refute the deterministic philosophers and the Mu'tazilites who claimed God is compelled to reward or punish. Understanding this distinction clarifies the Islamic stance on divine freedom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and literal translation.
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then its literal English translation. Try to get a feel for the commentator's style and the key terms before diving into analysis.
Pay attention to the genitive constructions like 'فبمحض الفضل'.
2
Identify the two definitions: pure grace and pure justice.
Find where the commentator defines 'pure grace' and 'pure justice'. Notice how each definition is built: grace as giving by complete choice, justice as placing things in their proper place.
The refutations of philosophers and Mu'tazilites are embedded in these definitions.
3
Understand the refutations of philosophers and Mu'tazilites.
The commentator contrasts Ahl al-Sunna with the philosophers (who say reward is compelled by causality) and the Mu'tazilites (who say it is obligatory). See how the phrases 'بالفضل' and 'الخالص' respectively refute these groups.
4
Reflect on the broader theological principle.
Why does it matter that reward is pure grace? It underscores God's absolute freedom and sovereignty. The servant's obedience does not create a claim on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ) أي: فإثابته لنا إنَّما هي بفضله المحض ؛ أي: الخالص، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الصفة للموصوف، ومعنى الفضل المحض : الإعطاء عن اختيار كامل لا عن إيجاب، بحيث يثيبنا ولا اختيار له في الإثابة أبداً؛ لكونها علة تنشأ عنها معلولاتها من غير اختيار لها، كما يقوله الحكماء، ولا عن وجوب بحيث تصير الإثابة مستحقة لازمة يقبح عليه تعالى تركها فيثيبنا باختياره لكن مع الوجوب كما يقوله المعتزلة، فمذهب أهل السنة أنَّ إثابته تعالى لنا بالفضل الخالص غير مشوبة بإيجاب ولا وجوب. فقولنا : (بالفضل) ردُّ لكلام الحكماء. وقولنا : (الخالص) ردُّ لكلام المعتزلة، ويدلُّ لمذهب أهل السنة أنَّ طاعات العبد وإن كثرتُ لا تفي بشكر بعض ما أنعم الله به عليه فكيف يتصور استحقاقه عوضاً عليها؟ . قوله : (وَإِنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ أي : وإن يعذبنا فتعذيبه إنَّما هو بالعدل المحض (۱)؛ أي: الخالص، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الصفة للموصوف كما مرَّ في نظيره ومعنى العدل المحض وضع الشيء في محله من غير اعتراض على الفاعل، ضدُّ الظلم الذي هو وضع الشيء في غير محله مع الاعتراض على فاعله . (۱) قوله : (بالعدل المحض) وصف العدل بأنه محض لبيان الواقع ؛ لأن عدل الله لا يكون إلا محضاً. وقوله : ( معنى العدل المحض) الأولى إسقاط (المحض) ؛ لأن ما ذكره معنى للعدل بقطع النظر عن كونه محضاً، وعبارة المصنف في شرحه: (ومعنى (العدل)، ولم يذكر لفظ المحض، والمقصود بقول المصنف : (وإن يعذب فبمحض العدل الردُّ على المعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب تعذيب العاصي، لقولهم بوجوب إثابة الطائع ، وبَنَوا ذلك على قاعدتهم من أن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية التي منها الطاعة والمعصية. وأما أهل السنة فقاعدتهم أن الله هو الخالق للأفعال كلّها ومنها الطاعة والمعصية، وبنوا على ذلك أن الإثابة بالفضل والتعذيب بالعدل، وليسا واجبين عليه تعالى، يُفهم ذلك كله من شرح صاحب المتن. انتهى أجهوري.
His saying: 'By pure grace' meaning: His rewarding us is only by His pure grace; i.e., the unadulterated. So the genitive construction in his speech is of the type 'attribute to described'. The meaning of pure grace is: giving by complete choice, not by compulsion, such that He rewards us while He has no choice in rewarding ever, because it would be a cause that gives rise to its effects without choice, as the philosophers say. Nor by obligation such that reward becomes necessarily due and it would be reprehensible for Him to abandon it, so He rewards us by His choice but with obligation, as the Mu'tazilites say. The school of Ahl al-Sunna is that His reward to us is by pure grace, unadulterated by compulsion or obligation. So our saying 'by grace' is a refutation of the philosophers' statement. And our saying 'pure' is a refutation of the Mu'tazilites' statement. And it indicates for the school of Ahl al-Sunna that the obediences of the servant, even if numerous, do not suffice for gratitude for some of what God has bestowed upon him; so how can he be imagined to deserve compensation for them? His saying: 'And if He punishes, then by pure justice' meaning: And if He punishes us, then His punishment is only by pure justice (1); i.e., the unadulterated. So the genitive construction in his speech is of the type 'attribute to described', as passed in its counterpart. And the meaning of pure justice is placing a thing in its proper place without objection against the agent, the opposite of injustice which is placing a thing in other than its proper place with objection against its agent. (1) His saying: 'by pure justice' – describing justice as 'pure' is to clarify the reality; because God's justice is only pure. And his saying: 'the meaning of pure justice' – it is better to drop 'pure' because what he mentioned is the meaning of justice irrespective of it being pure. The author's expression in his commentary is: 'And the meaning of justice', and he did not mention the word 'pure'. And the intent of the author's saying: 'And if He punishes, then by pure justice' is the refutation of the Mu'tazilites regarding their statement of the necessity of punishing the disobedient, just as they assert the necessity of rewarding the obedient. They built that on their principle that the servant creates his own voluntary actions which include obedience and disobedience. As for Ahl al-Sunna, their principle is that God is the creator of all actions, including obedience and disobedience. They built on that that reward is by grace and punishment by justice, and they are not obligatory upon Him, may He be exalted. All that is understood from the commentary of the author of the text. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of two key terms from Jawharat al-Tawhid: 'pure grace' and 'pure justice'. The commentator first explains 'pure grace' that God's reward is by His pure choice, not compelled as philosophers claim (obligation of effect from cause) nor obligatory as Mu'tazilites claim (moral necessity). He emphasizes that Ahl al-Sunna hold reward as pure grace without any compulsion or obligation. Then he explains 'pure justice' as placing things in their proper place, opposite of injustice. He refutes the Mu'tazilite position that punishment for sin is obligatory due to the servant creating his own actions. Instead, Ahl al-Sunna hold God creates all actions, so reward is by grace and punishment by justice, neither being obligatory upon God.

Key Terms
فضل محض
عدل محض
إثابة
تعذيب
مذهب أهل السنة
المعتزلة
الحكماء
إيجاب
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believe that God is obligated to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient because humans create their own actions, thus earning reward or punishment by right.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm that God creates all actions, including human actions, and therefore reward is purely by His grace and punishment by His justice, neither being a necessary obligation upon Him.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which group claims that reward becomes obligatory (وجوب) upon God?
A) The philosophers
B) The Mu'tazilites
C) Ahl al-Sunna
D) The Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, divine reward is pure grace and divine punishment is pure justice—neither is obligatory upon God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your every act of obedience, even if multiplied, cannot repay even one of God's blessings, would you still feel entitled to Paradise? What does your heart say when you fall short—does it blame God for being unfair, or does it realize that any reward is pure grace? This week, whenever you perform an act of worship, pause and say: 'I do this not because I am earning something, but because He deserves my gratitude.' Will you commit to that?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought God must punish every sin, but Ahl al-Sunna say punishment is pure justice—God punishes or forgives as He wills. When you see a wrongdoer seemingly getting away, does envy or anger arise in your heart? Are you demanding that God act according to your sense of fairness? Repent from that today and trust that God's justice is perfect, even if hidden. Will you let go of that grudge right now?
?
Your salvation depends entirely on God's pure grace, not on your deeds. Does that humble you or make you uneasy? Do you still try to earn His favor by your own efforts, as if you could obligate Him? This lesson forces you to abandon all self-reliance and cling to His mercy alone. Starting tonight, will you change your prayer from 'I deserve this' to 'I beg You for Your grace'?

4 min
Nothing is Obligatory Upon God
Sanusi denies any obligation upon God and explains the meaning of divine choice, reconciling apparent scriptural obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nothing is obligatory upon God, and how the Ash'ari tradition reconciles verses that appear to impose obligations with God's absolute freedom of choice.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by external obligation; He acts by free choice, and all promises are from His grace.

This lesson explores a core Ash'ari doctrine: that God is not subject to any obligation, whether of action or omission. The author explains why this follows from God's nature as a free agent, and then addresses scriptural verses that seem to say otherwise, showing they are really promises of grace. By the end, you will see how this preserves God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key claim: 'There is no obligation upon Him.'
Pay attention to the logical structure: claim, proof, then reconciliation of objections.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author provides a clear logical argument: if something were obligatory, God would not be a chooser. Grasp why obligation and free choice are incompatible.
3
Examine the scriptural reconciliation
The author addresses verses like Surah Hud 6 that appear to impose obligation, explaining they are promises of grace. Reflect on how this preserves divine freedom.
Also note the reference to an earlier discussion on iṭā'ah (compliance) which ties this to broader themes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ) أي : ليس عليه تعالى واجبٌ مِنْ فعل أو ترك؛ لأنَّه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار، ولو وجب عليه فعل أو ترك لما كان مختاراً؛ لأنَّ المختار هو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ،ترك، وأما الآيات الدالة على الوجوب عليه تعالى نحو : ﴿وَمَا دابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللَّهِ رِزْقُهَا) [مُود : (٦) فمحمولةٌ على أن المراد بها الوعد تفضلاً، وكذلك الأحاديث الدالة على ذلك، وتقدَّم الكلام في نظيره من الإيطاء (٢)، فلا تغفل.
His statement: 'There is no obligation upon Him' means: There is no obligation upon God Almighty, whether of action or omission, because He acts by choice. If an action or omission were obligatory upon Him, He would not be a chooser, for the chooser is one who, if He wills, He acts, and if He wills, He refrains. As for the verses that indicate obligation upon Him, such as {And there is no creature on earth but its sustenance is from Allah} [Hud: 6], they are interpreted to mean that the intended sense is a promise of grace. The same applies to the hadiths indicating that. And the discussion of its like has preceded in the section on iṭā'ah (2), so do not be heedless.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a proof. It defines the poem's line 'mā ʿalayhi wājib' as meaning that nothing is obligatory upon God. The reason is that God is a free agent (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār). If something were obligatory, He would not be free. The author then addresses the apparent conflict with verses that seem to impose obligations on God, like Surah Hud 6, explaining that these are promises of grace (tafaḍḍul), not obligations. He refers to an earlier discussion on iṭā'ah (probably meaning following the divine command).

Key Terms
واجب
فاعل بالاختيار
المختار
الآيات
الوعد تفضلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is arbitrary; because nothing is obligatory upon Him, He could be unjust or break promises.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God is a chooser who acts according to wisdom. His promises are from grace, not obligation, and He fulfills them out of generosity and truthfulness, not compulsion.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain verses such as 'And there is no creature on earth but its sustenance is from Allah' that appear to impose an obligation on God?
A) They are abrogated
B) They are metaphorical
C) They are promises of grace (tafaḍḍul)
D) They are literal obligations

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to perform or omit any action because He is the free chooser; any scriptural language of obligation is understood as a promise of grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not obligated to give you anything, and all His promises are from grace, does your dua feel more like a beggar's plea or a demand? How will you change the way you ask after this lesson?
?
Can you recall a blessing you felt you 'deserved' because of your effort? How does knowing that even your sustenance is a promise of grace, not an obligation, shift your gratitude? Will you thank Him more humbly now?
?
What would change in your heart if you truly lived knowing that God owes you nothing and everything you have is a free gift? Would you still obey Him if you knew your deeds earned nothing? What is your real motivation for worship?

The Hadith on Good and Evil and the Mu'tazila Refutation
Interpretation of hadith and refutation of Mu'tazila by logical consequence and textual proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, and evil is not attributed to You' is interpreted by Ahl al-Sunna to affirm that all things occur by God's will, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Evil is not a means to draw near to God, but it still occurs by His will.

This passage addresses a potential objection from the Mu'tazila who might use a well-known hadith to argue that evil is not willed by God. The author clarifies the correct interpretation of the hadith and then exposes a logical flaw in the Mu'tazila view. He also provides a decisive proof from another hadith. By the end, you will see how the Sunni position remains consistent with both scripture and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice the two hadiths cited and the logical argument.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the consequence argument (lazim).
2
Understand the interpretation of the first hadith
The author explains that 'evil is not attributed to You' means evil is not a means of drawing near to God, not that it is outside His will. This is key.
3
Analyze the consequence for Mu'tazila
If evil is not willed by God, then most of what happens in His dominion (since evils are more numerous) would be outside His will, which is absurd. See how the author draws this implication.
4
Consider the second hadith as proof
The hadith 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not' directly refutes the Mu'tazila position. Reflect on how this settles the matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرد على مذهب أهل السنة حديث : «الخيرُ بيديكَ، والشرُّ ليسَ إِلَيْكَ» (١) ، لأن معناه الخير بقدرتك وإرادتك، والشرُّ لا يُتقرَّبُ به إليك. ويلزم على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة : أنَّ أكثر ما يقع في ملكه تعالى غيرُ مرادٍ له ؛ لأنَّ الشرور أكثر من الخيرات ویرده قوله : «ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن» (۲)
And the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, and evil is not attributed to You' does not refute the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna, because its meaning is: good is by Your power and will, and evil is not a means of drawing near to You. And it follows from what the Mu'tazila hold that most of what occurs in His dominion is not willed by Him, because evils are more than goods. And that is refuted by His saying: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not.'
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a potential objection from the Mu'tazila and also shows an internal refutation of the Mu'tazila position. The author first addresses a hadith that might seem to contradict the Ash'ari position that all things occur by God's will. The hadith states that good is in God's hands but evil is not attributed to Him. The author explains that this means good occurs by God's power and will, while evil is not a means of drawing near to God—not that evil occurs without His will. Then the author points out a consequence of the Mu'tazila view: if they deny that evil is willed by God, then most of what happens in God's kingdom (since evils are more numerous than goods) is not willed by God, which is absurd. This consequence is refuted by the general principle found in another hadith: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not,' which affirms that everything that happens is by God's will. Thus, the Mu'tazila position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الخير بيديك
الشر ليس إليك
المعتزلة
ما شاء الله كان
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Good is in Your hands...' means that evil is not willed by God at all.
Correction: The author explains that the hadith refers to moral and relational attribution: evil is not a means of drawing near to God, but it still occurs by God's will.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the phrase 'evil is not attributed to You' in the hadith?
A) It means evil is not willed by God.
B) It means evil is not a means of drawing near to God.
C) It means the hadith is weak and not used as evidence.
D) It means God does not create evil.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Good is in Your hands, and evil is not attributed to You' does not deny that evil occurs by God's will; rather, it denies that evil is a means of drawing near to Him, and the Mu'tazila position is refuted by both logical consequence and another hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or witness evil in the world, do you truly believe it is from Allah's will? How does this lesson change the way you respond to such events—do you submit to His decree or question it? Ask Allah to grant you acceptance and to make you among those who seek nearness to Him through good deeds, not evil.
?
The hadith says evil is not a means of drawing near to Allah. Are there sins or forbidden desires you have used as a means to 'get close' to something you thought was good? How can you redirect your intentions to seek nearness to Allah only through what He loves?
?
If you were to fully internalize that everything that happens is by Allah's will—both good and evil—how would your fear, hope, and trust in Him be transformed? What one change can you make today to live by this belief more sincerely?

Qur'anic Evidence for Divine Vision
Sanusi presents three Qur'anic verses as proof for ru'ya and refutes a Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an proves that believers will see Allah in the afterlife through three specific verses.

Core Idea
The Qur'an affirms divine vision; Mu'tazila reinterpretations fail.

The author begins by establishing the sources of proof for ru'ya. The first and foremost is the Qur'an, which contains clear verses indicating that the righteous will look upon their Lord. In this passage, three key verses are presented, along with a refutation of the Mu'tazila scholar al-Jubba'i who misinterpreted the first verse. Understanding these verses is essential for grounding the belief in divine vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three verses
Carefully read the Arabic passage and locate the three Qur'anic verses cited. Notice how the author introduces each one.
The verses are from Surah al-Qiyama, Surah Yunus, and Surah al-Mutaffifin.
2
Understand the author's explanation of verse 75:22-23
The author explains the grammar and refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation. Note how the predicate 'nāẓira' indicates looking, not waiting.
Al-Jubba'i changed 'ilā' to a noun meaning 'favor' to avoid the implication of vision.
3
Reflect on the consensus of exegetes on verse 10:26
The majority interpret 'al-ziyada' (increase) as looking at Allah's face. This shows the strong tradition of understanding ru'ya from this verse.
4
Note the simplicity of verse 83:23
This verse explicitly states 'looking' without need for interpretation, reinforcing the proof.
5
Consider the overall Qur'anic proof
The three verses together provide a cumulative argument for ru'ya, each adding clarity and refuting alternative readings.
The author's systematic refutation of al-Jubba'i shows the importance of defending the apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات
موجود يصح أن يُرى) ، فالباري عزَّ وجلَّ يصح أن يُرى، لكن لم تقع دنيا لغير نبينا ) ، وواجبةٌ شرعاً في الآخرة، كما أطبق عليه أهل السنة؛ للكتاب والسنة والإجماع:
أما الكتاب فآيات كثيرة:
منها قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]، ومعنى (ناضرة) حسنة، وهو صفةٌ للوجوه، وهو المسوغ للابتداء به، و(ناظرة) خبره. وحمل الجبائي النظر في الآية على الانتظار، وجعل (إلى) اسماً بمعنى النعمة، والمعنى عنده: منتظرة نعمة ربِّها.
ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ﴾ [يونس: 26]؛ فَإِنَّ الْحُسْنَى: هِيَ الْجَنَّةُ، وَالزِّيَادَةُ هِيَ النَّظَرُ لِوَجْهِهِ الْكَرِيمِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ جُمْهُورُ الْمُفَسِّرِينَ.
ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿عَلَى الْأَرَائِكِ يَنظُرُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 23].
وأما السنَّةُ فأحاديث كحديث: إنَّكم سترون ربّكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر، والتشبيه للرؤية في عدم الشكّ والخفاء، لا للمرئي كما قد يتوهم، والتعبير بالسين في الحديث؛ لأنَّ القيامة قد قَرُبت وأَوَّلَ المعتزلة الحديث بأنَّ المعنى: سترون رحمة ربكم.
وأما الإجماع فهو أنَّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرؤية في الآخرة، قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداؤه فلم يروه تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنون ربهم يوم القيامة لم يُعَيَّر الكافرون بالحجاب، قال تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ مَّحْجُوبُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 15].
وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه: لمَّا حَجب قوماً بالسُّخط، دلَّ على أن قوماً يرونه بالرضا. ثم قال: (أما والله لو لم يوقن محمد بن إدريس بأنه يرى ربَّه في الميعاد لما عبده في الدنيا، وهذا من كلام المدلّلين نفعنا الله بهم، وإلا فالله يستحق العبادة لذاته). وقال ابن العربي: (إنَّ رؤية الله جعلت تقوية للمعرفة الحاصلة في الدنيا، فما راء كمن سمعا).
The divine matters. [It is] an existent that is valid to be seen, so the Creator, mighty and majestic, is valid to be seen, but it did not occur in this world for other than our Prophet, and it is legally obligatory in the afterlife, as the people of the Sunnah have agreed upon, based on the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book, there are many verses: among them His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The meaning of 'radiant' is beautiful, and it is an adjective for the faces, which is what permits it to be the subject, and 'looking' is its predicate. Al-Jubba'i interpreted 'looking' in the verse as 'waiting', and made 'ila' a noun meaning 'favor', and his interpretation is: waiting for the favor of their Lord. Among them is His saying, exalted: 'For those who have done good is the best (reward) and more' (10:26). The best (reward) is Paradise, and the 'more' is looking at His noble face, as the majority of exegetes said. Among them is His saying, exalted: 'On couches, looking' (83:23). As for the Sunnah, there are hadiths such as the hadith: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' The similitude is for the vision in terms of absence of doubt and hiddenness, not for the seen object as might be imagined. The expression with 'sīn' in the hadith is because the resurrection has drawn near. The Mu'tazila interpreted the hadith as meaning: you will see the mercy of your Lord. As for consensus, the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, were united upon the occurrence of vision in the afterlife. Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled His enemies so they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. If the believers were not to see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached for the veil. Allah said: 'No indeed, on that Day they will be veiled from their Lord' (83:15). Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled a people with displeasure, it indicates that a people see Him with pleasure. Then he said: 'By Allah, if Muhammad ibn Idris were not certain that he would see his Lord at the appointed time, he would not have worshipped Him in this world. This is among the speech of those who are convinced, may Allah benefit us by them, and otherwise Allah is deserving of worship by His essence.' Ibn al-Arabi said: 'Indeed, vision of Allah is made to strengthen the knowledge attained in this world, for the one who sees is not like the one who hears.'
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrinal proofs for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife, which is a core belief of Ahl al-Sunna. It begins by stating that Allah is an existent that can be seen, and that this vision is possible in this world only for our Prophet (peace be upon him) and is legally obligatory in the afterlife, based on three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and consensus. The author first cites Qur'anic verses. Verse 75:22-23 describes faces looking at their Lord. The author clarifies the grammar: 'nādira' (radiant) is the subject and 'nāẓira' (looking) is the predicate. He then refutes the Mu'tazila scholar al-Jubba'i, who interpreted 'nazara' as 'waiting' and 'ilā' as 'favor', thus denying vision. The second verse (10:26) mentions 'ziyāda' (increase), which the majority of exegetes interpret as looking at Allah's face. The third verse (83:23) describes the righteous looking from couches. For the Sunnah, the author cites the famous hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the moon. He emphasizes that the similitude is about clarity and certainty, not about Allah being like the moon. The use of 'sa- (future) indicates the nearness of the Hour. The Mu'tazila reinterpreted the hadith as seeing mercy. For consensus (ijmā'), the author reports that the Companions were unanimous on ru'ya in the afterlife. He supports this with quotes from Imam Malik (who argues from the contrast with the disbelievers' veil in 83:15) and Imam al-Shafi'i, who states that certainty of seeing Allah in the afterlife motivated his worship. Ibn al-Arabi adds that ru'ya strengthens knowledge obtained in this world, comparing seeing to hearing. This section firmly establishes the doctrine using authoritative sources and refutes Mu'tazila interpretations.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
النظر (al-nazar)
الزيادة (al-ziyada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' means waiting for a favor, not actual vision.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i's interpretation is refuted by the author by clarifying the grammar: 'nāẓira' is a predicate and implies looking, not waiting.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'al-ziyada' (increase) in verse 10:26 according to the majority of exegetes?
A) An increase in wealth
B) Looking at Allah's face
C) More rewards in Paradise
D) A longer life

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an provides explicit proof that believers will see their Lord in the afterlife, and Mu'tazila reinterpretations are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord,' do you truly believe that you will see Allah with your own eyes? Or does some part of you rationalize it away, like the Mu'tazila did? Search your heart for any hidden doubt and ask yourself: where does that doubt come from, and what would it take to uproot it? Commit today to seeking certainty on this matter.
?
If the 'more' in 'For those who have done good is the best and more' is indeed the vision of Allah, then what are you doing right now to ensure that you will receive that 'more'? Is your longing for Allah's vision reflected in your daily choices, or do you live as if material pleasures are enough? Name one concrete change you will make this week to increase your hope of seeing Him.
?
The passage points out that seeing Allah is legally obligatory in the afterlife—meaning it is a necessary part of true faith. Does your belief in ru'ya feel like a distant theological concept, or does it stir in you a deep ache to behold the Face of your Lord? If it is the former, how can you turn that lifeless knowledge into a burning desire? Write a short prayer asking Allah to grant you the vision and to purify your intention.

Sunnah Evidence for Divine Vision
Sanusi cites the prophetic hadith on ru'ya and clarifies the similitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic hadith proving divine vision and how to correctly understand its similitude.

Core Idea
You will see your Lord as clearly as the full moon.

After the Qur'an, the author turns to the Sunnah, citing the well-known hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the moon. He clarifies that the similarity is in clarity, not in the nature of the seen, and refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation that it refers to seeing mercy. This hadith is a cornerstone of the Sunni proof for ru'ya.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the author's commentary
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The hadith is cited and then explained by the author.
The hadith is: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.'
2
Understand why the similitude is about clarity
The author states that the comparison is in terms of absence of doubt and hiddenness, not for the seen object. This refutes any anthropomorphic misunderstanding.
3
Note the Mu'tazila reinterpretation and why it fails
The Mu'tazila interpreted the hadith as meaning you will see Allah's mercy. The author rejects this by implying the literal meaning is retained.
The use of 'sa-' (future) indicates the nearness of the resurrection, not a different object of vision.
4
Reflect on the significance of this hadith
This hadith is a clear proof that believers will see Allah, and its interpretation must preserve the doctrine of ru'ya.

Source Text

موجود يصح أن يُرى) ، فالباري عزَّ وجلَّ يصح أن يُرى، لكن لم تقع دنيا لغير نبينا ) ، وواجبةٌ شرعاً في الآخرة، كما أطبق عليه أهل السنة؛ للكتاب والسنة والإجماع:

منها قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]، ومعنى (ناضرة) حسنة، وهو صفةٌ للوجوه، وهو المسوغ للابتداء به، و(ناظرة) خبره. وحمل الجبائي النظر في الآية على الانتظار، وجعل (إلى) اسماً بمعنى النعمة، والمعنى عنده: منتظرة نعمة ربِّها.
ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ﴾ [يونس: 26]؛ فَإِنَّ الْحُسْنَى: هِيَ الْجَنَّةُ، وَالزِّيَادَةُ هِيَ النَّظَرُ لِوَجْهِهِ الْكَرِيمِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ جُمْهُورُ الْمُفَسِّرِينَ.
ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿عَلَى الْأَرَائِكِ يَنظُرُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 23].
وأما السنَّةُ فأحاديث كحديث: إنَّكم سترون ربّكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر، والتشبيه للرؤية في عدم الشكّ والخفاء، لا للمرئي كما قد يتوهم، والتعبير بالسين في الحديث؛ لأنَّ القيامة قد قَرُبت وأَوَّلَ المعتزلة الحديث بأنَّ المعنى: سترون رحمة ربكم.
وأما الإجماع فهو أنَّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرؤية في الآخرة، قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداؤه فلم يروه تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنون ربهم يوم القيامة لم يُعَيَّر الكافرون بالحجاب، قال تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ مَّحْجُوبُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 15].
وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه: لمَّا حَجب قوماً بالسُّخط، دلَّ على أن قوماً يرونه بالرضا. ثم قال: (أما والله لو لم يوقن محمد بن إدريس بأنه يرى ربَّه في الميعاد لما عبده في الدنيا، وهذا من كلام المدلّلين نفعنا الله بهم، وإلا فالله يستحق العبادة لذاته). وقال ابن العربي: (إنَّ رؤية الله جعلت تقوية للمعرفة الحاصلة في الدنيا، فما راء كمن سمعا).
The divine matters. [It is] an existent that is valid to be seen, so the Creator, mighty and majestic, is valid to be seen, but it did not occur in this world for other than our Prophet, and it is legally obligatory in the afterlife, as the people of the Sunnah have agreed upon, based on the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book, there are many verses: among them His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The meaning of 'radiant' is beautiful, and it is an adjective for the faces, which is what permits it to be the subject, and 'looking' is its predicate. Al-Jubba'i interpreted 'looking' in the verse as 'waiting', and made 'ila' a noun meaning 'favor', and his interpretation is: waiting for the favor of their Lord. Among them is His saying, exalted: 'For those who have done good is the best (reward) and more' (10:26). The best (reward) is Paradise, and the 'more' is looking at His noble face, as the majority of exegetes said. Among them is His saying, exalted: 'On couches, looking' (83:23). As for the Sunnah, there are hadiths such as the hadith: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' The similitude is for the vision in terms of absence of doubt and hiddenness, not for the seen object as might be imagined. The expression with 'sīn' in the hadith is because the resurrection has drawn near. The Mu'tazila interpreted the hadith as meaning: you will see the mercy of your Lord. As for consensus, the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, were united upon the occurrence of vision in the afterlife. Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled His enemies so they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. If the believers were not to see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached for the veil. Allah said: 'No indeed, on that Day they will be veiled from their Lord' (83:15). Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled a people with displeasure, it indicates that a people see Him with pleasure. Then he said: 'By Allah, if Muhammad ibn Idris were not certain that he would see his Lord at the appointed time, he would not have worshipped Him in this world. This is among the speech of those who are convinced, may Allah benefit us by them, and otherwise Allah is deserving of worship by His essence.' Ibn al-Arabi said: 'Indeed, vision of Allah is made to strengthen the knowledge attained in this world, for the one who sees is not like the one who hears.'
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrinal proofs for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife, which is a core belief of Ahl al-Sunna. It begins by stating that Allah is an existent that can be seen, and that this vision is possible in this world only for our Prophet (peace be upon him) and is legally obligatory in the afterlife, based on three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and consensus. The author first cites Qur'anic verses. Verse 75:22-23 describes faces looking at their Lord. The author clarifies the grammar: 'nādira' (radiant) is the subject and 'nāẓira' (looking) is the predicate. He then refutes the Mu'tazila scholar al-Jubba'i, who interpreted 'nazara' as 'waiting' and 'ilā' as 'favor', thus denying vision. The second verse (10:26) mentions 'ziyāda' (increase), which the majority of exegetes interpret as looking at Allah's face. The third verse (83:23) describes the righteous looking from couches. For the Sunnah, the author cites the famous hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the moon. He emphasizes that the similitude is about clarity and certainty, not about Allah being like the moon. The use of 'sa- (future) indicates the nearness of the Hour. The Mu'tazila reinterpreted the hadith as seeing mercy. For consensus (ijmā'), the author reports that the Companions were unanimous on ru'ya in the afterlife. He supports this with quotes from Imam Malik (who argues from the contrast with the disbelievers' veil in 83:15) and Imam al-Shafi'i, who states that certainty of seeing Allah in the afterlife motivated his worship. Ibn al-Arabi adds that ru'ya strengthens knowledge obtained in this world, comparing seeing to hearing. This section firmly establishes the doctrine using authoritative sources and refutes Mu'tazila interpretations.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
التشبيه (al-tashbih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith implies Allah is like the moon, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: The author explains that the similitude is for the vision in terms of clarity and lack of doubt, not for the object seen.

Checkpoint
What does the 'sīn' (future tense) in the hadith indicate according to the author?
A) That the vision will happen soon
B) That the vision is uncertain
C) That the vision is metaphorical
D) That the vision is only for the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The hadith confirms that believers will see Allah with absolute clarity, and any anthropomorphic interpretation is incorrect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate standing before Allah on the Day of Resurrection to see Him as clearly as the full moon, does your heart tremble with longing or with fear? What specific habits in your life today are preparing you for that moment of vision?
?
Sanusi warns against the Mu'tazila's distortion of the hadith—have you ever been tempted to reinterpret clear truths of Islam to avoid discomfort or intellectual scrutiny? What would it cost you to accept this vision without qualification?
?
If you truly believed that you will see Allah with absolute clarity, how would it change the way you speak about Him, pray to Him, and yearn for Him right now? Are you living as if that vision is real?

Consensus (Ijma') on Divine Vision
Sanusi presents the consensus of the Companions and quotes three scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the Companions and early scholars supports the belief in seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The consensus of the righteous predecessors seals the proof.

The author completes the threefold proof with consensus. He quotes Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, who argue from the contrast with the disbelievers' veil, and Ibn al-Arabi, who states that vision strengthens faith. This consensus demonstrates that ru'ya was an undisputed belief among the early Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the statements of the three scholars
Carefully read the Arabic passage. The author presents statements from Imam Malik, Imam al-Shafi'i, and Ibn al-Arabi.
Each scholar provides a different angle to support ru'ya.
2
Understand Imam Malik's argument from the veil
Imam Malik argues that if believers did not see Allah, disbelievers would not be reproached for being veiled. This is a logical inference from the Qur'anic verse (83:15).
3
Appreciate al-Shafi'i's personal conviction
Al-Shafi'i asserts that without certainty of seeing Allah, he would not worship Him. This shows the centrality of ru'ya to faith.
He also notes that Allah is still worthy of worship by His essence, but the hope of vision motivates.
4
Reflect on Ibn al-Arabi's comparison
Ibn al-Arabi says that vision strengthens knowledge; 'the one who sees is not like the one who hears'.
This highlights the superiority of direct vision over mere reports.
5
Consider the overall consensus
The fact that these early authorities spoke with one voice proves that ru'ya was a settled doctrine from the time of the Companions.

موجود يصح أن يُرى) ، فالباري عزَّ وجلَّ يصح أن يُرى، لكن لم تقع دنيا لغير نبينا ) ، وواجبةٌ شرعاً في الآخرة، كما أطبق عليه أهل السنة؛ للكتاب والسنة والإجماع:
أما الكتاب فآيات كثيرة:
منها قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]، ومعنى (ناضرة) حسنة، وهو صفةٌ للوجوه، وهو المسوغ للابتداء به، و(ناظرة) خبره. وحمل الجبائي النظر في الآية على الانتظار، وجعل (إلى) اسماً بمعنى النعمة، والمعنى عنده: منتظرة نعمة ربِّها.

The Two Stations: Permissibility and Occurrence
Author distinguishes two stations for ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the rational possibility (jawaz) and actual occurrence (wuqu') of seeing Allah, and why the author focuses on the first.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible in principle; its actual occurrence is a separate issue.

This passage sets the stage by clarifying that there are two distinct questions about ru'ya: Is it possible? And did or will it happen? The author's primary concern here is the first question, the permissibility. Let's read how he introduces this foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two stations
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the terms 'jawaz' and 'wuqu' and how the author explicitly names them as two stations (maqamayn).
The author references al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid as the source of this distinction.
2
Understand why the first station is the focus
The author states that the context makes it clear he is addressing the first station, permissibility. Ask yourself: Why is it important to first establish that vision is possible before discussing its occurrence?
3
Reflect on the broader significance
This distinction prevents confusion between a theological possibility and historical/eschatological fact. The entire debate with the Mu'tazila hinges on this first station.
The Mu'tazila denied even the possibility of ru'ya, so the first station is the appropriate starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن هنا مقامين كما يُستفاد من كلام السعد في شرح المقاصد: أحدهما في جواز الرؤية، وثانيهما في وقوعها. والمتبادر من كلام المصنف المقام الأول كما هو قضية مرجع الضمير. قوله: (بِالأَبْصَارِ) ظاهره أن الرؤية بالحَدَقِ فقط، وهو أحد أقوال ثلاثة. ثانيها: أنها بجميع الوجوه، لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]. ثالثها: أنها بكل جزء من أجزاء البدن كما نُقل عن أبي يزيد البسطامي. قوله: (لَكِنْ بِلا كَيْف) لما كان قد يُتوهم من قوله: (ومنه أن ينظر بالأبصار) أنه تعالى يُرى بكيف كما في رؤية بعضنا بعضاً، استدرك عليه بقوله (لكن بلا كيف)؛ أي: بلا تكيف للمرئي بكيفية من كيفيات الحوادث؛ من مقابلة وجهة وتحيز وغير ذلك. وغرض المصنف بذلك الجواب عن شبهة المعتزلة العقلية التي تمسكوا بها في قولهم بإحالة الرؤية. وحاصلها: أنَّه تعالى لو كان مرتباً لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة، فيكون في جهة وحيز. وحاصل الجواب: أن قولكم: لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة ممنوع، فلزوم الجهة والحيز ممنوع إذ الرؤية قوة يجعلها الله في خلقه لا يشترط فيها مقابلة المرئي ولا كونه في جهة وحيز ولا غير ذلك، ودعوى الضرورة فيما نازع فيه الجمُّ الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموعة. غاية الأمر أنَّ هذه الأمور لازمةٌ عادةً لا عقلاً.
The conclusion is that there are two stations, as understood from the words of al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid: one concerning the permissibility of vision, and the second concerning its occurrence. What is apparent from the author's words is the first station, as is the case of the pronoun's reference. His saying: 'with the eyes' outwardly indicates that vision is only with the pupils, and this is one of three opinions. The second: that it is with all the face, due to the apparent meaning of His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The third: that it is with every part of the body, as reported from Abu Yazid al-Bistami. His saying: 'but without modality' – because it might be imagined from his saying: 'and among them is to look with the eyes' that the Exalted is seen with a modality as in the vision of some of us for others, he corrected it by saying 'but without modality'; i.e., without the seen object being characterized by any modality of the modalities of contingent things, such as facing a direction, occupying space, and so on. The author's purpose by this is to answer the rational doubt of the Mu'tazila which they clung to in their assertion of the impossibility of vision. Its summary: that if the Exalted were seen, He would necessarily face the seer, and thus be in a direction and space. The summary of the answer: that your statement: 'He would necessarily face the seer' is denied, and the necessity of direction and space is denied, for vision is a power that Allah places in His creation, not conditional upon the seen object facing nor upon it being in a direction or space or anything else. And the claim of necessity in what a great multitude of rational people have disputed is not acceptable. The most that can be said is that these things are necessary by custom, not by reason.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a theological clarification and refutation. It begins by establishing two distinct issues concerning ru'ya: (1) its permissibility (jawāz) – is it possible? and (2) its occurrence (wuqū‘) – did it happen or will it happen? The author notes that the primary focus of the text (al-Jawhara) is the first station. Then, he explains the author's phrase 'with the eyes': this literally means vision with the pupils, but three opinions exist: (i) only with the pupils; (ii) with the entire face, based on the verse; (iii) with every body part, as reported from Abu Yazid al-Bistami. The author then addresses the crucial qualification 'but without modality' (lākin bi-lā kayf). This is to prevent the misunderstanding that Allah is seen with a modality like physical beings. The vision is real but not subject to conditions like direction, location, etc. The main purpose is to refute the Mu'tazila rational argument that vision of Allah is impossible because it would require direction and spatial location (jiha and hayyiz), which are attributes of contingent beings. The answer is that the necessity of facing and location is denied; vision is a power Allah creates in the seer, not dependent on such conditions. The Mu'tazila claim of necessity is rejected because it is not a rational necessity but only a customary one (in this world). Thus, the doctrine of ru'ya without modality is preserved.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz)
وقوع (wuqu')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two stations (maqamayn) mentioned regarding ru'ya?
A) Permissibility and occurrence
B) Vision with eyes and vision with face
C) This world and the afterlife
D) Reason and revelation

Key Takeaway
The distinction between possibility and occurrence is crucial for correctly framing the theological discussion on ru'ya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your beliefs about Allah, do you often conflate what is possible with what actually happens? How might this distinction between jawaz and wuqu' protect you from making assumptions about Allah's actions based only on logical possibility? This week, when you catch yourself assuming something must happen because it seems possible, pause and ask: 'Is this a matter of possibility or actuality? And what does revelation say?'
?
The author separates the question of 'can Allah be seen?' from 'will He be seen?'. In your own life, do you separate questions of principle from questions of practice? Where might you be confusing the two, leading to wrong conclusions about your relationship with Allah? Write down one area where you need to make this distinction more carefully.
?
Studying theology ('ilm al-kalam) requires precise distinctions. Are you tempted to skip over such careful classifications as 'too technical'? How might your faith suffer if you neglect these fine-grained distinctions? Make a firm intention today to value precision in your learning, even when it seems small.

Three Opinions on How Allah is Seen
Enumeration of three opinions on the mode of vision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the three scholarly opinions on how the vision of Allah occurs: with the pupils, with the face, or with the whole body.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is real, but its precise modality is debated; three views exist from the sources.

The author's phrase 'with the eyes' might suggest vision is limited to the pupils. However, scholars have offered broader interpretations based on a Qur'anic verse and spiritual insight. Let's examine these three opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the three opinions
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify each opinion and its basis: the first is literal, the second uses a Qur'anic verse, the third comes from Abu Yazid al-Bistami.
The verse quoted is from Surah al-Qiyama, 75:22-23.
2
Note the scriptural basis for the second opinion
The second opinion interprets 'with the eyes' as 'with the face' because the Qur'an says 'faces... looking at their Lord.' This is a key proof for those who expand the mode of vision.
3
Consider the significance of the third opinion
Attributed to Abu Yazid al-Bistami, this opinion says the whole body sees. It reflects a spiritual and holistic understanding of the beatific vision.
This opinion is less common but shows the range of interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن هنا مقامين كما يُستفاد من كلام السعد في شرح المقاصد: أحدهما في جواز الرؤية، وثانيهما في وقوعها. والمتبادر من كلام المصنف المقام الأول كما هو قضية مرجع الضمير. قوله: (بِالأَبْصَارِ) ظاهره أن الرؤية بالحَدَقِ فقط، وهو أحد أقوال ثلاثة. ثانيها: أنها بجميع الوجوه، لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22-23]. ثالثها: أنها بكل جزء من أجزاء البدن كما نُقل عن أبي يزيد البسطامي. قوله: (لَكِنْ بِلا كَيْف) لما كان قد يُتوهم من قوله: (ومنه أن ينظر بالأبصار) أنه تعالى يُرى بكيف كما في رؤية بعضنا بعضاً، استدرك عليه بقوله (لكن بلا كيف)؛ أي: بلا تكيف للمرئي بكيفية من كيفيات الحوادث؛ من مقابلة وجهة وتحيز وغير ذلك. وغرض المصنف بذلك الجواب عن شبهة المعتزلة العقلية التي تمسكوا بها في قولهم بإحالة الرؤية. وحاصلها: أنَّه تعالى لو كان مرتباً لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة، فيكون في جهة وحيز. وحاصل الجواب: أن قولكم: لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة ممنوع، فلزوم الجهة والحيز ممنوع إذ الرؤية قوة يجعلها الله في خلقه لا يشترط فيها مقابلة المرئي ولا كونه في جهة وحيز ولا غير ذلك، ودعوى الضرورة فيما نازع فيه الجمُّ الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموعة. غاية الأمر أنَّ هذه الأمور لازمةٌ عادةً لا عقلاً.
The conclusion is that there are two stations, as understood from the words of al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid: one concerning the permissibility of vision, and the second concerning its occurrence. What is apparent from the author's words is the first station, as is the case of the pronoun's reference. His saying: 'with the eyes' outwardly indicates that vision is only with the pupils, and this is one of three opinions. The second: that it is with all the face, due to the apparent meaning of His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The third: that it is with every part of the body, as reported from Abu Yazid al-Bistami. His saying: 'but without modality' – because it might be imagined from his saying: 'and among them is to look with the eyes' that the Exalted is seen with a modality as in the vision of some of us for others, he corrected it by saying 'but without modality'; i.e., without the seen object being characterized by any modality of the modalities of contingent things, such as facing a direction, occupying space, and so on. The author's purpose by this is to answer the rational doubt of the Mu'tazila which they clung to in their assertion of the impossibility of vision. Its summary: that if the Exalted were seen, He would necessarily face the seer, and thus be in a direction and space. The summary of the answer: that your statement: 'He would necessarily face the seer' is denied, and the necessity of direction and space is denied, for vision is a power that Allah places in His creation, not conditional upon the seen object facing nor upon it being in a direction or space or anything else. And the claim of necessity in what a great multitude of rational people have disputed is not acceptable. The most that can be said is that these things are necessary by custom, not by reason.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a theological clarification and refutation. It begins by establishing two distinct issues concerning ru'ya: (1) its permissibility (jawāz) – is it possible? and (2) its occurrence (wuqū‘) – did it happen or will it happen? The author notes that the primary focus of the text (al-Jawhara) is the first station. Then, he explains the author's phrase 'with the eyes': this literally means vision with the pupils, but three opinions exist: (i) only with the pupils; (ii) with the entire face, based on the verse; (iii) with every body part, as reported from Abu Yazid al-Bistami. The author then addresses the crucial qualification 'but without modality' (lākin bi-lā kayf). This is to prevent the misunderstanding that Allah is seen with a modality like physical beings. The vision is real but not subject to conditions like direction, location, etc. The main purpose is to refute the Mu'tazila rational argument that vision of Allah is impossible because it would require direction and spatial location (jiha and hayyiz), which are attributes of contingent beings. The answer is that the necessity of facing and location is denied; vision is a power Allah creates in the seer, not dependent on such conditions. The Mu'tazila claim of necessity is rejected because it is not a rational necessity but only a customary one (in this world). Thus, the doctrine of ru'ya without modality is preserved.

Key Terms
بِالأَبْصَارِ (bi'l-absar)
بَلْ بِالْحَدَقِ (bal bi'l-hadaq)
وُجُوهٌ (wujuh)
نَاظِرَةٌ (nadzira)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse is cited to support the opinion that vision is with the entire face?
A) Surah al-An'am 6:103
B) Surah al-Qiyama 75:22-23
C) Surah al-A'raf 7:143
D) Surah al-Shura 42:11

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah is affirmed, but its precise modality is interpreted differently by scholars based on textual and spiritual insights.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second opinion says the whole face will be used to see Allah. Do you live now as if your entire being is oriented toward meeting Him, or do you only turn part of yourself toward God? What would change if you literally expected to see Him face-to-Face?
?
The first opinion limits vision to the eyes. If you could only see Allah with your eyes but your heart was blind to Him now, would that vision be enough? Examine your heart: have you prepared it to see Him, or are you content with just looking?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: Vision Without Modality
Refutation of Mu'tazila rational objection to ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational objection against seeing Allah and the Ash'ari response that vision is a special power not requiring direction or space.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is possible without physical constraints; it is a power He creates in the seer.

The Mu'tazila argued that seeing Allah is impossible because it would require Him to be in a direction and location—attributes impossible for the Creator. The author responds by affirming vision 'without modality' (bila kayf) and denying that such conditions are necessary. Let's unpack this crucial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection and the Ash'ari answer
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the Mu'tazila claim and the two parts of the answer: denial of necessity and the positive statement that vision is a power.
The answer distinguishes between customary necessity ('ada) and rational necessity ('aql).
2
Understand the meaning of 'bila kayf'
The qualification 'but without modality' means that the seen object (Allah) is not characterized by any modality of contingent things, such as direction or space.
This is a key Ash'ari concept to preserve divine transcendence.
3
Grasp the distinction between custom and reason
The answer states that direction and facing are required by custom ('ada) in this world, not by reason ('aql). Therefore, they are not necessary for a divinely empowered vision.
This undermines the Mu'tazila claim of rational impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila claim that seeing Allah requires Him to be in a direction?
A) They agree but say it is not a problem
B) They deny that vision requires facing, and say vision is a power from Allah not subject to customary conditions
C) They reinterpret the verses about vision
D) They say the vision is only metaphorical

The Ash'ari position is that vision of Allah is possible by a special power, unrestricted by customary conditions like direction or space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the Hereafter, do you unconsciously picture a physical form facing you? How does the teaching of 'bilā kayf' challenge you to purify that mental image and trust in a vision beyond all sensory categories? Will you actively train your mind to let go of spatial assumptions when thinking about Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected the vision of Allah because it didn't fit their rational framework. Are there any doctrines or verses of the Quran that you struggle to accept because they seem illogical to you? Can you honestly admit where your own reasoning might be limiting your faith, and are you ready to submit to revelation even when it defies your customary expectations?
?
The author describes vision as a 'power that Allah places in His creation'—a direct, unmediated gift. If you truly believed that Allah can give you an experience beyond all your sensory capacities, how would that change your longing for the Hereafter? Do you actually yearn for that vision, or has your hope become dull? What concrete step can you take today to revive that longing?
