Seeing Allah in Paradise and on the Day of Judgment
Author discusses ru'ya for awliya in Jannah and on the Day of Judgment with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two contexts in which believers see Allah in the afterlife: the continuous vision of the special ones in Paradise and the vision on the Day of Judgment, along with the evidence from hadith and the interpretive approach to 'uncovering the shin'.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is the ultimate reward, occurring for the righteous in Paradise daily and for all believers at the Resurrection.

The author now addresses the beatific vision (ru'ya) as a reward for the righteous. He first describes the special state of the awliya who see Allah continuously in Paradise, then presents the hadith evidence for the vision on the Day of Judgment, and finally explains the meaning of the Quranic expression 'uncovering the shin.' This passage combines a spiritual insight with a scriptural proof and a creedal clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the special ones
Notice the quote from Abu Yazid al-Bistami emphasizing how the awliya would complain about Paradise if veiled from Allah.
2
Understand the vision on the Day of Judgment
Follow the hadith sequence: the nations gather, the Muslims wait for their Lord, initial unrecognized manifestation, then the recognized manifestation and prostration.
3
Interpret 'uncovering the shin'
Learn that later scholars (khalaf) interpret it as removal of the veil, while the early predecessors (salaf) delegate the meaning to Allah.
4
Reflect on the creedal significance
Recognize that ru'ya is affirmed for believers, and that the hypocrite sees but does not prostrate, indicating his punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات
خواصهم كلَّ يوم بكرة وعشياً، وبعضهم لا يزال مستمرًا في الشهود حتى قال أبو يزيد البسطامي : (إِنَّ الله خواص من عباده لو حجبهم في الجنَّة عن رؤيته ساعة، لاستغاثوا الجنة ونعيمها كما يستغيثُ أهل النار من النار وعذابها). من وأما في عرصات القيامة كالموقف، فالصحيح وقوعها أيضاً؛ لأنه ورد في السنة ما يقتضي وقوعها لهم فيها، ففي الحديث : يُنادى إذا كان يوم القيامة، لتلزم كلُّ أمةٍ معبودها، فتقول هذه الأمة : هذا مكاننا حتى يأتينا ربنا، فيظهر لهم؛ أي: على الوجه الذي لا يعرفونه بأن يدخل عليهم غلطاً في كشفهم ، وإلا فهو تعالى منزه عن أن يتصفت بما لا يليقُ به، فيقول: أنا ربكم، فيقولون: نعوذُ بالله منك لست ربَّنا، فيتجلى لهم تجلّياً لائقاً بحال ذلك المقام ويكشف عن الساق ويقول : أنا ربكم، فيراه المؤمنون كما يعلمون ) أي : على وفق ما يعتقدون)، فيخرونَ سُجداً إلا المنافق (۱) انتهى. وهذا معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿يَوْمَ يُكْشَفُ عَن سَاقٍ ﴾ [القلم: (٤٢) ... الآية، وكشف الساق عند الخَلَف بمعنى رفع الحجاب، والسَّلَف يفوضون، انظر شراح البخاري».
The Divine Matters: Their special ones every morning and evening, and some of them are continuously in witnessing until Abu Yazid al-Bistami said: 'Allah has special ones among His servants that if He veiled them in Paradise from seeing Him for an hour, they would call for help from Paradise and its bliss just as the people of Hell call for help from the Fire and its torment.' And as for the courtyards of the Resurrection, such as the standing (al-mawqif), the correct view is that it occurs also; because it has come in the Sunnah what requires its occurrence for them there. For in the hadith: It will be called on the Day of Resurrection: Let every nation follow that which they worshipped. So this nation will say: This is our place until our Lord comes to us. So He will appear to them, i.e., in a way they do not recognize, so that error enters into their uncovering, otherwise He is exalted above being described with that which is not fitting for Him. So He will say: 'I am your Lord.' They will say: 'We seek refuge with Allah from you, you are not our Lord.' Then He will manifest Himself to them in a manifestation befitting the situation of that station and will uncover the shin and say: 'I am your Lord.' So the believers will see Him as they know (i.e., according to what they believe), and they will fall down in prostration except the hypocrite. End. And this is the meaning of His saying, exalted: 'The Day when the shin shall be uncovered' [Al-Qalam 68:42] and the uncovering of the shin according to the latter scholars means the removal of the veil, while the early predecessors delegated (the knowledge) to Allah. Look at the commentators of Sahih al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the occurrence of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife, both for the special servants (khawass) in Paradise and for all believers on the Day of Judgment. The author first mentions that the special ones (awliya) see Allah every morning and evening in Paradise, and some of them are continuously in a state of witnessing (shuhud). He quotes Abu Yazid al-Bistami, who says that if these special ones were veiled from seeing Allah for even an hour, they would cry out for relief from Paradise and its pleasures just as the people of Hell cry out for relief from the Fire. Then the author moves to the courtyards of the Resurrection (arasat al-qiyamah), specifically the standing (al-mawqif). He states that the correct view (al-sahih) is that ru'ya occurs there as well, because there is evidence from the Sunnah. He then cites the well-known hadith in which, on the Day of Judgment, the nations are called to follow their objects of worship. This nation (the Muslims) says that they will wait for their Lord. Then Allah appears to them initially in a form they do not recognize, and they seek refuge from Him. Then He manifests Himself (yatajalla) in a way appropriate to that station and 'uncovers the shin' (kashf 'an al-saq). At that point, the believers see Him as they know Him (i.e., according to their belief), and they prostrate, except the hypocrite. The author then explains that the verse 'The Day when the shin shall be uncovered' (Quran 68:42) by the later scholars (khalaf) is interpreted as the removal of the veil, while the early predecessors (salaf) delegated the meaning to Allah. He directs the reader to the commentators of Sahih al-Bukhari for further details.

Key Terms
رؤية
خواص
شهود
الموقف
تجلي
كشف الساق
السلف
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'uncovering of the shin' is a literal physical action of Allah.
Correction: According to the later scholars, it means the removal of the veil, and the early predecessors delegated the meaning to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the hypocrite when the believers see Allah on the Day of Judgment?
A) He sees Allah and prostrates with them.
B) He is veiled and does not see Allah.
C) He sees Allah but does not prostrate.
D) He is not present at the scene.

Key Takeaway
The righteous will see Allah in Paradise continuously, and on the Day of Judgment all believers will see Him after a dual manifestation, while the hypocrite sees but does not prostrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you long for the vision of Allah as the special ones did, who would call for help from Paradise itself if veiled for an hour? Or have your desires become so attached to worldly pleasures that the thought of seeing your Lord does not stir your heart to desperation? What would it take for you to become one of those who cannot bear even a moment without His sight?
?
On the Day when He manifests Himself in a way you do not recognize, will you be among those who say 'We seek refuge with Allah from you' and then prostrate when He reveals Himself, or will you be the hypocrite who sees but refuses to bow? What in your life right now reveals the state of your heart towards His unseen reality?
?
The early predecessors (salaf) delegated the meaning of 'uncovering of the shin' to Allah, while the later scholars interpreted it as removal of the veil. Are you content to humble yourself before the mystery of His attributes, or do you, like the present age, demand that everything be explained in terms you can mentally grasp? Where is the trust in your heart when you encounter divine unknowns?

The Prophet's Vision of Allah in this World: Affirmation and Principle
Author affirms the Prophet's vision in this world and addresses the Aisha-Ibn Abbas disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of scholars affirmed the Prophet's vision of Allah with his physical eyes during the Night Journey, and that the principle 'the affirmer outweighs the denier' resolves the disagreement between Aisha and Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
The Prophet saw Allah with his eyes; Ibn Abbas's affirmation takes precedence over Aisha's denial.

Having established that seeing Allah is rationally possible, the author now turns to its actual occurrence. He affirms that the Prophet saw his Lord during the Night Journey, and addresses a key disagreement among the companions. The passage provides a crucial principle for weighing conflicting reports.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the transition
The author moves from discussing possibility to actuality. Notice the phrase 'هذا تخلص من بحث إلى بحث آخر'.
2
Understand the majority view
The Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes (بعيني رأسه) according to most scholars, contrary to the claim that his eyesight was transferred to his heart.
3
Learn the principle of affirmer over denier
Ibn Abbas affirmed the vision, Aisha denied it. The rule that the affirmer (مثبت) is preferred over the denier (نافي) settles the matter, as stated by Ma'mar ibn Rashid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هَذَا ) أي : افهم هذا فهو مفعول لمحذوف، أو هذا كما علمت، فهو مبتدأ خبره محذوف أو نحو ذلك، فهذا تخلص من بحث إلى بحث آخر؛ لأنَّ الكلام السابق كان متعلقاً بجواز رؤيته تعالى، فانتقل عنه إلى الإخبار بوقوعها في الدنيا . قوله: (وَلِلْمُخْتَارِ دُنْيَا ثَبَتَتْ) أي: وقعت رؤيته تعالى في الدنيا ليلة الإسراء للمختار الذي هو نبينا ، وفي التعبير بالمختار مناسبة؛ لأنه اختير لهذا المقام، والراجح عند أكثر العلماء أنه رأى ربه سبحانه وتعالى بعيني رأسه وهما محلّهما، خلافاً لمن قال : حُوِّلا لقلبه ؛ لحديث ابن عباس وغيره. وقد نفت السيدة عائشة رضي الله عنها وقوعها له ، لكن قدم عليها ابن عباس لأنه مثبت، والقاعدة أنَّ المثبت مقدَّم على النافي، حتى قال معمر بن راشد: ما عائشة عندنا بأعلم من ابن عباس وكان يراه تعالى في كلِّ مرةٍ من مرات المراجعة . ومن كلام ابن وفا : إنما كان ترجيع موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في شأن الصلوات، ليتكرر مشاهدة أنوار المرات وأنشد يقول : [من البسيط] وَالسِّرُّ في قَولِ مُوسَى إِذْ يُرَاجِعُهُ لِيَجْتَلِي النُّورَ فِيهِ حَيْثُ يَشْهَدُهُ يَبْدُو سَنَاهُ عَلَى وَجْهِ الرَّسُولِ فَيَا اللَّهِ حُسْنُ رَسُولٍ إِذْ يُرَدِّدُهُ فالحكمة الباطنيَّةُ : اقتباس النور من وجهه ؛ ففي كل مرة يزداد نوراً، والحكمة الظاهرية : التخفيف. واختلف في وقوعها للأولياء على قولين للأشعري :
His saying: (هَذَا) – meaning: Understand this, for it is an omitted object, or 'this as you know', it is a subject with an omitted predicate or the like. So this is a transition from one discussion to another; because the previous speech was related to the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted, so he moves from it to informing about its occurrence in this world. His saying: (وَلِلْمُخْتَارِ دُنْيَا ثَبَتَتْ) – meaning: His vision, exalted, occurred in this world on the night of the Isra' for the Chosen One who is our Prophet. In the expression 'al-Mukhtar' there is appropriateness, because he was chosen for this station. The more preponderant view according to most scholars is that he saw his Lord, glorified and exalted, with the eyes of his head while they were in their place, contrary to those who said they were transferred to his heart, due to the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas and others. Lady 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her, denied its occurrence for him, but Ibn 'Abbas is given precedence over her because he is an affirmer, and the rule is that the affirmer is given precedence over the denier, so much so that Ma'mar ibn Rashid said: 'A'isha is not more knowledgeable than Ibn 'Abbas in our view.' And he (the Prophet) saw Him, exalted, in every one of the times of revision (of prayers). And from the speech of Ibn Wafa: 'The repetition of Moses, peace be upon him, to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, concerning the matter of the prayers was so that he might repeatedly witness the lights of the times.' And he recited the poetry: [al-Basit] 'The secret in the saying of Moses when he returned to him, to behold the light in it where he witnesses it, its brightness appears on the face of the Messenger, so by Allah, how beautiful is a Messenger who repeats it. So the inner wisdom is the acquisition of light from his face; in each time he increases in light, and the outward wisdom is lightening (the number of prayers). And there is disagreement regarding its occurrence for the saints (awliya) according to two sayings of al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage transitions from discussing the rational possibility of seeing Allah to the actual occurrence of seeing Allah in this world, specifically for the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) during the Night Journey (Isra'). The author explains the poem's line 'وَلِلْمُخْتَارِ دُنْيَا ثَبَتَتْ' (and for the Chosen One, it is affirmed in this world), meaning the vision occurred. He notes that the majority of scholars hold that the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes (bi 'ayni ra'sihi) while they were in their place, contrary to those who claim his eyesight was transferred to his heart. The author mentions that Aisha denied the occurrence of the vision, but Ibn Abbas affirmed it. The rule is that the affirmer (muthbit) is given precedence over the denier (nafi). He quotes Ma'mar ibn Rashid saying that Aisha is not more knowledgeable than Ibn Abbas. The author then discusses a wisdom behind Moses' repeated requests to the Prophet regarding the reduction of prayers: it was so that the Prophet could repeatedly witness the lights (anwar) of Allah. He cites poetry from Ibn Wafa explaining the inner wisdom: gaining light from the Prophet's face each time, increasing in light; the outward wisdom is the lightening of the prayer obligation. Finally, the author notes that there is disagreement among the Ash'aris about whether the vision occurs for saints (awliya) in this world, with two opinions of al-Ash'ari mentioned (but the passage ends before listing them).

الإسراء
المختار
بعيني رأسه
مثبت
نافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Aisha's denial of the Prophet seeing Allah is definitive and settles the matter.
Correction: The passage shows that Ibn Abbas's affirmation takes precedence because he is an affirmer, and the principle of Islamic jurisprudence gives priority to the affirmer over the denier.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Ibn Abbas given precedence over Aisha regarding the Prophet's vision of Allah?
A) Because he was older and more experienced.
B) Because he was a male companion.
C) Because he affirmed the vision (muthbit) and the affirmer outweighs the denier.
D) Because Aisha was not present at the Isra'.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes during the Isra', and the principle that the affirmer outweighs the denier supports Ibn Abbas's testimony over Aisha's denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter conflicting claims about Allah's attributes, do you tend to affirm what is reported or deny what you cannot grasp? Which tendency is stronger in you, and why? What would it take for you to give precedence to affirmation over denial in matters of the unseen?
?
The Prophet was honored with seeing Allah in this world. Do you truly long to see Allah in the hereafter? If so, what are you doing today to purify your heart and increase your yearning for that vision? If not, what is holding you back from desiring the highest reward?
?
Ibn Abbas affirmed what Aisha denied, and the scholars gave him precedence. In your own life, when someone more knowledgeable affirms something that contradicts your assumptions, do you humbly accept or stubbornly resist? How can you cultivate the humility to prefer the testimony of those who affirm, even when it challenges your comfort zone?

The Inner Wisdom of Moses' Repetitions during the Isra'
Author explains the wisdom behind Moses' repeated returns to the Prophet during the Isra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inner and outer wisdom behind Moses' repeated returns to the Prophet during the Isra', as explained by Ibn Wafa.

Core Idea
Moses' repetitions were to allow the Prophet to repeatedly witness divine lights.

After affirming the vision, the author delves into a beautiful explanation of why Moses kept returning to the Prophet during the negotiation of prayers. Ibn Wafa reveals a profound spiritual wisdom: each return enabled the Prophet to gain more divine light. This lesson explores that inner wisdom and its outward benefit.

Guided Path
1
Connect to the previous discussion
The vision of Allah was repeated during the revisions of prayers. The author quotes Ibn Wafa's explanation of Moses' repetitions.
2
Understand the inner wisdom
The repetition allowed the Prophet to repeatedly witness the lights (أنوار) of Allah, increasing his spiritual luminosity with each return.
The poetry by Ibn Wafa illustrates this: 'The secret in the saying of Moses… to behold the light…'
3
Also the outward wisdom
The outward benefit was the reduction of the number of prayers from fifty to five, lightening the obligation for the community.

What was the inner wisdom behind Moses' repeated returns to the Prophet according to Ibn Wafa?
A) To argue about the number of prayers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the repeated returns of Moses were opportunities for the Prophet to increase in divine light. In your own life, are you treating your repeated acts of worship—like daily prayers—as mere routines, or as chances to draw nearer to Allah's light? What would change if you approached each salah as a fresh opportunity to witness more of His manifestation?
?
Moses was not bargaining; he was facilitating the Prophet's spiritual ascent through the repetition. Do you see the tests and trials that come back to you repeatedly as burdens to be rid of, or as divine setups for you to increase in insight and closeness to Allah? How can you shift your perspective starting today?
?
The passage reveals an inner wisdom hidden behind an outer form—the reduction of prayers was the visible result, but the real gain was increased light. Are you more focused on the outward outcomes of your worship (e.g., finishing quickly, ticking boxes) or the inner transformation it should produce? What is one practical step you can take to realign your intention toward the inner wisdom?

Divine Attributes from Self-Sufficiency
Sanusi deduces six necessary attributes from divine self-sufficiency, then adds hearing, sight, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical connection between God's self-sufficiency (istighna') and the necessary divine attributes derived from the first testimony (shahada).

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency logically entails six essential attributes, including hearing, sight, and speech.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that the first testimony of faith—'La ilaha illa Allah'—implies more than just the negation of other deities. It necessitates a set of positive attributes that define God's essence. Building on the concept of divine self-sufficiency, the text deduces six attributes and then includes the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech as essential concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical link from shahada to istighna'
The previous discussion established that the shahada implies God's independence of anything else (istighna'). This step sets the foundation for the attributes.
The author says 'if you know that' referring to prior knowledge.
2
Identify the six necessary attributes
Read the list: wajib al-wujud (necessary existence), qidam (pre-eternity), baqa' (everlastingness), mukhalafat lil-hawadith (unlike events), qiyam bi-nafsihi (self-subsistence), and tanzih (transcendence above deficiencies).
Each attribute flows logically from istighna'.
3
Recognize that hearing, sight, and speech are included
The author says these are 'included in that' (يدخل في ذلك). Understand that these three attributes are subsumed under transcendence from deficiencies.
Their concomitants (He, Seeing, Speaking) are also affirmed.
4
Note the footnote about the hadith and idafa
The footnote cites Bukhari and Muslim and discusses the grammatical relationship in the phrase. This is a secondary point; focus first on the main attributes.
The idafa discussion is about whether utterance is a condition for worldly rulings, faith, or correctness of faith.
5
Reflect on the completeness of this list
Consider how these attributes logically safeguard God's uniqueness. This lesson establishes the conceptual chain from self-sufficiency to essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الاستغناء يستلزم وجوب وجوده، وقدمه، وبقائه، ومخالفته للحوادث وقيامه بنفسه وتنزهِهِ عن النقائص، ويدخل في ذلك السمع، والبصر والكلام ولوازمها وهي كونه سميعاً، وبصيراً، ومتكلماً، بناء على القول (1) أخرجه البخاري (۸)، ومسلم (۱۹). (۲) من إضافة الدال للمدلول، إن قلنا : النطق شرط لإجراء الأحكام الدنيوية. ومن إضافة الجزء للكل، إن قلنا : النطق شرط من الإيمان . ومن إضافة السبب للمسبب إن قلنا : النطق شرط لصحة الإيمان . انظر المزيد على إتحاف المريد للسحيمي ج ١ (٥٢١ /ب).
So if you know that, then know that self-sufficiency necessitates the necessity of His existence, His pre-eternity, His everlastingness, His being unlike events, His self-subsistence, and His transcendence above deficiencies. And included in that are hearing, sight, and speech, and their necessary concomitants, which are His being Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, based on the statement (1) Reported by al-Bukhari (8) and Muslim (19). (2) From adding the indicator to the indicated, if we say: utterance is a condition for applying worldly rulings. And from adding the part to the whole, if we say: utterance is a condition of faith. And from adding the cause to the effect, if we say: utterance is a condition for the correctness of faith. See more in It'haf al-Murid by al-Sahimi, vol. 1 (521/b).
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a deductive proof. It begins by drawing a logical conclusion from the previous discussion: if you understand that the shahada implies God's self-sufficiency (istighna'), then know that self-sufficiency entails a series of necessary divine attributes. The attributes are: 1) Wajib al-wujud (necessary existence), 2) Qidam (pre-eternity), 3) Baqa' (everlastingness), 4) Mukhalafatuhu lil-hawadith (being unlike events/creation), 5) Qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi (self-subsistence), and 6) Tanzzuhuhu 'an al-naqa'is (transcendence above deficiencies). The passage then adds that included in these are the attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and speech (kalam), and their concomitants: Hearing (Sami'), Seeing (Basir), and Speaking (Mutakallim). This is based on a statement (likely the earlier discussion). The passage concludes with a footnote that cites the hadith from al-Bukhari and Muslim, and another footnote that discusses the type of idafa (construct) in the phrase 'شهادنا الإسلام' depending on whether utterance is a condition for worldly rulings, for faith itself, or for the correctness of faith. This footnote is a grammatical and theological elaboration.

التنزه عن النقائص
السمع

سميع
بصير
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the shahada only negates other gods, not realizing it implies all divine attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that the shahada implies God's self-sufficiency, which in turn necessitates a set of positive attributes including necessary existence, pre-eternity, etc., and even hearing, sight, and speech.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which attribute is NOT directly listed as entailed by self-sufficiency?
A) Necessity of existence
B) Pre-eternity
C) Omnipotence (qudra)
D) Transcendence above deficiencies

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency (istighna') logically entails that He is necessarily existent, pre-eternal, everlasting, unlike creation, self-subsisting, and transcendent, including His hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the attributes derived from self-sufficiency. Which of these attributes makes you most aware of your own dependence on God, and how does that change your attitude in worship today?
?
God is unlike events (mukhalafa lil-hawadith). In what area of your life are you still expecting God to behave like a created being, and how can you realign your expectation with this truth?
?
The passage says God's hearing, sight, and speech are included in His transcendence above deficiencies. When you sin, do you truly believe that God hears and sees you? How would your actions change if you lived with constant awareness of His seeing and hearing?

Divine Attributes

Self-Sufficiency and the Necessary Attributes of Allah
Derivation of 23 beliefs from self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of self-sufficiency (الاستغناء) logically entails eleven necessary attributes of Allah, their impossible opposites, and one possible act, forming twenty-three beliefs.

Core Idea
Allah's self-sufficiency necessitates His attributes and freedom from all needs.

The first testimony logically implies that Allah is self-sufficient and independent of all creation. From this self-sufficiency, scholars derive a set of necessary attributes without which Allah would be in need of others. This lesson explores those attributes and the logical structure of their necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the author's logical flow from self-sufficiency to specific attributes.
The passage uses a conditional proof: if these attributes were not necessary, Allah would be in need.
2
Identify the necessary attributes
From the passage, list the eleven necessary attributes that self-sufficiency entails. They include existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, etc.
The attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are included based on the doctrine of states.
3
Understand the opposites and the possible
Recognize that each necessary attribute has an impossible opposite, and there is one possible belief: that Allah is not compelled to do or refrain from any act.
This gives 11 necessary, 11 impossible, and 1 possible = 23 beliefs.
4
Ask why this matters
Reflect on how these attributes are not arbitrary but are logical entailments of the meaning of 'self-sufficient'.
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الاستغناء يستلزم وجوب وجوده، وقدمه، وبقائه، ومخالفته للحوادث وقيامه بنفسه وتنزهِهِ عن النقائص، ويدخل في ذلك السمع، والبصر والكلام ولوازمها وهي كونه سميعاً، وبصيراً، ومتكلماً، بناء على القول بالأحوال إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات، لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث أو المحل أو مَنْ يدفع عنه النقائص، فهذه إحدى عشرة عقيدة من الواجبات، وإذا وجبت هذه الصفات استحالت أضدادها، فهذه إحدى عشرة عقيدة من المستحيلات. ويستلزم أيضاً نفي وجوب فعل شيء من الممكنات أو تركه، وإلا لزم افتقاره إلى فعل ذلك الشيء أو تركه ليتكمل به فهذه عقيدة الجائز، فجملة ما يستلزمه الاستغناء ثلاثة وعشرون عقيدة. وأما الافتقار فيستلزم الحياة والقدرة والإرادة والعلم ولوازمها وهي كونه حيا وقادراً ومريداً وعالماً بناء على القول بالأحوال، ويستلزم أيضاً الوحدانية، فهذه تسعة العقائد الواجبات ومتى وجبت هذه الصفات استحالت أضدادها، فهذه تسعةٌ من العقائد المستحيلات، فجملة ما استلزمه الافتقار ثمانية عشرة عقيدة، فإذا ضُمَّت للثلاثة والعشرين السابقة كان المجموع واحداً وأربعين، الواجب له تعالى منها عشرون والمستحيل عليه عشرون والجائز عليه واحدٌ، فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلى الثلاثة الراجعة له تعالى.
When you know that, then know that self-sufficiency entails the necessity of His existence, His pre-eternity, His everlastingness, His unlike the originated, His self-subsistence, and His transcendence above defects, and includes in that hearing, sight, and speech and their necessary concomitants which are His being Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, based on the doctrine of states. For if these attributes were not necessary for Him, He would be in need of the originator or the substratum or someone to repel defects from Him. So these are eleven beliefs from the necessary. And when these attributes are necessary, their opposites are impossible, so these are eleven beliefs from the impossible. And it also entails the negation of necessity of doing any possible thing or leaving it, otherwise there would follow His need for doing that thing or leaving it to be perfected by it. So this is one belief from the possible. So the total of what self-sufficiency entails is twenty-three beliefs. As for dependence, it entails life, power, will, and knowledge and their concomitants which are His being Living, Powerful, Willing, and Knowing, based on the doctrine of states, and it also entails oneness. So these are nine beliefs from the necessary. And when these attributes are necessary, their opposites are impossible, so these are nine from the impossible. So the total of what dependence entails is eighteen beliefs. When added to the previous twenty-three, the sum is forty-one: the necessary for Him, exalted, of them is twenty, the impossible for Him is twenty, and the possible for Him is one. So indeed the first sentence included the three divisions of rational judgment pertaining to Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a detailed derivation of the beliefs (العقائد) that follow necessarily from the first testimony. The author takes the two implications of the testimony—self-sufficiency (الاستغناء) and dependence (الافتقار)—and extracts from each the necessary, impossible, and possible attributes of Allah.

First, from self-sufficiency: Allah must be necessary in existence, pre-eternal, everlasting, unlike created things, self-subsisting, and transcendent. Additionally, He must have the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech (and their concomitants of being Hearing, Seeing, Speaking). The author notes this is based on the doctrine of states (الأحوال), a nuanced position in kalam regarding the ontological status of attributes. The proof is given: if these attributes were not necessary, Allah would be in need of something else to give them to Him. Thus, these eleven attributes are necessary (واجبة), and their opposites are impossible (مستحيلة), giving another eleven. Finally, from self-sufficiency also follows the negation of any necessity for Allah to do or refrain from any possible act—He is free from compulsion—which is one possible (جائز) belief. Total: 11+11+1=23 beliefs from self-sufficiency.

Second, from dependence: Allah must have life, power, will, and knowledge (and their concomitants of being Living, Powerful, Willing, Knowing), plus oneness (الوحدانية). That gives 9 necessary attributes. Their opposites are impossible, so 9 impossible. Total from dependence: 18 beliefs.

Adding 23 from self-sufficiency and 18 from dependence gives 41 beliefs: 20 necessary (11+9), 20 impossible (11+9), and 1 possible. The author concludes that the first testimony thus contains the three divisions of rational judgment (الحكم العقلي) regarding Allah.

الوجوب
القدم
البقاء
المخالفة للحوادث
القيام بالنفس
التنزيه
السمع
البصر
الكلام
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the attributes of Allah are arbitrarily chosen or can be added or removed.
Correction: The attributes are necessary logical entailments of self-sufficiency; without them, Allah would be in need, contradicting the very meaning of self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
How many necessary attributes are derived from self-sufficiency in this passage?
A) 6
B) 11
C) 23
D) 20

Key Takeaway
Self-sufficiency logically requires twenty-three beliefs: eleven necessary attributes, their eleven impossible opposites, and one possible belief about Allah's freedom in action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is completely self-sufficient and needs nothing from you, why do you sometimes act as if your worship adds something to Him? How does this affect the sincerity of your actions?
?
When you consider that Allah is free from all need, does it challenge any expectation you have that He 'owes' you for your good deeds? What would change if you truly believed He is under no obligation to reward you?
?
In what area of your life are you still behaving as if you can give Allah something He lacks? What would it mean for you to internalize that He is infinitely self-sufficient and needs absolutely nothing from His creation?

Dependence and the Remaining Attributes of Allah
Derivation of 18 beliefs from dependence and summation to 41.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the implication of dependence (الافتقار) entails nine necessary attributes, their impossible opposites, and how the total beliefs reach forty-one.

Core Idea
Allah's exclusive worthiness of worship implies His complete dependence of all else, which in turn demands His life, power, will, knowledge, and oneness.

The first testimony not only implies Allah's self-sufficiency but also that all creation depends on Him. This dependence (الافتقار) logically requires that Allah possess certain attributes that enable Him to be the sole creator and sustainer. This lesson continues the derivation, completing the forty-one beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the author shifts from self-sufficiency to dependence.
The passage begins with 'As for dependence (وأما الافتقار)'.
2
Identify the necessary attributes from dependence
List the attributes that dependence entails: life, power, will, knowledge, their concomitants, and oneness. That gives nine necessary beliefs.
The doctrine of states is again invoked for the concomitants.
3
Understand the opposites and total
Each necessary attribute has an impossible opposite, giving nine impossible beliefs. Adding to the previous twenty-three yields forty-one total beliefs.
The author concludes that the first testimony covers the three rational judgments.
4
Reflect on the three categories
Notice how the 20 necessary, 20 impossible, and 1 possible correspond to the divisions of rational judgment.
This shows the comprehensive nature of the first testimony.

Prophethood as Divine Favor
Commentary on the first part of the line: transition, definition of favor, and reference of demonstrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophethood is described as a divine favor (faḍl) and the rhetorical function of the transition in the poem.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a pure gift from Allah, not earned by human effort.

The poem line 'Bal dhālika faḍlu llāh' asserts that prophethood is not a matter of human merit but a grant from Allah. The commentator explains the phrase as a transitional inversion (iḍrāb intiqālī) that shifts focus without negating previous points. This lesson unpacks the meaning of divine favor and the interpretation of the demonstrative 'dhālika'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rhetorical transition
Read the first sentence: the commentator identifies 'bal' as a transitional inversion. Understand that this shifts the discussion from one aspect to another without nullifying the earlier point.
Contrast with iḍrāb ibṭālī which would negate the previous.
2
Define divine favor (faḍl)
The commentator defines faḍl as giving without any recompense, immediate or deferred. Notice that this quality is exclusive to Allah.
This definition grounds why prophethood cannot be earned.
3
Interpret the demonstrative 'dhālika'
See that 'dhālika' refers to prophethood. Understand the two possible readings: either as an effect of favor or as the selection for prophethood itself, which is a particular instance of favor.
The second reading avoids needing to assume an omitted noun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بَلْ ذَاكَ فَضْلُ اللهِ) هذا إضراب انتقاليّ لا إبطالي، واسم الإشارة عائد على المذكور من النبوة. والفضل : إعطاء الشيء بغير عوض لا عاجل ولا آجل، ولذا لا يكون لغيره تعالى، وفي الكلام حذف مضاف والتقدير : بل المذكور من النبوة أثر فضل الله، وقد فسَّرَ الشارح اسم الإشارة بالاصطفاء للنبوة والاختيار للرسالة، وعليه فلا حاجة لتقدير المضاف المذكور، وإن قدره الشارحُ مع ذلك التفسير; لأن الاصطفاء للنبوة والاختيار للرسالة جُزئي من جزئيات فضل الله لا أثره. وقوله : (يُؤْتِيهِ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ ) أي : آتاه وأعطاه لمن شاءه وأراده في الأزل لذلك ممن كان مستجمعاً لشروط النبوة، فالمراد بالمضارع الماضي فيهما، وإنما عبر بالمضارع استحضاراً للصورة العجيبة وإنما كان المضارع بمعنى الماضي في الأول; لأن إيتاء النبوة قد انقطع بعده ، فإنه خاتم النبيين، وفي الثاني لأن مشيئته وإرادته تعالى لذلك ثابتة فى الأزل، وإن تأخَّرَ الإيتاء بالفعل فيما لا يزال والضمير المنصوب في (يؤتيه) عائد على الفضل بمعنى المتفضل به لا بالمعنى السابق، ففي الكلام استخدام، وإنما قلنا ذلك ; لأن الفضل بالمعنى السابق لا يتصف بذلك. قوله : ( جَلَّ الله ) أي تنزّه الله عن أن يُنال شيءٌ لم يكن أراد إعطاءه. : وقوله : (وَاهِبُ المِنَنْ ) أي : معطي العطايا بدون عوض، فالواهب بمعنى المعطي بدون عوض والمنن بمعنى العطايا ; أي الأمور التى تؤول إلى كونها عطايا ففي كلامه مَجَاز الأول، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل كما في قوله : «مَنْ قتل قتيلاً فله : سَلَبُهُ » (١) ، أي : من قتل شخصاً يؤول أمره إلى كونه قتيلاً فله سَلَبُهُ، كذا قيل، والحق أنه ليس من المجاز في شيء لا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل ; لأن المراد من قتل قتيلاً بهذا القتل لا بغيره حتى يلزم ما ذُكر ، ولذلك شنّع السبكي في «عروس الأفراح» على من جعل الحديث المذكور من مَجَاز الأول، فالمراد هنا العطايا بهذا الإعطاء. قال الشارح: وظاهر السياق أن المراد بالمنن الكاملة كالنبوة; أي: فتكون (ال) للعهد والمعهود النوع الكامل منها، والأحسن أن تكون للاستغراق، فإنه تعالى واهب الجميع المنن جليلها وحقيرها . بقي أنه قد تقرر أن أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، مع أن (الواهب) لم يرد، وإنما الوارد في الأسماء (الوهاب)، وحينئذ فكيف يطلق المصنف (الواهب) عليه تعالى ; وقد يُقال : إن المصنف جارٍ على طريقة مَنْ يكتفي بورود المادة، أو على طريقة مَنْ يجوز إطلاق كل ما يدل على الكمال، وإن لم يرد وهذا على تسليم عدم ورود (الواهب)، وأما على وروده كما عزاه بعضُهم لابن حجر في شرحه على المنهاج (٢) في باب العقيقة فلا إشكال.
His saying: (But that is the favor of Allah). This is a transitional inversion, not an invalidating one. The demonstrative pronoun refers to what was mentioned of prophethood. Al-faḍl (favor) is giving something without compensation, neither immediate nor deferred, and therefore it cannot be from other than Him. In the speech there is an omitted noun: the estimation is 'but the mentioned thing from prophethood is an effect of Allah's favor.' The commentator interpreted the demonstrative pronoun as the selection for prophethood and the choice for messengerhood. On that basis, there is no need to estimate the mentioned noun, even though the commentator estimated it along with that interpretation, because the selection for prophethood and the choice for messengerhood are particular instances of Allah's favor, not its effect. His saying: (He gives it to whom He wills) means: He gave and bestowed it to whom He willed and intended from eternity for that from among those who possessed the conditions of prophethood. So the intended meaning of the present tense verb is the past in both cases. It is expressed with the present tense to evoke the wondrous image. The present tense was in the meaning of the past in the first because the giving of prophethood has ceased after him, for he is the seal of the prophets; and in the second because His will and intention for that are established in eternity, even if the actual giving is deferred in what is to come. The object pronoun in (yuʾtīhi) refers to al-faḍl in the sense of the one who bestows favor (al-mutafaḍḍil bihi), not the previous sense. So in the speech there is usage (istikhdām). We only said that because al-faḍl in the previous sense cannot be described with that. His saying: (Exalted is Allah) means: Allah is transcendent that anything could be obtained which He did not intend to give. His saying: (And the Giver of gifts) means: the Bestower of gifts without compensation. So al-wāhib is in the sense of the giver without compensation, and al-minan is in the sense of gifts, i.e., things that conclude by being gifts. So in his speech there is a metaphor of the first type; otherwise, it would entail taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil (redundant acquisition) as in his saying: 'Whoever kills a slain person, then his spoils are for him.' That is, whoever kills a person whose affair concludes by being slain, then his spoils are for him. That is said, but the truth is that it is not metaphor at all; it does not entail taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil because what is meant is whoever kills a slain person by this specific killing, not by another, so that what was mentioned would be entailed. Therefore, al-Subkī strongly censured in 'Arūs al-Afrāḥ' those who considered the mentioned hadith as metaphor of the first type. So what is meant here is gifts by this specific giving. The commentator said: The apparent context is that what is meant by al-minan is the complete ones like prophethood; i.e., the definite article is for the covenant, and the covenanted is the complete type of it. But the better is that it is for generality (istiʿghrāq), for He is the Giver of all gifts, their great and their insignificant. It remains that it has been established that the names of Allah are tawqīfiyya (based on revealed text), even though (al-Wāhib) has not been reported [in the revealed texts]; rather, what is reported among the names is (al-Wahhāb). So then how does the author apply (al-Wāhib) to Him? It may be said: The author is following the method of those who are satisfied with the occurrence of the root, or the method of those who permit the application of everything that indicates perfection, even if not reported. This is on the assumption that (al-Wāhib) has not been reported. But according to its occurrence—as some have attributed to Ibn Ḥajar in his commentary on al-Minhāj in the chapter on al-ʿAqīqa—there is no problem.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the line 'Bal dhālika faḍlu llāh yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu jalla llāhu wāhibu l-minan'. The commentator begins by identifying the rhetorical function: it is an abrupt transition (iḍrāb intiqālī) that corrects or shifts, not invalidates the previous statement. The demonstrative 'dhālika' refers to prophethood previously mentioned. He defines faḍl as giving without any recompense, a quality exclusive to Allah. He notes an implied deletion: the meaning is that the mentioned prophethood is 'an effect of Allah's favor'. Alternatively, 'dhālika' could refer to the selection for prophethood itself, which is a part of Allah's favor. Then he explains 'yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu' as past tense (ā'tāhu) even though the verb is in the present tense—this use of the present is to evoke the wondrous nature. The first verb refers to the giving of prophethood which has ceased because Muhammad is the seal; the second refers to the eternal will, which is ongoing. The pronoun in 'yuʾtīhi' refers to al-faḍl in the sense of 'the bestower', not the favor itself, thus a case of istikhdām (one word used in two different senses). Then 'jalla llāhu' means He is exalted above anything being obtained contrary to His will. Then 'wāhibu l-minan' is explained as 'giver of gifts without compensation'. The commentator addresses a potential logical redundancy (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil): if it is already established that He gives, saying 'He gives gifts' seems redundant. He argues this is not a metaphor; rather, the phrase specifies that He gives gifts by this particular giving, just as in the hadith 'man qatala qatīlan fa-lahu salabuhu' is not a metaphor because the killing referred to is a specific one. The commentator then discusses the definite article 'al' in 'al-minan': whether it indicates a specific covenant (al-ʿahd) meaning the complete type like prophethood, or generality (al-istighrāq). He prefers generality. Finally, he raises an important theological point: Allah's names are tawqīfiyya (determined by revelation). The name 'al-Wāhib' is not among the established names (only 'al-Wahhāb' is). So how can the author use it? He offers two solutions: either the author follows the view that the mere occurrence of the root suffices, or the view that any term indicating perfection can be used even if not explicitly reported. Alternatively, if 'al-Wāhib' is indeed reported (as some claim from Ibn Ḥajar's commentary), then there is no issue at all.

Key Terms
فضل
إضراب انتقالي
اصطفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that prophethood is earned through personal effort or merit.
Correction: The commentator emphasizes that prophethood is a pure favor (faḍl) from Allah, given without any recompense.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the rhetorical function of 'bal' in this line?
A) It is an invalidating inversion (iḍrāb ibṭālī)
B) It is a transitional inversion (iḍrāb intiqālī)
C) It is a confirmation (taqrīr)
D) It is a correction (taṣḥīḥ)

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a gratuitous divine favor, not earned; the transition 'bal' shifts focus without negating previous points.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing, do you immediately think you did something to deserve it? Reflect on the definition of 'فضل' as giving without compensation. How does this truth challenge your sense of entitlement?
?
If Allah's favor is purely gratuitous, then every good thing in your life is a gift you never earned. How would your worship change if you truly internalized that you owe Him everything and He owes you nothing?
?
The commentator says that prophethood is itself a direct instance of favor, not just an effect. Consider one major blessing in your life. Do you view it as a distant effect of Allah's mercy, or as an immediate gift from Him? How does that perspective affect your gratitude?

Verb Tense and Pronoun Usage
Commentary on the verb tense and pronoun reference in the line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the use of the present tense verb to refer to past actions and the rhetorical device of istikhdām in the pronoun reference.

Core Idea
The present tense can evoke a vivid past event, and one word can serve two meanings simultaneously.

The line continues with 'yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu', where the verb is in the present tense but refers to a past giving of prophethood. The commentator explains this as a stylistic choice to evoke wonder. He also discusses the pronoun 'hu' in 'yuʾtīhi', showing it refers to 'al-faḍl' but in the sense of the Bestower (al-mutafaḍḍil), thus employing istikhdām (using one word in two senses).

Guided Path
1
Examine the present tense usage
Read the commentator's explanation: 'yuʾtīhi' is in the present tense but means the past (ā'tāhu). Note the reason: to evoke the wondrous nature of the event.
The first 'yuʾtī' refers to prophethood given only once; the second 'yashāʾu' refers to eternal will.
2
Understand the pronoun separation
The pronoun in 'yuʾtīhi' does not refer to al-faḍl as favor itself, but to al-mutafaḍḍil (the Bestower). This is a case of istikhdām.
Istikhdām means using a word in two different senses in one context.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
By distinguishing the Bestower from the gift, the commentator reinforces that the act of giving is attributed to Allah's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بَلْ ذَاكَ فَضْلُ اللهِ) هذا إضراب انتقاليّ لا إبطالي، واسم الإشارة عائد على المذكور من النبوة. والفضل : إعطاء الشيء بغير عوض لا عاجل ولا آجل، ولذا لا يكون لغيره تعالى، وفي الكلام حذف مضاف والتقدير : بل المذكور من النبوة أثر فضل الله، وقد فسَّرَ الشارح اسم الإشارة بالاصطفاء للنبوة والاختيار للرسالة، وعليه فلا حاجة لتقدير المضاف المذكور، وإن قدره الشارحُ مع ذلك التفسير; لأن الاصطفاء للنبوة والاختيار للرسالة جُزئي من جزئيات فضل الله لا أثره. وقوله : (يُؤْتِيهِ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ ) أي : آتاه وأعطاه لمن شاءه وأراده في الأزل لذلك ممن كان مستجمعاً لشروط النبوة، فالمراد بالمضارع الماضي فيهما، وإنما عبر بالمضارع استحضاراً للصورة العجيبة وإنما كان المضارع بمعنى الماضي في الأول; لأن إيتاء النبوة قد انقطع بعده ، فإنه خاتم النبيين، وفي الثاني لأن مشيئته وإرادته تعالى لذلك ثابتة فى الأزل، وإن تأخَّرَ الإيتاء بالفعل فيما لا يزال والضمير المنصوب في (يؤتيه) عائد على الفضل بمعنى المتفضل به لا بالمعنى السابق، ففي الكلام استخدام، وإنما قلنا ذلك ; لأن الفضل بالمعنى السابق لا يتصف بذلك. قوله : ( جَلَّ الله ) أي تنزّه الله عن أن يُنال شيءٌ لم يكن أراد إعطاءه. : وقوله : (وَاهِبُ المِنَنْ ) أي : معطي العطايا بدون عوض، فالواهب بمعنى المعطي بدون عوض والمنن بمعنى العطايا ; أي الأمور التى تؤول إلى كونها عطايا ففي كلامه مَجَاز الأول، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل كما في قوله : «مَنْ قتل قتيلاً فله : سَلَبُهُ » (١) ، أي : من قتل شخصاً يؤول أمره إلى كونه قتيلاً فله سَلَبُهُ، كذا قيل، والحق أنه ليس من المجاز في شيء لا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل ; لأن المراد من قتل قتيلاً بهذا القتل لا بغيره حتى يلزم ما ذُكر ، ولذلك شنّع السبكي في «عروس الأفراح» على من جعل الحديث المذكور من مَجَاز الأول، فالمراد هنا العطايا بهذا الإعطاء. قال الشارح: وظاهر السياق أن المراد بالمنن الكاملة كالنبوة; أي: فتكون (ال) للعهد والمعهود النوع الكامل منها، والأحسن أن تكون للاستغراق، فإنه تعالى واهب الجميع المنن جليلها وحقيرها . بقي أنه قد تقرر أن أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، مع أن (الواهب) لم يرد، وإنما الوارد في الأسماء (الوهاب)، وحينئذ فكيف يطلق المصنف (الواهب) عليه تعالى ; وقد يُقال : إن المصنف جارٍ على طريقة مَنْ يكتفي بورود المادة، أو على طريقة مَنْ يجوز إطلاق كل ما يدل على الكمال، وإن لم يرد وهذا على تسليم عدم ورود (الواهب)، وأما على وروده كما عزاه بعضُهم لابن حجر في شرحه على المنهاج (٢) في باب العقيقة فلا إشكال.
His saying: (But that is the favor of Allah). This is a transitional inversion, not an invalidating one. The demonstrative pronoun refers to what was mentioned of prophethood. Al-faḍl (favor) is giving something without compensation, neither immediate nor deferred, and therefore it cannot be from other than Him. In the speech there is an omitted noun: the estimation is 'but the mentioned thing from prophethood is an effect of Allah's favor.' The commentator interpreted the demonstrative pronoun as the selection for prophethood and the choice for messengerhood. On that basis, there is no need to estimate the mentioned noun, even though the commentator estimated it along with that interpretation, because the selection for prophethood and the choice for messengerhood are particular instances of Allah's favor, not its effect. His saying: (He gives it to whom He wills) means: He gave and bestowed it to whom He willed and intended from eternity for that from among those who possessed the conditions of prophethood. So the intended meaning of the present tense verb is the past in both cases. It is expressed with the present tense to evoke the wondrous image. The present tense was in the meaning of the past in the first because the giving of prophethood has ceased after him, for he is the seal of the prophets; and in the second because His will and intention for that are established in eternity, even if the actual giving is deferred in what is to come. The object pronoun in (yuʾtīhi) refers to al-faḍl in the sense of the one who bestows favor (al-mutafaḍḍil bihi), not the previous sense. So in the speech there is usage (istikhdām). We only said that because al-faḍl in the previous sense cannot be described with that. His saying: (Exalted is Allah) means: Allah is transcendent that anything could be obtained which He did not intend to give. His saying: (And the Giver of gifts) means: the Bestower of gifts without compensation. So al-wāhib is in the sense of the giver without compensation, and al-minan is in the sense of gifts, i.e., things that conclude by being gifts. So in his speech there is a metaphor of the first type; otherwise, it would entail taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil (redundant acquisition) as in his saying: 'Whoever kills a slain person, then his spoils are for him.' That is, whoever kills a person whose affair concludes by being slain, then his spoils are for him. That is said, but the truth is that it is not metaphor at all; it does not entail taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil because what is meant is whoever kills a slain person by this specific killing, not by another, so that what was mentioned would be entailed. Therefore, al-Subkī strongly censured in 'Arūs al-Afrāḥ' those who considered the mentioned hadith as metaphor of the first type. So what is meant here is gifts by this specific giving. The commentator said: The apparent context is that what is meant by al-minan is the complete ones like prophethood; i.e., the definite article is for the covenant, and the covenanted is the complete type of it. But the better is that it is for generality (istiʿghrāq), for He is the Giver of all gifts, their great and their insignificant. It remains that it has been established that the names of Allah are tawqīfiyya (based on revealed text), even though (al-Wāhib) has not been reported [in the revealed texts]; rather, what is reported among the names is (al-Wahhāb). So then how does the author apply (al-Wāhib) to Him? It may be said: The author is following the method of those who are satisfied with the occurrence of the root, or the method of those who permit the application of everything that indicates perfection, even if not reported. This is on the assumption that (al-Wāhib) has not been reported. But according to its occurrence—as some have attributed to Ibn Ḥajar in his commentary on al-Minhāj in the chapter on al-ʿAqīqa—there is no problem.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the line 'Bal dhālika faḍlu llāh yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu jalla llāhu wāhibu l-minan'. The commentator begins by identifying the rhetorical function: it is an abrupt transition (iḍrāb intiqālī) that corrects or shifts, not invalidates the previous statement. The demonstrative 'dhālika' refers to prophethood previously mentioned. He defines faḍl as giving without any recompense, a quality exclusive to Allah. He notes an implied deletion: the meaning is that the mentioned prophethood is 'an effect of Allah's favor'. Alternatively, 'dhālika' could refer to the selection for prophethood itself, which is a part of Allah's favor. Then he explains 'yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu' as past tense (ā'tāhu) even though the verb is in the present tense—this use of the present is to evoke the wondrous nature. The first verb refers to the giving of prophethood which has ceased because Muhammad is the seal; the second refers to the eternal will, which is ongoing. The pronoun in 'yuʾtīhi' refers to al-faḍl in the sense of 'the bestower', not the favor itself, thus a case of istikhdām (one word used in two different senses). Then 'jalla llāhu' means He is exalted above anything being obtained contrary to His will. Then 'wāhibu l-minan' is explained as 'giver of gifts without compensation'. The commentator addresses a potential logical redundancy (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil): if it is already established that He gives, saying 'He gives gifts' seems redundant. He argues this is not a metaphor; rather, the phrase specifies that He gives gifts by this particular giving, just as in the hadith 'man qatala qatīlan fa-lahu salabuhu' is not a metaphor because the killing referred to is a specific one. The commentator then discusses the definite article 'al' in 'al-minan': whether it indicates a specific covenant (al-ʿahd) meaning the complete type like prophethood, or generality (al-istighrāq). He prefers generality. Finally, he raises an important theological point: Allah's names are tawqīfiyya (determined by revelation). The name 'al-Wāhib' is not among the established names (only 'al-Wahhāb' is). So how can the author use it? He offers two solutions: either the author follows the view that the mere occurrence of the root suffices, or the view that any term indicating perfection can be used even if not explicitly reported. Alternatively, if 'al-Wāhib' is indeed reported (as some claim from Ibn Ḥajar's commentary), then there is no issue at all.

Key Terms
يؤتيه
استخدام
مضارع بمعنى الماضي
مشيئة أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the present tense necessarily indicates present or future time; here it indicates past.
Correction: In Arabic rhetoric, present tense can be used for past events to create vividness, especially for significant events like prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the pronoun in 'yuʾtīhi' refers to al-mutafaḍḍil (the Bestower) rather than al-faḍl (the favor)?
A) Because the pronoun must agree in gender
B) Because 'favor' in the previous sense cannot be described as being given
C) Because the poet specified it
D) Because of the rhyme

Key Takeaway
The present tense can evoke past events, and the pronoun reference illustrates istikhdām (using one word in two senses).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator reveals that the pronoun 'hi' does not point to the gift (prophethood) but to the Giver. How often do you focus on the blessings Allah gives rather than on Allah Himself? Will you train your heart to see the Bestower in every favor?
?
The present tense verb brings the past event of prophethood to life as if it were present. How alive is the reality of Muhammad’s prophethood in your daily consciousness? Do you merely believe it as a historical fact, or does it shape your present moments?
?
If the giving of prophethood has ceased because Muhammad is the seal, what does that mean for your responsibility toward his message? Have you truly received that prophethood by following it, or are you content with mere belief?

The Name al-Wāhib and Tawqīfiyya
Commentary on 'jalla llāhu wāhibu l-minan' and the permissibility of the name al-Wāhib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the issue of using the name 'al-Wāhib' for Allah given the tawqīfiyya principle, and the resolution offered by the commentator.

Core Idea
Allah's names are tawqīfiyya, but 'al-Wāhib' can be justified by occurrence of the root or by a reported variant.

The poem concludes with 'jalla llāhu wāhibu l-minan'. The commentator first explains 'jalla llāhu' as transcending any limitation on His giving. Then he addresses the name 'al-Wāhib': it is not among the established divine names (only 'al-Wahhāb' is), yet the author uses it. He discusses two justifications: either the author follows a lenient view that permits any term indicating perfection, or 'al-Wāhib' is actually reported (as some attribute to Ibn Ḥajar).

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'jalla llāhu'
Read the short explanation: 'jalla llāhu' means Allah is exalted above anything being obtained without His will.
This connects to the preceding verb: He gives to whom He wills.
2
Examine the issue of tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil
The commentator discusses whether saying 'wāhibu l-minan' is redundant (tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil). He argues it is not, because the giving is specified to the particular gifts.
He uses a hadith about spoils as an analogy.
3
Analyze the tawqīfiyya problem
Identify the problem: Allah's names are based on revelation, and 'al-Wāhib' is not found in the Quran or authentic hadith, only 'al-Wahhāb' is. The commentator offers two solutions.
4
Evaluate the solutions
First solution: some scholars permit using any name that shares the root if the meaning is praiseworthy. Second solution: 'al-Wāhib' is actually reported (Ibn Ḥajar's commentary).
The second solution removes the need for justification.

Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the line 'Bal dhālika faḍlu llāh yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu jalla llāhu wāhibu l-minan'. The commentator begins by identifying the rhetorical function: it is an abrupt transition (iḍrāb intiqālī) that corrects or shifts, not invalidates the previous statement. The demonstrative 'dhālika' refers to prophethood previously mentioned. He defines faḍl as giving without any recompense, a quality exclusive to Allah. He notes an implied deletion: the meaning is that the mentioned prophethood is 'an effect of Allah's favor'. Alternatively, 'dhālika' could refer to the selection for prophethood itself, which is a part of Allah's favor. Then he explains 'yuʾtīhi li-man yashāʾu' as past tense (ā'tāhu) even though the verb is in the present tense—this use of the present is to evoke the wondrous nature. The first verb refers to the giving of prophethood which has ceased because Muhammad is the seal; the second refers to the eternal will, which is ongoing. The pronoun in 'yuʾtīhi' refers to al-faḍl in the sense of 'the bestower', not the favor itself, thus a case of istikhdām (one word used in two different senses). Then 'jalla llāhu' means He is exalted above anything being obtained contrary to His will. Then 'wāhibu l-minan' is explained as 'giver of gifts without compensation'. The commentator addresses a potential logical redundancy (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil): if it is already established that He gives, saying 'He gives gifts' seems redundant. He argues this is not a metaphor; rather, the phrase specifies that He gives gifts by this particular giving, just as in the hadith 'man qatala qatīlan fa-lahu salabuhu' is not a metaphor because the killing referred to is a specific one. The commentator then discusses the definite article 'al' in 'al-minan': whether it indicates a specific covenant (al-ʿahd) meaning the complete type like prophethood, or generality (al-istighrāq). He prefers generality. Finally, he raises an important theological point: Allah's names are tawqīfiyya (determined by revelation). The name 'al-Wāhib' is not among the established names (only 'al-Wahhāb' is). So how can the author use it? He offers two solutions: either the author follows the view that the mere occurrence of the root suffices, or the view that any term indicating perfection can be used even if not explicitly reported. Alternatively, if 'al-Wāhib' is indeed reported (as some claim from Ibn Ḥajar's commentary), then there is no issue at all.

Key Terms
جل الله
واهب
وهاب
توقيفية
تحصيل الحاصل
منن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think using 'al-Wāhib' is impermissible because it is not in the Quran or authentic hadith.
Correction: The commentator shows that it can be justified either by the occurrence of the root or by a reported usage from Ibn Ḥajar.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the main reason for concern over using the name 'al-Wāhib'?
A) It is a synonym for al-Wahhāb
B) It has not been reported in the revealed texts (Quran/Sunnah) while names are tawqīfiyya
C) It implies giving without will
D) It is not a name of majesty

Key Takeaway
The name 'al-Wāhib' is permissible either because it shares the root with 'al-Wahhāb' or because it is actually reported; this illustrates the scope of tawqīfiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah, do you restrict yourself only to the names you know from the 99, or do you ever use derived forms like al-Wāhib? What holds you back from using a name that conveys a perfect attribute if you feel it is appropriate?
?
The commentator presents two methods for allowing al-Wāhib: one based on the root occurring, and one based on any term of perfection. Which method do you find more convincing, and why? How does your understanding of tawqīfiyya influence your daily du'a?
?
Imagine yourself in a situation where you desperately need a gift from Allah, but the name al-Wāhib is not officially listed among the 99. Would you still dare to call upon Him using that name? What does your answer reveal about your trust in divine mercy versus strict adherence to terminology?

The Meaning of 'Wahib al-Minan' and Avoiding Metaphor
Defines 'Wahib al-Minan' and resolves a metaphor issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal meaning of 'Wahib al-Minan' and why a metaphorical interpretation would lead to redundancy.

Core Idea
Allah gives gifts without compensation, and the literal reading avoids logical redundancy.

In this passage, the commentator defines the phrase 'Wahib al-Minan' from the poem. He first explains 'jalla Allah' as transcendence, then defines 'Wahib' as the Giver without compensation and 'al-Minan' as gifts. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding involving a metaphorical interpretation (majaz awwal) that would lead to a logical error (tahsil al-hasil). By comparing to a hadith, he shows that the intended meaning is literal and specific.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation for the explanation of 'Wahib' and 'al-Minan'. Notice the precise language: 'giving without compensation'.
2
Understand the metaphor issue
The commentator warns that if 'al-Minan' is taken metaphorically, it would imply 'things that become gifts', leading to tahsil al-hasil (obtaining the obtained).
This is similar to the hadith about killing a slain person.
3
See the resolution
The commentator clarifies that the intended meaning is literal: the gifts are given by this specific giving. He cites al-Subki who criticized those who considered the hadith as an example of metaphor.
Thus the phrase is straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( جَلَّ الله أي تنزّه الله عن أن يُنال شيءٌ لم يكن أراد إعطاءه. : وقوله : (وَاهِبُ المِنَنْ ) أي : معطي العطايا بدون عوض، فالواهب بمعنى المعطي بدون عوض والمنن بمعنى العطايا ؛ أي الأمور التى تؤول إلى كونها عطايا ففي كلامه مَجَاز الأول، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل كما في قوله : «مَنْ قتل قتيلاً فله : سَلَبُهُ » (١) ، أي : من قتل شخصاً يؤول أمره إلى كونه قتيلاً فله سَلَبُهُ، كذا قيل، والحق أنه ليس من المجاز في شيء لا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل ؛ لأن المراد من قتل قتيلاً بهذا القتل لا بغيره حتى يلزم ما ذُكر ، ولذلك شنّع السبكي في «عروس الأفراح» على من جعل الحديث المذكور من مَجَاز الأول، فالمراد هنا العطايا بهذا الإعطاء. قال الشارح: وظاهر السياق أن المراد بالمنن الكاملة كالنبوة؛ أي: فتكون (ال) للعهد والمعهود النوع الكامل منها، والأحسن أن تكون للاستغراق، فإنه تعالى واهب الجميع المنن جليلها وحقيرها . بقي أنه قد تقرر أن أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، مع أن (الواهب) لم يرد، وإنما الوارد في الأسماء (الوهاب)، وحينئذ فكيف يطلق المصنف (الواهب) عليه تعالى ؛ وقد يُقال : إن المصنف جارٍ على طريقة مَنْ يكتفي بورود المادة، أو على طريقة مَنْ يجوز إطلاق كل ما يدل على الكمال، وإن لم يرد وهذا على تسليم عدم ورود (الواهب)، وأما على وروده كما عزاه بعضُهم لابن حجر في شرحه على المنهاج (٢) في باب العقيقة فلا إشكال.
His statement: (Glory be to Allah) meaning: Allah is transcendent above that anything should be attained that He had not intended to give. And His statement: (And the Bestower of favors) meaning: the Giver of gifts without compensation. So 'al-Wahib' means the Giver without compensation, and 'al-Minan' means gifts, i.e., things that eventually become gifts. Thus in his speech there is the first type of metaphor (majaz awwal), otherwise there would be a redundancy (tahsil al-hasil) as in his saying: 'Whoever kills a slain person, then for him is his spoils' (Hadith). Meaning: whoever kills a person whose affair ends up being slain, then for him is his spoils. This is what is said, but the truth is that it is not a metaphor at all and no redundancy arises, because the intended meaning is 'whoever kills a slain person by this killing, not by another', so that what was mentioned occurs. Therefore al-Subki in 'Arous al-Afrah' condemned those who considered the aforementioned hadith as an example of the first type of metaphor. So the intended meaning here is 'the gifts by this giving'. The commentator said: The apparent context is that what is meant by 'al-Minan' are the perfect favors like prophethood; so the definite article is for the specific covenant, and the covenanted is the complete type of them. But better is that it be for generality, for He, exalted, is the Bestower of all favors, their great and their insignificant. There remains that it has been established that the names of Allah, exalted, are tawqifiyya (based on revelation), while (al-Wahib) has not been reported, and what is reported among the names is (al-Wahhab). Then how does the author apply (al-Wahib) to Him, exalted? And it might be said: The author follows the method of those who are content with the occurrence of the root, or the method of those who permit applying every term that indicates perfection, even if not reported. And this is on the assumption that (al-Wahib) has not been reported. But if it has been reported, as some attribute to Ibn Hajar in his commentary on al-Minhaj in the chapter of Aqiqah, then there is no problem.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on the phrase 'Wahib al-Minan' from the Jawharat al-Tawhid poem. It first explains the meaning of 'jalla Allah' as transcendence, then defines 'Wahib al-Minan' as the Giver of gifts without compensation. The author addresses a potential redundancy (tahsil al-hasil) by comparing to a hadith: 'Whoever kills a slain person, then for him is his spoils.' The commentator argues that this is not a metaphor (majaz) because the killing is specific. He cites al-Subki who criticized those who considered the hadith as an example of the first type of metaphor. The commentator then considers the meaning of 'al-Minan', suggesting it could refer to perfect favors like prophethood, but prefers the interpretation of generality—that Allah bestows all favors, great and small. Finally, the passage discusses the principle of tawqif (that divine names must be based on revelation). Since 'al-Wahib' is not among the reported names, the author's use is questioned. Possible justifications are offered: either the author follows those who accept the root matter, or those who allow any name indicating perfection. However, if 'al-Wahib' is reported (as some attribute to Ibn Hajar), there is no issue.

Misconception: That the hadith 'Whoever kills a slain person...' is an example of the first type of metaphor (majaz awwal).
Correction: Al-Subki criticized that view; the true interpretation is literal, referring to a specific killing, and no redundancy occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the metaphorical interpretation of 'Wahib al-Minan'?
A) Because it would imply that Allah gives compensation.
B) Because it would lead to redundancy (tahsil al-hasil).
C) Because the hadith analogy does not apply.
D) Because al-Subki allowed metaphors.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Wahib al-Minan' is to be understood literally as the Giver of gifts without compensation, avoiding any metaphorical interpretation that would lead to redundancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Wahib al-Minan', do you truly believe that every blessing you have is a gift from Allah without any merit on your part, or does your heart secretly think you deserve it? Pause and list one favor you received today that you did nothing to earn. How does that change your gratitude?
?
The lesson rejects a metaphorical interpretation that would make Allah's words redundant. Are there times when you, in your own mind, 'explain away' Allah's names or attributes to fit your limited understanding, rather than accepting them literally with trust? Ask yourself: Am I trying to tame the divine with logic, or am I submitting to His words as they are?
?
Al-Subki condemned those who misinterpret the hadith metaphorically. Is there a point in your life where you have insisted on a comfortable interpretation of a text rather than the literal, challenging one? Will you now commit to taking Allah's words at face value, even when they seem to strain human logic?

The Principle of Tawqif and the Name 'al-Wahib'
Discusses the permissibility of using 'al-Wahib' given the principle of tawqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that divine names are tawqifiyya and the justifications for using 'al-Wahib' despite not being revealed.

Core Idea
Divine names must be based on revelation, but 'al-Wahib' may be justified by its root or by attributing perfection.

After explaining the meaning of 'Wahib al-Minan', the passage raises a theological problem: it is established that Allah's names are tawqifiyya (based on revelation), yet 'al-Wahib' is not among the revealed names; only 'al-Wahhab' is. The commentator offers two possible justifications and notes that if 'al-Wahib' is indeed reported, there is no issue.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the principle of tawqif
Understand that divine names must be taken from revelation; one cannot invent a name for Allah.
2
Identify the problem
The author of the poem uses 'al-Wahib', but only 'al-Wahhab' is found in revelation. This seems to violate tawqif.
3
Analyze the justifications
Two justifications are given: (1) being content with the root matter (i.e., the root w-h-b is used in revelation), or (2) permitting any term that indicates perfection. Also, there is a report that Ibn Hajar mentioned 'al-Wahib' in his commentary.
These justifications show the flexibility within the principle.

Definition of 'Isma and Ruling on Asking for It
Commentator defines 'isma linguistically and technically, then rules on asking for it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'isma (linguistic and technical) and why it is not permissible to ask God for 'isma in its technical sense.

Core Idea
'Isma is God's preservation from sin, but asking for the technical sense is not allowed.

This lesson clarifies the meaning of 'isma as used in Islamic theology. The commentator distinguishes between the linguistic sense—general preservation—and the technical sense—preservation from sin such that sin becomes impossible. Based on this distinction, he explains why we cannot request 'isma in the technical sense from God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and find the linguistic and technical definitions of 'isma. Notice how they differ.
The linguistic definition is 'absolute preservation'.
2
Understand the ruling on asking
The commentator states it is not permissible to ask for 'isma in the technical sense, but it is permissible in the linguistic sense. Reflect on why.
Technical 'isma implies impossibility of sin, which is a special grace.
3
Connect to the earlier discussion
The passage also addresses a potential redundancy: why mention 'isma for prophets again? The answer is to include angels.
This shows the author's care in covering all prophets and angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والباري الخالق من البرء وهو الخلق، وقد يقال : إن عصمة الأنبياء قد تقدمت في قوله : ( وواجب) في حقهم الأمانة)؛ إذ الأمانة العصمة (۱) ، وقد يُجاب بأنه إنما تعرّض لها ليجمع الملائكة مع الأنبياء في هي حكمها (٢) والاتصاف بها والعصمة لغةً مطلق الحفظ واصطلاحاً: حفظ الله للمكلف من الذنب مع استحالة وقوعه ولا يجوز لنا سؤال العصمة بهذا المعنى؛ كأن يقال: (اللهم إنا نسألك العصمة)، فإن أريد المعنى اللغوي جاز لنا سؤالها . واعلم أن المشهور عصمة جميع الملائكة، وقولهم : أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَن يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٣٠] ليس غيبةٌ ولا اعتراضاً على الله، بل مجرد استفهام، وما نقل في قصة هاروت وماروت مما يذكره المؤرخون لم يصح فيه شيء من الأخبار، بل هو من افتراء اليهود وكذبهم وتبعهم المؤرخون في ذكر ذلك، وقيل: كانا رجلين ،صالحين، وسُميا ملكين تشبيهاً لهما بالملكين (1) العبارة في (أ) : (إذا الأمانة في حقهم العصمة . (۲) في (ب): (الأحكام). (۳) وأثبت الزمخشري العصمة للسيدة مريم في كشافه»
And al-Bari is the Creator, from al-bar' meaning creation. It may be said that the protection of the prophets was already mentioned in his statement: 'And obligatory upon them is trustworthiness,' since trustworthiness is protection. It may be answered that he mentioned it here in order to include the angels with the prophets in its ruling and being described by it. And 'isma linguistically is absolute preservation, and technically is God's preservation of the morally responsible person from sin such that its occurrence is impossible. It is not permissible for us to ask for 'isma in this technical sense, such as saying 'O God, we ask you for protection.' If the linguistic meaning is intended, then it is permissible for us to ask for it. Know that the well-known position is the infallibility of all angels. Their statement 'Will You place therein one who causes corruption and sheds blood?' [al-Baqara 2:30] is not backbiting nor an objection to God, but merely an inquiry. And what has been transmitted concerning the story of Harut and Marut that historians mention, none of it is authentic from the reports; rather it is from the fabrications and lies of the Jews, and the historians followed them in mentioning that. It is said that they were two righteous men, and they were called 'angels' as a comparison to angels.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of 'isma. The commentator first explains the word 'al-Bari' (the Creator). He then addresses a potential redundancy: that the prophets' 'isma was already mentioned earlier in the poem under the attribute of trustworthiness (amana). The answer is that the poet re-mentions it here to explicitly include angels in the same ruling. Next, the commentator defines 'isma both linguistically (absolute preservation) and technically (God's preservation of the morally responsible person from sin such that it is impossible to commit). Importantly, he rules that one may not ask God for 'isma in the technical sense, as that would be asking for something that necessarily implies protection from sin, which is a special grace from God. However, asking in the linguistic sense (general preservation) is permissible. Then, the commentator affirms the well-known position that all angels are infallible. He explains the verse 'Will You place therein...' (Q. 2:30) is not a reproach or objection but a simple inquiry. He also refutes the story of Harut and Marut as fabricated by Jews and unreliable. Some say they were two righteous men called angels metaphorically. A footnote adds that al-Zamakhshari affirmed the 'isma of Maryam.

Correction: The commentator explicitly states that asking for 'isma in the technical sense is not permissible because it implies a special grace that cannot be requested.

Key Takeaway
The technical 'isma is a special grace from God that cannot be requested; only general preservation (linguistic sense) may be asked for.
